Divine Attributes

The Sixth Proof: Four Aspects of Divine Action Beyond Sense
Al-Razi enumerates four aspects proving divine actions beyond sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four rational aspects that demonstrate that knowledge of God's actions transcends sensory imagination.

Core Idea
God's actions are known by reason, not by sense or imagination.

Al-Razi presents a powerful proof that our understanding of God's creative acts does not rely on sensory experience. He begins by noting that even the anthropomorphists agree that knowledge of God's attributes is beyond sense perception. Then he elaborates four distinct aspects that show how our theological convictions contradict what sense and imagination would lead us to believe. Each aspect highlights a discontinuity between ordinary experience and divine action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a general claim, then four numbered 'wujuh' (aspects), then a conclusion.
2
Identify each aspect
Go through each of the four aspects. For each, note what sense tells us (e.g., change requires matter, agent change, purpose) and what we affirm about God that contradicts that.
3
Understand the conclusion
After the four aspects, Al-Razi concludes that wahm wa khayal (illusion and imagination) are excluded from knowledge of God's acts. Reflect on how the four aspects collectively support this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : أن نقول : إن معرفة [ أفعال (۱) ] الله تعالى وصفاته أقرب إلى العقل من معرفة ذات الله تعالى . ثم إن المشبهة وافقونا على أن معرفة أفعال الله ومعرفة (۲) ] صفاته ، على خلاف حكم الحس والخيال

أما تقرير هذا المعنى في أفعال الله تعالى فذلك من وجوه : الأول : إن الذي رأيناه وشاهدناه [ ليس [ إلا ) ] تغير الصفات مثل انقلاب الماء المذاب نباتا وانقلاب النبات حيونا (4) ] فأما حدوث الذوات والأجسام فهذا شيء ما شاهدناه وما عرفناه البتة . الثاني : إنا لا نعقل حدوث شيء يصاغ إلا عن مادة مخصوصة وإلا في زمان مخصوص . فإن تكون الخاتم والسوار من غير شيء يصاغ ذلك الخاتم والسوار منه ، غير مفهوم ولا محسوس . وأيضا : فيكون حدوث الخاتم والسوار من غير زمان ووقت غير معقول . ثم إن أرباب الملل والأديان أقروا بذلك واعترفوا به مع أنه غير موهوم ولا محسوس ، والثالث : إنا . لا نعقل [ فاعلا يفعل (٥) ] بعد ما لم يكن فاعلا إلا لأجل تغير حاله وتبدل صفته . ثم إن أرباب الملل اعترفوا بأنه خالق للعالم من غير تغير شيء في صفاته ولا تبدل في أحواله . والرابع : إنا لا نعقل فاعلا يفعل فعلا إلا لجلب منفعة أو لدفع مضرة . ثم إنا اعترفنا بأنه تعالى خلق هذا العالم من غير شيء من هذه الأحوال. فيثبت : أن الوهم والخيال معزولان في معرفة أفعال الله تعالى
The sixth proof: we say that knowledge of the acts [ (1) ] of God, exalted, and His attributes is closer to reason than knowledge of the essence of God, exalted. Then the anthropomorphists (mushabbiha) agreed with us that knowledge of God's acts and knowledge (2) of His attributes are contrary to the judgment of sense and imagination.

As for the elaboration of this meaning concerning God's acts, it is from aspects:
First: that which we have seen and witnessed is [only (?) ] change of qualities, such as the transformation of melted water into plants and the transformation of plants into animals (4). As for the origination of substances and bodies, this is something we have not witnessed nor known at all.
Second: we do not conceive the origination of something fashioned except from a particular matter and except in a particular time. For the coming into existence of a ring and bracelet from nothing from which that ring and bracelet are fashioned is inconceivable and not sensed. Also: the coming into existence of a ring and bracelet without a time and moment is irrational. Then the people of religions and creeds assented to that and acknowledged it, even though it is not imagined nor sensed.
Third: we do not conceive an agent acting (5) after not having been an agent except due to a change in his state and transformation of his attribute. Then the people of religions acknowledged that He is the Creator of the world without any change in His attributes nor transformation in His states.
Fourth: we do not conceive an agent performing an act except for attracting benefit or repelling harm. Then we acknowledged that He, exalted, created this world without any of these conditions. So it is established that illusion and imagination are excluded from knowledge of God's acts.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof, which starts by stating that knowledge of God's actions and attributes is more accessible to reason than knowledge of His essence. The anthropomorphists (mushabbiha) agree that such knowledge does not rely on sense perception. The author then elaborates on this point regarding God's actions through four aspects (wujuh).

First: In our observable experience, we only witness changes in qualities (e.g., water becoming plants, plants becoming animals), not the origination of substances themselves. The origination of bodies is beyond our direct observation.

Second: We cannot conceive of something being created without pre-existing matter and a specific time. Yet religious people affirm creation ex nihilo and God's timeless act, which are contrary to imagination.

Third: We cannot conceive of an agent who previously was not an agent becoming an agent without a change in his state. Yet Muslims affirm that God created the world without any change in His attributes.

Fourth: We cannot conceive of an agent acting without a purpose of benefit or harm. Yet God's creation is free from such motivations.

Each aspect shows that universally accepted religious beliefs about God's actions go beyond what sense and imagination can grasp. The conclusion is that imagination (wahm wa khayal) is excluded from knowledge of God's acts.

Key Terms
حجة (hujja)
مشبهة (mushabbiha)
حكم الحس
تغير الصفات
حدوث الذوات
فاعل (fa'il)
منفعة (manfa'a)
مضرة (madarra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof denies the use of reason in theology.
Correction: It argues that reason itself recognizes the limits of sensory imagination, not that reason is abandoned.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the four aspects mentioned in the sixth proof?
A) We only observe changes in qualities, not the origination of substances.
B) We cannot conceive creation without pre-existing matter and time.
C) We cannot conceive an agent acting after not acting without a change in his state.
D) We cannot conceive an agent acting except to benefit himself or harm others.

Key Takeaway
The four aspects collectively prove that our knowledge of God's actions is based on rational assent, not on sensory or imaginative perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God creating the world, do you unconsciously imagine it like a craftsman making something? Which of the four aspects most challenges that mental picture, and how will you actively correct your imagination from now on?
?
The fourth aspect says God created without any benefit or harm. If that is true, then your worship does not add to God's benefit nor does disobedience harm Him. How does this truth change the way you view your own acts of worship—are they for you or for Him?
?
You often judge God's actions based on whether they seem beneficial or harmful to you. This proof calls that standard into question. Will you commit to accepting God's decrees even when they appear purposeless by your limited measure? What specific situation in your life right now tests this commitment?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
12 min
The Sixth Proof: Imagination Fails for Divine Actions and Attributes
Al-Razi presents the sixth argument, with four aspects for actions and three for attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi systematically demonstrates that imagination and fantasy are incapable of grasping God's actions and attributes, using seven distinct aspects.

Core Idea
Imagination cannot reach divine actions or attributes; only intellect can.

Al-Razi continues his series of rational proofs for God's transcendence. In this sixth argument, he targets the anthropomorphists (المشبهة) who rely on imagination to understand God. He argues that since even human actions and attributes often exceed imagination, divine ones certainly do. The argument is structured in two clear sections: four aspects concerning actions, three concerning attributes. Each aspect contrasts what we normally perceive or conceive with what we affirm about God.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the clear division into actions (أفعال) and attributes (صفات). Pay attention to the numbered 'aspects' (وجوه) in each section.
The passage is long but logically structured.
2
Identify the two main sections
The passage has two halves: one on divine actions (4 aspects) and one on divine attributes (3 aspects). Mark where each begins. The conclusion ties them together.
Look for 'أما تقرير هذا المعنى في أفعال الله تعالى' and 'وأما تقرير هذا العجز في الصفات'.
3
Study each aspect under 'actions'
For each of the four aspects, ask: what does imagination normally assume? How does God's action contradict that? Al-Razi's pattern is: 'we do not conceive of X except under condition Y, yet God does X without Y'.
The four aspects cover: creation of essences, creation from nothing/timeless, agent change, and motivation.
4
Study each aspect under 'attributes'
Similarly for the three aspects: infinite knowledge, action without tools/fatigue, and all-encompassing hearing/sight. Each shows imagination's limitation.
The first aspect about infinite knowledge is particularly subtle.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Al-Razi wraps up by stating that if imagination fails for actions and attributes (the nearer domain), it is even more incapable for the essence, which is more sublime and obscure. This is an a fortiori argument.
This strengthens the overall case for tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : أن نقول : إن معرفة أفعال الله تعالى وصفاته أقرب إلى العقل من معرفة ذات الله تعالى . ثم إن المشبهة وافقونا على أن معرفة أفعال الله ومعرفة صفاته ، على خلاف حكم الحس والخيال . أما تقرير هذا المعنى في أفعال الله تعالى فذلك من وجوه : الأول : إن الذي رأيناه وشاهدناه ليس إلا تغير الصفات مثل انقلاب الماء المذاب نباتا وانقلاب النبات حيوانا . فأما حدوث الذوات والأجسام فهذا شيء ما شاهدناه وما عرفناه البتة . الثاني : إنا لا نعقل حدوث شيء يصاغ إلا عن مادة مخصوصة وإلا في زمان مخصوص . فإن تكون الخاتم والسوار من غير شيء يصاغ ذلك الخاتم والسوار منه ، غير مفهوم ولا محسوس . وأيضا : فيكون حدوث الخاتم والسوار من غير زمان ووقت غير معقول . ثم إن أرباب الملل والأديان أقروا بذلك واعترفوا به مع أنه غير موهوم ولا محسوس . والثالث : إنا لا نعقل فاعلا يفعل بعد ما لم يكن فاعلا إلا لأجل تغير حاله وتبدل صفته . ثم إن أرباب الملل اعترفوا بأنه خالق للعالم من غير تغير شيء في صفاته ولا تبدل في أحواله . والرابع : إنا لا نعقل فاعلا يفعل فعلا إلا لجلب منفعة أو لدفع مضرة . ثم إنا اعترفنا بأنه تعالى خلق هذا العالم من غير شيء من هذه الأحوال . فيثبت : أن الوهم والخيال معزولان في معرفة أفعال الله تعالى . وأما تقرير هذا العجز في الصفات : فذلك من وجوه : الأول : إنا لا نعقل ذاتا يكون عالما بمعلومات لا نهاية لها على التفصيل دفعة واحدة . فإنا إذا جربنا أنفسنا وجدنا أنها متى اشتغلت باستحضار معلوم معين ، امتنع عليها في تلك الحالة استحضار معلوم آخر . ثم إنا مع ذلك نعتقد بأنه تعالى عالم بجميع المعلومات على التفصيل من غير أن يحصل في ذلك العلم اشتباه والتباس . فكان كونه تعالى عالما بجميع المعلومات أمرا على خلاف مقتضى الوهم والخيال . الثاني : إن كل من فعل فعلا فلا بد له من آلة أو أداة . وأن الأفعال الشاقة تكون سببا للكلال واللغوب والتعب . ثم إنا نعتقد أنه تعالى يدير من العرش إلى تحت الثرى مع أنه منزه المشقة والتعب . والثالث : إنا نعتقد أنه تعالى يسمع أصوات الخلق من العرش إلى ما تحت الثرى ، ويرى الصغير والكبير فوق أطباق السماوات العلى وتحت الأرض السفلى . ومعلوم أن الوهم والخيال لا يتصوران هذه الأحوال . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن الوهم والخيال قاصران عن معرفة أفعال الله تعالى وصفاته . ومع ذلك فإنا نثبت أن أفعال الله تعالى وصفاته على مخالفة الوهم والخيال . ومن المعلوم بالبديهة : أن معرفة كنه الذات أعلى وأجل وأغمض من معرفة الأفعال والصفات . قلما عزلنا الوهم والخيال في المقام الأقرب الأظهر ، فلأن نعزلهما في المقام الأغمض الأصعب كان أولى .
The sixth argument: that we say: knowledge of God’s actions and attributes is closer to the intellect than knowledge of God’s essence. Then the anthropomorphists agreed with us that knowledge of God’s actions and knowledge of His attributes are contrary to the judgment of sense and imagination. As for the elaboration of this meaning regarding God’s actions, it is from aspects: First: that what we have seen and witnessed is only change of attributes, like the transformation of melted water into plant and the transformation of plant into animal. As for the origination of essences and bodies, this is something we have never witnessed nor known at all. Second: we do not conceive of the origination of anything that is formed except from a specific matter and except in a specific time. For the coming into being of a ring and a bracelet from nothing from which that ring and bracelet are formed is inconceivable and imperceptible. Also, the coming into being of the ring and bracelet without time and moment is irrational. Then the people of religious communities affirmed and acknowledged that, even though it is not imagined nor perceived. Third: we do not conceive of an agent acting after not having been an agent except due to a change in its state and alteration of its attribute. Then the people of religious communities acknowledged that He is the creator of the world without any change in His attributes nor alteration in His states. Fourth: we do not conceive of an agent performing an act except for gaining benefit or repelling harm. Then we acknowledged that He the Exalted created this world without any of these conditions. So it is established: that imagination and fantasy are isolated regarding knowledge of God’s actions. And as for the elaboration of this incapacity regarding attributes, it is from aspects: First: we do not conceive of an essence that knows infinite particulars all at once. For if we examine ourselves, we find that whenever we are occupied with recalling a particular known thing, it becomes impossible for us in that state to recall another known thing. Then, despite that, we believe that He the Exalted knows all particulars in detail without any confusion or obscurity occurring in that knowledge. So His being knowledgeable of all particulars is contrary to what imagination and fantasy require. Second: every doer of an action must have a tool or instrument, and that strenuous actions cause fatigue, exhaustion, and weariness. Then we believe that He the Exalted manages from the Throne to beneath the earth, even though He is free from hardship and weariness. Third: we believe that He the Exalted hears the voices of the creatures from the Throne to beneath the earth, and sees the small and the large above the layers of the high heavens and beneath the lowest earth. And it is known that imagination and fantasy cannot conceive these states. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that imagination and fantasy are incapable of knowing God’s actions and attributes. Nevertheless, we affirm that God’s actions and attributes are contrary to imagination and fantasy. And it is obvious by necessity: that knowledge of the inner reality of the essence is higher, more sublime, and more obscure than knowledge of actions and attributes. If we have excluded imagination and fantasy in the nearer and clearer domain, then excluding them in the more obscure and difficult domain is even more appropriate.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth argument, structured in two main sections. First, the author states that knowledge of God's actions and attributes is more accessible to reason than knowledge of His essence. The anthropomorphists (المشبهة) already concede that these are contrary to sense and imagination. The author then elaborates on divine actions through four 'aspects' (وجوه). First, our empirical experience only shows changes of attributes (e.g., water to plant, plant to animal), not the actual creation of essences—we have never witnessed absolute origination. Second, we cannot conceive of something coming into existence from nothing or without time; yet religions affirm creation ex nihilo, which is beyond imagination. Third, we normally require a change in the agent to explain a new act, but God created the world without any change in Himself. Fourth, we naturally think of acts as motivated by benefit or harm, but God created without such motives. Each point shows that God's actions contradict imagination, so imagination is isolated (معزول) regarding them. The second section covers attributes through three aspects. First, our minds cannot hold infinite knowledge simultaneously, yet God knows all particulars at once. Second, our experience ties action to tools and effort, but God acts without any instrument or fatigue. Third, our perception is limited in range, but God hears and sees everything everywhere. These show that attributes also exceed imagination. The conclusion: since knowledge of the essence is even higher and more obscure, if imagination fails regarding actions and attributes (the nearer domain), it is even more appropriate to exclude it regarding the essence.

Key Terms
وهم
خيال
أفعال
صفات
فاعل
عالم
الكلال
اللغوب
التعب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imagination can grasp all aspects of God because we can picture God's actions and attributes using human analogies.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that even in the 'nearer' domain of actions and attributes, imagination fails (e.g., creation from nothing, timeless creation, infinite knowledge). The essence is even more obscure, so imagination must be excluded entirely.

Checkpoint
According to the first aspect of divine actions, why is imagination incapable?
A) Because we have never witnessed the creation of substances from nothing.
B) Because we cannot conceive of a ring being made from nothing.
C) Because God's actions are always motivated by benefit.
D) Because imagination is stronger than intellect.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi demonstrates through seven specific aspects that imagination is fundamentally incapable of understanding God's actions and attributes, and therefore must be excluded when discussing the divine essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God, do you catch yourself picturing limits or human-like qualities? How can you actively replace those images with what the intellect affirms from this proof?
?
This lesson shows that your imagination is a barrier to knowing God. In what specific area of your faith (prayer, trust, hope) have you been relying on what 'makes sense' to you instead of what revelation and reason teach? What will you change today?
?
Al-Razi says that if imagination fails for actions and attributes, it fails even more for the essence. Are you content to leave the essence of God as unknown, or do you try to force it into a mental image? What is the fear behind wanting to 'picture' God?

First Definition of Jawhar: The Indivisible Atom
First of four definitions of substance and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot be a substance in the sense of an indivisible atom that occupies space.

Core Idea
If one atom were necessary, all atoms would be necessary—impossible.

The first definition of jawhar is the extended indivisible atom. This definition only matters if one accepts atomism. Here, the author shows that if the atom were necessary and eternal, then all atoms would share that property, which is absurd. Let's examine the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the first definition
Read the Arabic and translation for the first meaning of jawhar: the extended indivisible atom.
Notice the conditional: it only applies to those who affirm the indivisible atom.
2
Understand the argument from similarity
If one atom is necessary, then all atoms are similar and would also be necessary. This impossible consequence refutes the possibility.
The argument relies on the principle that all atoms are of the same kind.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The author states that this is impossible (muhal), so God cannot be a substance in this sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع في إقامته الدلائل على أنه يمتنع كونه هو هذا اعلم : أن الجوهر قد يراد به أحد أمور أربعة : الأول : إنه المتحيز الذي لا يقبل القسمة . وهو بناء على إثبات الجوهر الفرد، ومن لم يقل به ، يسقط عنه هذا البحث ، ومن قال به قال : إن الجواهر كلها متماثلة ، فلو كان واحد منها قديماً ، واجب الوجود لذاته ، كانت كلها كذلك . وهو محال . والتفسير الثاني للجوهر: إنه الذي يصح طريان الأعراض عليه. فإذا دللنا على أن واجب الوجود لذاته ، يجب أن يكون واجب الوجود في جميع صفاته ، كان ذلك دليلاً على امتناع التغير عليه . والتفسير الثالث للجوهر : هو الذي يضاف إليه شيء آخر، فيحصل من تركيبه مع غيره شيء ثالث . وذلك أيضاً محال لأن تركيبه مع غيره موقوف على كونه متحيزاً في نفسه ، وذلك محال . والتفسير الرابع : قال الشيخ الرئيس أبو علي : ( الجوهر هو الماهية التي إذا وجدت في الأعيان كانت لا في موضع ) . وقد بالغنا في شرح هذا التعريف في باب الجوهر والعرض ، إلا أن هذا التعريف لا يصدق إلا على الماهية التي يكون وجودها مغايراً لماهيتها . قال الشيخ الرئيس : ( وهذا في حق الله محال . لأنـا قـد دللنا : على أن وجود الله تعالى عين ماهيته . وأعلم أن هذه المسألة قد تقدم القول فيها على الاستقصاء . وهو بحث
Chapter Four: On establishing proofs that it is impossible for Him to be this. Know that 'substance' (jawhar) may refer to one of four things: First: that which is extended (mutahayyiz) and does not accept division. This is based on the affirmation of the indivisible atom. Whoever does not affirm it is not engaged in this inquiry. And whoever affirms it says that all atoms are similar, so if one of them were eternal, necessary of existence by itself, then all would be likewise. That is impossible. The second interpretation of substance: that which is susceptible to accidents (a‘rād). So if we prove that the Necessary Existent by itself must be necessary in all His attributes, that is a proof that change (taghayyur) is impossible for Him. The third interpretation of substance: that to which another thing is added, resulting from its composition with another a third thing. That is also impossible, because its composition with another depends on its being extended in itself, and that is impossible. The fourth interpretation: The Chief Master Abu Ali said: 'Substance is the quiddity (māhiyya) which, when it exists in re, is not in a locus.' We have elaborated on this definition in the chapter on substance and accident. However, this definition applies only to a quiddity whose existence is other than its quiddity. The Chief Master said: 'This is impossible with respect to God, because we have proven that the existence of God is identical to His quiddity.' And know that this issue has been discussed thoroughly before. And it is a discussion.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is Chapter Four of the work, which aims to establish proofs that God cannot be a substance (jawhar). The author first clarifies that jawhar can have four different meanings, and then refutes each in turn. First meaning: the indivisible atom that occupies space and cannot be divided. The author notes that this definition only applies if one accepts the theory of atoms. If one does, then all atoms are similar, so if one were eternal and necessary, all would be; this is impossible. Thus God cannot be an atom. Second meaning: that which can receive accidents. Since the Necessary Existent must be necessary in all His attributes, He cannot undergo change, so He cannot be a substance in this sense. Third meaning: that which can combine with another to form a third thing. Composition requires extension, which is impossible for God. Fourth meaning: a quiddity whose existence in re is not in a locus, as defined by Avicenna. But this definition only applies to quiddities where existence is distinct from essence; in God, existence is identical to essence, so this definition does not apply. The author states that this issue has been discussed thoroughly elsewhere. The passage ends with 'and it is a discussion' (wa huwa bahth), indicating a conclusion.

Key Terms
جوهر (jawhar)
متحيز (mutahayyiz)
الجوهر الفرد (indivisible atom)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that calling God a 'substance' in the sense of an atom is plausible because He is indivisible.
Correction: This passage shows that if one atom were necessary, all atoms would be necessary, leading to a contradiction. Therefore, God cannot be an atom.

Checkpoint
According to the first definition, why can't God be a substance?
A) Because God is not extended.
B) Because all atoms are similar, so if one were necessary, all would be.
C) Because atoms do not exist.
D) Because God is indivisible.

Key Takeaway
God cannot be a substance as an indivisible atom because that would entail multiple necessary existents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on God's oneness, do you truly believe that nothing shares His essence? This lesson shows that even if there were indivisible atoms, they would all be similar, so none could be God. How deeply does the uniqueness of God penetrate your heart? Are there any 'atoms' in your life—things you treat as ultimate or necessary—that compete with God's exclusive necessity?
?
The passage uses a conditional argument: if atoms exist, then they are all similar, so none can be necessary. Have you ever considered that your own existence is contingent like an atom? What would it mean for you to truly live as if only God is necessary, and everything else—including your very self—is dependent on Him?
?
The author says that those who do not believe in atoms are not engaged in this inquiry. But in your own life, there are systems of thought you accept that shape how you see God. Are you willing to follow the logic wherever it leads, even if it challenges your comfortable assumptions? This passage demands that our reasoning about God be rigorous and honest—are you ready for that?

Second Definition: Susceptible to Accidents
Second definition of substance and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Necessary Existent cannot be a substance in the sense of being susceptible to accidents.

Core Idea
The Necessary Existent must be necessary in all attributes—no change allowed.

The second definition of jawhar is that which can receive accidents (a‘rād). Since the Necessary Existent is necessary in every respect, it cannot undergo change. Let's see how this refutes the definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the second definition
Read the Arabic and translation for jawhar as that which is susceptible to accidents.
2
Understand the implication of necessity
If God is necessary by Himself, He must be necessary in all His attributes. This means He cannot undergo change (taghayyur).
Change implies potentiality and contingency.
3
Connect to the refutation
A substance that receives accidents is subject to change. Since God cannot change, He is not a substance in this sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع في إقامته الدلائل على أنه يمتنع كونه هو هذا اعلم : أن الجوهر قد يراد به أحد أمور أربعة : الأول : إنه المتحيز الذي لا يقبل القسمة . وهو بناء على إثبات الجوهر الفرد، ومن لم يقل به ، يسقط عنه هذا البحث ، ومن قال به قال : إن الجواهر كلها متماثلة ، فلو كان واحد منها قديماً ، واجب الوجود لذاته ، كانت كلها كذلك . وهو محال . والتفسير الثاني للجوهر: إنه الذي يصح طريان الأعراض عليه. فإذا دللنا على أن واجب الوجود لذاته ، يجب أن يكون واجب الوجود في جميع صفاته ، كان ذلك دليلاً على امتناع التغير عليه . والتفسير الثالث للجوهر : هو الذي يضاف إليه شيء آخر، فيحصل من تركيبه مع غيره شيء ثالث . وذلك أيضاً محال لأن تركيبه مع غيره موقوف على كونه متحيزاً في نفسه ، وذلك محال . والتفسير الرابع : قال الشيخ الرئيس أبو علي : ( الجوهر هو الماهية التي إذا وجدت في الأعيان كانت لا في موضع ) . وقد بالغنا في شرح هذا التعريف في باب الجوهر والعرض ، إلا أن هذا التعريف لا يصدق إلا على الماهية التي يكون وجودها مغايراً لماهيتها . قال الشيخ الرئيس : ( وهذا في حق الله محال . لأنـا قـد دللنا : على أن وجود الله تعالى عين ماهيته . وأعلم أن هذه المسألة قد تقدم القول فيها على الاستقصاء . وهو بحث
Chapter Four: On establishing proofs that it is impossible for Him to be this. Know that 'substance' (jawhar) may refer to one of four things: First: that which is extended (mutahayyiz) and does not accept division. This is based on the affirmation of the indivisible atom. Whoever does not affirm it is not engaged in this inquiry. And whoever affirms it says that all atoms are similar, so if one of them were eternal, necessary of existence by itself, then all would be likewise. That is impossible. The second interpretation of substance: that which is susceptible to accidents (a‘rād). So if we prove that the Necessary Existent by itself must be necessary in all His attributes, that is a proof that change (taghayyur) is impossible for Him. The third interpretation of substance: that to which another thing is added, resulting from its composition with another a third thing. That is also impossible, because its composition with another depends on its being extended in itself, and that is impossible. The fourth interpretation: The Chief Master Abu Ali said: 'Substance is the quiddity (māhiyya) which, when it exists in re, is not in a locus.' We have elaborated on this definition in the chapter on substance and accident. However, this definition applies only to a quiddity whose existence is other than its quiddity. The Chief Master said: 'This is impossible with respect to God, because we have proven that the existence of God is identical to His quiddity.' And know that this issue has been discussed thoroughly before. And it is a discussion.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is Chapter Four of the work, which aims to establish proofs that God cannot be a substance (jawhar). The author first clarifies that jawhar can have four different meanings, and then refutes each in turn. First meaning: the indivisible atom that occupies space and cannot be divided. The author notes that this definition only applies if one accepts the theory of atoms. If one does, then all atoms are similar, so if one were eternal and necessary, all would be; this is impossible. Thus God cannot be an atom. Second meaning: that which can receive accidents. Since the Necessary Existent must be necessary in all His attributes, He cannot undergo change, so He cannot be a substance in this sense. Third meaning: that which can combine with another to form a third thing. Composition requires extension, which is impossible for God. Fourth meaning: a quiddity whose existence in re is not in a locus, as defined by Avicenna. But this definition only applies to quiddities where existence is distinct from essence; in God, existence is identical to essence, so this definition does not apply. The author states that this issue has been discussed thoroughly elsewhere. The passage ends with 'and it is a discussion' (wa huwa bahth), indicating a conclusion.

Key Terms
أعراض (accidents)
تغير (change)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the second definition not apply to God?
A) Because God does not have attributes.
B) Because God's attributes are necessary and unchanging.
C) Because accidents do not exist.
D) Because God is not a substance.

Key Takeaway
God cannot be a substance as that which receives accidents, because His necessity entails immutability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is unchanging in all His attributes, including His knowledge and will, how do you reconcile this with your experience of prayer and supplication? Are you content to submit to a God whose will does not shift, or does this unsettle your desire for a God who responds to you personally? Commit to reflecting on whether you truly trust in His eternal will.
?
The passage argues that God's necessity means He cannot receive accidents. But you, as a contingent being, are constantly subject to change—your moods, your beliefs, your actions. Does this contrast drive you to seek stability in the Unchanging One? How can you orient your life towards Him rather than towards fleeting states?
?
When you consider your own attributes—are they necessary or accidental? Can you point to a single quality in yourself that is as necessary as God's? This passage exposes the radical gap between the Creator and the creature. Does this humble you, or do you still secretly think you can become like Him in some way? Repent from any assumption of self-sufficiency.

Third Definition: Composable Entity
Third definition of substance and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot be a substance in the sense of being a part of a composite.

Core Idea
Composition requires extension, which is impossible for the Necessary Existent.

The third definition defines jawhar as that which can combine with another to form a third thing. This implies composition and dependence on parts. The author argues that composition requires the substance to be extended, which is impossible for God. Let's explore.

Guided Path
1
Read the third definition
Read the Arabic and translation for jawhar as that which can combine with another to form a third thing.
2
Understand the requirement of extension
Composition with another implies that the substance itself must be extended (mutahayyiz), occupying space.
The author states this is impossible for God.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Therefore, God cannot be a substance in this sense because He is not extended.

Fourth Definition: Avicenna's Quiddity Not in a Locus
Fourth definition of substance by Avicenna and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot be a substance in Avicenna's sense of a quiddity whose existence is other than its essence.

Core Idea
God's existence is identical to His essence, so Avicenna's definition does not apply.

The fourth definition, from Avicenna, defines substance as a quiddity that exists not in a subject. But this applies only to quiddities where existence is distinct from essence. In God, existence and essence are identical. Thus, He is not a substance in this sense. The author also references prior discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth definition
Read the Arabic and translation for Avicenna's definition of jawhar.
2
Understand the condition
The definition only applies to a quiddity whose existence is other than its quiddity.
3
Apply to God
Since God's existence is identical to His essence, the definition does not hold for Him.
The author says Avicenna himself stated this is impossible for God.

Conclusion: Identity of Existence and Essence is a Verbal Dispute
The author concludes that the identity claim is a verbal dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that God's existence is identical to His essence is considered a mere verbal dispute that leads to an impossible self-implication.

Core Idea
The identity of existence and essence is a verbal dispute, not a meaningful theological claim.

In this concluding passage, the author rejects the theological position that God's existence is identical to His essence. He argues that such a claim is merely verbal and leads to a logical impossibility if interpreted as asserting that God is a substance. This brief but decisive remark clarifies the limits of rational discourse about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation.
Start by reading the original Arabic and the literal translation to get a sense of the author's concluding statement.
2
Identify the author's verdict.
Notice that the author calls the dispute 'pure verbal' (لفظي محض). This is the key conclusion.
3
Understand the conditional argument.
The author explains that if the claim is interpreted as meaning God is a substance, then it is a self-implication and impossible. See how the conditional 'if you mean... then...' works.
4
Reflect on why it is verbal.
Consider why this makes the dispute merely verbal: it hinges on a definition that leads to a logical error, not on substantive reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لفظي محض ، لأن من يقول : وجوده عين ماهيته ، يقول : إن عنيت بكونه جوهراً ، هذا القدر فليس فيه إلا إلزام الشيء على نفسه وهو محال . والله أعلم .
Pure verbal (dispute), because he who says: His existence is identical to His essence, says: if you mean by His being a substance, [then] this amount is nothing but binding the thing to itself, and that is impossible. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a concluding remark rejecting a certain theological position. The author states that the claim 'His existence is identical to His essence' (وجود عين ماهيته) is a purely verbal dispute (لفظي محض). He argues that if one intends by this to assert that God is a substance (جوهر), then this is merely a self-referential implication (إلزام الشيء على نفسه) and is impossible (محال). Thus the position is invalid.

Key Terms
وجود عين ماهيته
جوهر
إلزام الشيء على نفسه
لفظي محض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the identity claim is a profound truth about God's simplicity.
Correction: The author shows that it is a verbal trick; if taken literally as implying God is a substance, it becomes a meaningless tautology.

Checkpoint
What does the author claim about the statement 'His existence is identical to His essence'?
A) It is true
B) It is a verbal dispute
C) It is a proof of God's simplicity
D) It is a necessary belief

Key Takeaway
The claim that God's existence is identical to His essence is dismissed as a verbal dispute because it leads to an impossible self-reference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever clung to a religious belief that, when you really examined it, turned out to be nothing but a tautology—true by definition but meaningless? How did you respond when you realized it?
?
The author dismisses the identity claim as a verbal dispute. Are there theological concepts you defend that you cannot explain without falling into empty repetition? What would it cost you to admit that and seek deeper understanding?
?
In your prayer or reflection, do you ever use phrases about God that you do not actually understand? How can this lesson move you to purify your speech about Allah from empty formulations?

Second Objection: Composition as Eternal Union
The objector concedes composition but argues it is not impossible for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to the composition argument and how the objector tries to evade the impossibility of composition by positing eternally inseparable parts.

Core Idea
Even if God were composite, the parts could be eternal and inseparable.

This passage presents the second objection to the argument from the throne. The objector concedes that touching the throne implies composition but challenges the assumption that composition is impossible for God. Instead, the objector suggests a special kind of composition where parts are permanent and never separate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note its concessive structure
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objection begins by conceding the point ('sallamnā') before raising questions.
This is a common dialectical move in kalam.
2
Identify the two questions the objector asks
The objector first asks why composition is considered impossible, then proposes an alternative: perhaps the parts are permanently united.
3
Reflect on why this objection is significant
Consider how this objection tests the original argument's assumption that all composition implies contingency. The objector tries to weaken that link.
This shows the importance of defining terms precisely in theological debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أنه يلزم منه كونه تعالى مركبا من الأجزاء والأبعاض . فلم قلتم : إن ذلك محال ؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه وإن كان مؤلفا من الأجزاء ، إلا أنها لا تقبل التفرق والتمزق بل هي واجبة البقاء على نسق واحد ؟
We concede that it necessitates that the Exalted be composed of parts and members. But why do you say that is impossible? And why is it not permissible to say that He, even if composed of parts, yet they do not accept division and fragmentation, but rather they are necessarily permanent in one arrangement?
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is the second objection. The objector makes a concessive argument (sallamnā): even if we accept that touching the throne implies that Allah is composed of parts (tarkīb min al-ajzā' wa al-ab'ād), why should that be impossible? The objector suggests that composition per se is not automatically impossible for God. Specifically, the objector proposes that the parts could be eternal and inseparable, always remaining in the same arrangement without any possibility of division or disintegration. In other words, the composition could be of a different kind from that of physical bodies, which are subject to separation and decay. The objector is challenging the assumption that all composition entails impermanence or contingency. This objection raises a nuanced point: the kalām tradition generally holds that composition implies need for parts, which implies contingency, but here the objector tries to bypass that by positing that the parts are not independent or separable.

Key Terms
مركب
أجزاء
أبعاض
تفرق
تمزق
واجبة البقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objector is defending a composite deity as a genuine theological position.
Correction: The objection is a dialectical move to test the strength of the original argument, not an assertion that God is actually composite.

Checkpoint
What is the objector's main point in the second objection?
A) Composition is impossible for God because it implies need
B) Even if God is composed of parts, the parts could be eternal and inseparable
C) The throne is not actually composed of parts
D) The argument only applies to the throne, not to God

Key Takeaway
The objector's attempt to salvage composition by appealing to eternal inseparable parts shows the dialectical nature of kalam arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you assert something about God, do you base it on rational proof or inherited assumption? This objection challenges us to justify why composition is impossible. Can you provide a reasoned defense, or do you rely on what you've been taught without examination?
?
The objector concedes a key point to advance the discussion. Is there a belief about God's nature that you hold so tightly that you would refuse to even consider an alternative perspective? What would it take for you to re-examine that belief?
?
If you were in a debate about God's attributes, would you be willing to concede a point for the sake of truth, as the objector does here? Or would you cling to every position out of fear of error? Which attitude reflects deeper faith?

The Third Shubah: Knowledge of Particulars Implies Bodiliness
Presents the third argument for divine corporealism based on knowledge of particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument for divine corporealism: how the premise that God knows particulars leads, via mental distinction of identical forms, to the claim that God must be a body occupying space.

Core Idea
Mental distinction of identical forms requires a divisible substrate, leading to anthropomorphism.

This passage presents the third 'doubt' (shubah) used by corporealists to argue that God must be a body. The argument begins with a universally accepted premise—that God knows particulars—and then builds an intricate proof based on how we distinguish two identical mental images. By analyzing the conditions for mental distinction, the argument claims that the knower of such images must be divisible. We will walk through the steps of this argument and see how both philosophers and anthropomorphists respond.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
The passage is a continuous proof, so pay attention to each logical step.
2
Identify the main claim and structure
The passage opens with a premise (God knows particulars) and then states the claim (the knower of corporeals must be a body). The rest is a proof using an example of two squares. Note how the proof moves from external to mental distinction, then to the need for different substrates.
The conclusion is presented as a result of a syllogism.
3
Trace the logical steps of the proof
Follow the argument: start with imagining two equal squares side by side. The distinction between them cannot be due to essence (since they share essence) or external existence (since we can imagine them when nonexistent). So it must be due to accidents, which require different substrates (places). Therefore, the mental images occupy different places, implying the perceiver has parts.
The proof hinges on the principle that distinction by accidents requires distinct substrates.
4
Understand the conclusion and responses
The argument concludes that God, as a knower of particulars, must be a body in space. Then it notes the two opposing positions: philosophers deny God knows particulars, anthropomorphists accept the conclusion. Recognize that this is presented as a doubt to be refuted, not the author's own view.
This illustrates how the same premise can lead to different theological stances.
5
Reflect on the nature of the argument
Consider that this is a 'shubah'—a problematic argument that seems plausible but is ultimately flawed. Think about where the argument might break down from an orthodox perspective.
The author (likely al-Razi) will later refute this argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : لا نزاع بين المسلمين في أنه تعالى عالم بالجزئيات فنقول : العالم بالجسمانيات لابد وأن يكون جسمها حاصلا في الحيز . والدليل عليه : أنا إذا تخيلنا مربعا مجنحا بمربعين متساويين. فنحن نميز في خيالنا بين هذين المربعين الواقعين على هذين الطرفين ، وهذا الامتياز إما أن يكون واقعا في الوجود الخارجي ، أو في الوجود الذهني. والأول باطل لأنه يمكننا أن نتخيل هذه الصورة حال كونها معدومة في الخارج. فيثبت : أن هذا الامتياز واقع في الذهن ، وليس ذلك الامتياز بسبب حصول الامتياز في الماهية ولوازمها ، لأن الماهية ولوازمها مشترك فيها بين هذين المربعين ، فوجب أن يكون سبب هذا الامتياز شيء من العوارض. لكن الامتياز في العوراض لا يحصل إلا عند التغاير في القابل فإن الصورتين لو اجتمعنا في محل واحد ، وفي قابل واحد ، لكان كل ما يعرض عارضا لأحدهما ، كان هو بعينه عارضـا للآخر ، وحينئذ بصير ذلك العارض مشتركا فيه . فلا يكون موجبا للتغاير والتباين فثبت أنه لا يمكن حصول الامتياز في الذهن بين هذين المربعين الواقعين على الجانبين إلا إذا كان محل أحدهما غير محل الآخر ، فثبت أن المدرك لهذه الصورة لا بد وان يكون جسما منقسمها ، فثبت أنه تعالى مدرك للجزئيات ، وثبت أن كل من كان مدركا للجزئيات ، وجب أن يكون جسما حاصلا في الحيز والجهة ، ينتج : أنه تعالى حاصل في الحيز . واعلم : أنه ثبت بالوجه الذي ذكرناه : أن إدراك الجزئيات لا يحصل إلا عند حصول الجسمية . ثم إن الفلاسفة قالوا : لكنه ليس بجسم ، فيمتنع كونه مدركا للجزئيات . والمجسمة قالوا : لكنه مدرك للجزئيات ، فوجب كونه جسمها.
The third doubt: There is no dispute among Muslims that He, exalted, is knower of particulars. So we say: The knower of corporeal things must necessarily be a body occupying space. And the proof for it is: When we imagine a square with two equal squares, we distinguish in our imagination between these two squares located on these two sides. This distinction is either actual in external existence or in mental existence. The first is false because we can imagine this image while it is nonexistent externally. Thus it is established that this distinction is in the mind, and that distinction is not due to a distinction in the essence and its concomitants, because the essence and its concomitants are shared between these two squares. So it must be that the cause of this distinction is something from among the accidents. But distinction in accidents only occurs when there is a difference in the substrate, for if the two forms were combined in one place and in one substrate, then every accident that occurs to one would be the very accident occurring to the other, and then that accident would become shared. So it would not cause diversity and separation. Thus it is established that distinction in the mind between these two squares located on the two sides is impossible unless the place of one is other than the place of the other. So it is established that the perceiver of this image must necessarily be a divisible body. So it is established that He, exalted, is a knower of particulars, and it is established that everyone who is a knower of particulars must necessarily be a body occupying space and direction. The result: He, exalted, occupies space. And know: it is established by the method we mentioned that perception of particulars only occurs when bodiliness occurs. Then the philosophers said: But He is not a body, so it is impossible for Him to be a knower of particulars. And the anthropomorphists said: But He is a knower of particulars, so it is necessary that He be a body.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument (shubah) for divine corporealism, based on God's knowledge of particulars. The argument begins with the premise that all Muslims agree God knows particulars. Then it claims that the knower of corporeal things must be a body in space. The proof uses an example: we imagine two equal squares side by side and distinguish them in our mind. This distinction cannot be due to external existence because we can imagine them when they don't exist externally. So the distinction is mental. The distinction cannot be due to essence or its concomitants since both squares share the same essence. So it must be due to accidents. But distinction by accidents requires different substrates; otherwise, if they shared the same substrate, the same accident would apply to both, eliminating distinction. Therefore, the two mental images must have different places in the perceiver's mind, implying the perceiver (the mind) must have parts and be a body. Hence, God, who knows particulars, must be a body occupying space. The passage notes that philosophers (falasifa) reject this by denying that God knows particulars, while anthropomorphists (mujassima) accept the conclusion.

Key Terms
مجانبة
مباينة
امتياز
قابل
ماهية
لوازم
عوارض
جسم
حيز
جهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that this argument is presented as a valid proof for God's corporeality by the author.
Correction: The argument is presented as a 'shubah' (problematic doubt) that the author will refute. It is not the author's own position, but a representation of the anthropomorphists' reasoning.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise that leads the argument to conclude that God must be a body?
A) God knows all things.
B) Mental distinction of two identical squares requires different locations in a divisible substrate.
C) The philosophers deny God's knowledge of particulars.
D) Muslims agree that God is a knower of particulars.

Key Takeaway
The third shubah argues that because God knows particulars and mental distinction of identical forms requires a divisible substrate, God must be a body—but this argument is presented as a problematic doubt to be refuted, not as sound doctrine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim that God knows every detail of your life—your secret thoughts, your private sins. But does your heart truly believe that His knowledge does not reduce Him to something like a human knower? Or do you unconsciously imagine Him as a being with limits, bound by space and time? Let this lesson challenge your mental image of God.
?
When you encounter arguments like this one, do you feel a temptation to 'make God manageable' by fitting Him into your categories? What would it cost you to let go of all mental images and submit to His absolute transcendence?
?
If God's knowledge of particulars is not like ours, how should that change the way you relate to His awareness of your every thought and action? Are you living as if He is truly present and knowing, or as if He is distant and uncaring?

The Fourth Doubt: Attributes Imply Bodiliness
Presents the fourth argument for divine corporealism based on attributes subsisting in a subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument for divine corporealism, which claims that being described with attributes logically requires God to be a body occupying space.

Core Idea
The argument assumes attributes must be located in a direction to be intelligible.

This lesson examines the fourth doubt (shubah) raised by anthropomorphists. They argue that since God is described with attributes like knowledge and power, and since we rationally conceive of attributes as subsisting in a subject that occupies a specific direction, God must be a body in space. We will walk through their reasoning, see how they force a dilemma, and note the opposing responses from the Mu'tazila and the anthropomorphists.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the argument is structured: one premise about God's attributes, another about what attributes imply, and a conclusion. Also note the two opposing responses at the end.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two premises
The argument has two key premises: (1) God is an essence described with attributes. (2) Anything described with attributes must be a body in space. Locate these in the text.
The text first states the doubt, then explains the first premise, then the second.
3
Understand the reasoning for the second premise
The second premise is justified by analyzing the meaning of an attribute subsisting in an essence. The author claims that the only intelligible meaning is that the attribute is present in a specific direction, following the location of its subject. Without that, no difference remains between essence and attribute.
This is the crux of the argument.
4
See how the conclusion follows
If every attribute-bearing subject must occupy space, then God, having attributes, must occupy space. The conclusion is that God is a body in a specific direction (al-ḥayyiz).
This is the corporealist conclusion.
5
Note the two responses
The Mu'tazila deny that God occupies space, so they deny that God has attributes. The anthropomorphists accept the conclusion and affirm that God occupies space. These are the two horns of a dilemma.
The author (Razi) presents this as a doubt to be refuted later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة : إنه تعالى ذات موصوف بالصفات ، وكل ما كان كذلك فلا بد وأن يكون جسما حاصلا في الحيز . بيان الأول : أنه تعالى عالم قادر ، والمعقول من كونه ذاتا قائما بالنفس ، مغاير للمعقول من كونه عالما قادرا . ولذلك فإنه يمكننا أن نعقل كونه ذاتا قائمة بالنفس مع الشك في كونه تعالى عالما قادراً ، والمعلوم مغاير لغير المعلوم، فيثبت : أن العلم والقدرة صفتان قائمتان بالذات، وبيان أن كل ما كان موصوفا بالصفات فلا بد وأن يكون جسما حاصلا في الحيز : هو أن المعقول من قيام الصفة بالذات كون الصفة حاصلة في تلك الجهة المعينة ، تبعا لحصول محلها في تلك الجهة. فإنا إذا رفعنا هذا المعنى من العقل فحينئذ لا يبقى في العقل تفاوت بين الذات وبين الصفة . وحينئذ لا يكون كون أحدهما ذاتا والآخر يجب أن يكون حاصلا في الحيز ، لزم القطع بكونه تعالى حاصلا في الحيز. والحاصل : أنه ثبت أنه لا معنى لا تصاف الذات بالصفة إلا حصول الصفة في الجهة المعينة ، تبعا لحصول محلها في تلك الجهة · ثم إن المعتزلة قالوا : لكنه يمتنع كونه تعالى حاصلا في الحيز ، فيمتنع كونه موصوفا بالصفات · والمجسمة قالوا : لكنه ثبت كونه موصوفا بالصفات ، فوجب الجزم بكونه تعالى حاصلا في الحيز .
The fourth doubt: He, exalted, is an essence described with attributes, and everything that is like that must necessarily be a body occupying space. Explanation of the first: He, exalted, is knowing, powerful. The intelligible of His being an essence subsisting by itself is different from the intelligible of His being knowing, powerful. Therefore, we can conceive of His being an essence subsisting by itself while doubting whether He, exalted, is knowing, powerful. The known is different from what is not known. Thus it is established that knowledge and power are two attributes subsisting in the essence. And the explanation that everything that is described with attributes must necessarily be a body occupying space is that the intelligible of the attribute subsisting in the essence is that the attribute is present in that specific direction, following the presence of its subject in that direction. For if we remove this meaning from the mind, then no difference remains in the mind between the essence and the attribute. Then, since one is an essence and the other must occupy space, it necessarily follows that He, exalted, occupies space. The conclusion: it is established that there is no meaning to the essence being described by an attribute except the attribute's presence in the specific direction, following the presence of its subject in that direction. Then the Mu'tazila said: But it is impossible for Him, exalted, to occupy space, so it is impossible for Him to be described with attributes. And the anthropomorphists said: But it is established that He is described with attributes, so it is necessary to decisively assert that He, exalted, occupies space.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument for divine corporealism, based on the relationship between a subject and its attributes. The argument starts with the premise that God is an essence described with attributes (like knowledge and power). It then claims that anything described with attributes must be a body occupying space. To prove this, it first establishes that the attributes are distinct from the essence: we can conceive of the essence without conceiving the attributes, so they are distinct realities. Then it argues that the very meaning of an attribute 'subsisting' in an essence is that the attribute is located in a specific direction, because it follows the location of its subject. If we deny that, there would be no intelligible difference between the essence and the attribute. Therefore, the essence must occupy space for the attribute to be in a specific direction. The conclusion is that God occupies space. The Mu'tazila respond by denying that God can occupy space, thus denying He has attributes. The anthropomorphists accept the conclusion.

Key Terms
ذات
صفات
قيام الصفة بالذات
جهة
حيز
محل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the argument proves divine corporeality conclusively.
Correction: The argument is presented as a doubt (shubah) to be refuted. Later, scholars like Razi show that the premise about subsistence requiring spatial location is false; attributes can subsist in a non-spatial subject.

Checkpoint
What is the key reasoning for the claim that an essence with attributes must be a body?
A) Because attributes are identical to the essence.
B) Because the only intelligible meaning of attribute-subsistence is the attribute being in a specific direction following its subject.
C) Because the Mu'tazila deny God's attributes.
D) Because God is powerful.

Key Takeaway
The fourth doubt forces a dilemma: either deny attributes (Mu'tazila) or affirm divine corporeality (anthropomorphists), but orthodox theology rejects the premise that attributes require spatial location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The doubt forces a dilemma: either deny that God has attributes or affirm that He occupies space. Have you ever felt that your limited human understanding of 'knowledge' or 'power' forces you to imagine God in human terms? What is one attribute of God that you habitually imagine as if it were located somewhere? How can you train yourself to think of it without spatial imagination?
?
The Mu'tazila denied attributes to avoid anthropomorphism. Are you ever tempted to simplify Allah's attributes to avoid confusion, even though the Quran describes Him with them? What would you lose if you denied one of Allah's attributes?
?
The anthropomorphists affirmed attributes but ended up making God like a body. Reflect: Do you sometimes unconsciously picture Allah in your mind when you pray? Does this lesson challenge you to purify your concept of Allah from any image? What concrete step can you take to worship Allah without spatial or bodily imagination?

The Rational Distinction Between Essence and Attributes
Razi proves the reality of attributes by conceptual distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi proves that divine attributes are real and distinct from the essence by analyzing rational concepts.

Core Idea
Essence and attributes are rationally distinct; therefore attributes are real.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi begins by examining the rational concepts of essence and attributes. He argues that we can conceive of God's essence without conceiving of His knowledge or power, proving that these concepts are distinct. This establishes that attributes like knowledge and power are real qualities subsisting in the essence. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the logical progression from distinction to establishment.
2
Identify the key distinction
The passage claims the rational concept of essence is different from that of attributes. Verify this by seeing that one can be conceived without the other.
3
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that since concepts are distinct and known separately, the attributes are real and subsist in the essence.
This is a foundational proof for real attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعقول من كونه ذاتا قائما بالنفس ، مغاير للمعقول من كونه عالما قادرا . ولذلك فإنه يمكننا أن نعقل كونه ذاتا قائمة بالنفس مع الشك في كونه تعالى عالما قادراً ، والمعلوم مغاير لغير المعلوم، فيثبت : أن العلم والقدرة صفتان قائمتان بالذات، وبيان أن كل ما كان موصوفا بالصفات فلا بد وأن يكون جسما حاصلا في الحيز : هو أن المعقول من قيام الصفة بالذات كون الصفة حاصلة في تلك الجهة المعينة ، تبعا لحصول محلها في تلك الجهة. فإنا إذا رفعنا هذا المعنى من العقل فحينئذ لا يبقى في العقل تفاوت بين الذات وبين الصفة . وحينئذ لا يكون كون أحدهما ذاتا والآخر يجب أن يكون حاصلا في الحيز ، لزم القطع بكونه تعالى حاصلا في الحيز. والحاصل : أنه ثبت أنه لا معنى لا تصاف الذات بالصفة إلا حصول الصفة في الجهة المعينة ، تبعا لحصول محلها في تلك الجهة · ثم إن المعتزلة قالوا : لكنه يمتنع كونه تعالى حاصلا في الحيز ، فيمتنع كونه موصوفا بالصفات · والمجسمة قالوا : لكنه ثبت كونه موصوفا بالصفات ، فوجب الجزم بكونه تعالى حاصلا في الحيز .
And the rational concept of His being an essence subsisting in itself is distinct from the rational concept of His being knowing, powerful. Therefore, we can conceive of His being an essence subsisting in itself while doubting His being, exalted, knowing, powerful, and the known is distinct from the unknown, so it is established that knowledge and power are two attributes subsisting in the essence. And the explanation that everything described by attributes must necessarily be a body occurring in space is that the rational concept of the subsistence of the attribute in the essence is that the attribute occurs in that specific direction, following the occurrence of its locus in that direction. For if we remove this meaning from the intellect, then there remains no distinction in the intellect between the essence and the attribute. Then since one of them is an essence and the other must occur in space, it follows necessarily that He, exalted, occurs in space. The conclusion: It is established that there is no meaning to the attribution of an essence by an attribute except the occurrence of the attribute in the specific direction, following the occurrence of its locus in that direction. Then the Mu'tazila said: But it is impossible for Him, exalted, to occur in space, so it is impossible for Him to be described by attributes. And the Mujassima said: But it is established that He is described by attributes, so it is necessary to affirm that He, exalted, occurs in space.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage defines the rational concept of attributes and argues that if attributes are understood as really distinct from the essence, they must be located in a direction, leading to anthropomorphism. The author begins by distinguishing the rational concept of essence from that of attributes. He uses an example: we can conceive of God's essence without conceiving of His knowledge or power, showing that the concepts are different. Therefore, knowledge and power are real attributes. Then he explains that the only way to understand the relation of an attribute to an essence is that the attribute occurs in the direction of the essence. If that meaning is removed, no distinction remains. Hence, if one insists on real attributes, one must conclude that God is in a direction. This leads to the Mu'tazila denying attributes to avoid spatiality, and the Mujassima affirming direction to preserve attributes. The passage shows the logical extremes.

Key Terms
ذات (essence)
صفة (attribute)
قائم بالنفس (subsisting in itself)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine attributes are not distinct from the essence and are merely human concepts.
Correction: Razi shows that the rational concepts are distinct, and we can conceive of essence without attributes, proving they are real and not mere mental constructs.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do we know that knowledge and power are distinct attributes of God?
A) Because God told us so.
B) Because we can conceive of His essence without them.
C) Because they are opposites.
D) Because they are mentioned separately in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The rational distinction between essence and attributes proves that attributes like knowledge and power are real and subsist in the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God's knowledge is a real attribute distinct from His essence, how does that change your certainty that He knows every thought you have? Are you living as if His knowledge is merely a concept?
?
When you doubt God's attributes—such as His power—are you still affirming His essence? Reflect on a moment when you questioned God's power. Did that question shake your belief in His existence? According to Razi, you can doubt the attribute while still affirming the essence. How does that help you in times of trial?
?
The passage shows that the rational concept of God's essence is distinct from His attributes. Are you guilty of reducing God to a concept or to His attributes? How can you worship Him as an essence with real attributes?

The Spatial Implication of Attributes and Sectarian Responses
Razi derives spatial location from real attributes and presents sectarian positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that if attributes are real, they require spatial location, and how the Mu'tazila and Mujassima responded.

Core Idea
Real attributes imply direction; Mu'tazila reject attributes, Mujassima accept direction.

After establishing that attributes are real, Razi examines the implication: the only way to understand an attribute subsisting in an essence is that the attribute occurs in the direction of the essence. This leads to the conclusion that God must be in space. The Mu'tazila avoid this by denying attributes, while the Mujassima accept it. This lesson explores this dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Note how the argument builds from the meaning of attribute subsistence.
2
Follow the logic to spatiality
The passage argues that if an attribute subsists in an essence, the only rational meaning is that the attribute occurs in the direction of the essence. If we remove that meaning, no distinction remains.
This forces a conclusion: if attributes are real, God must be in a direction (space).
3
See the two responses
The Mu'tazila deny attributes to avoid spatiality; the Mujassima accept spatiality to preserve attributes. Notice how each takes one horn of the dilemma.
Razi is presenting the logical extremes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعقول من كونه ذاتا قائما بالنفس ، مغاير للمعقول من كونه عالما قادرا . ولذلك فإنه يمكننا أن نعقل كونه ذاتا قائمة بالنفس مع الشك في كونه تعالى عالما قادراً ، والمعلوم مغاير لغير المعلوم، فيثبت : أن العلم والقدرة صفتان قائمتان بالذات، وبيان أن كل ما كان موصوفا بالصفات فلا بد وأن يكون جسما حاصلا في الحيز : هو أن المعقول من قيام الصفة بالذات كون الصفة حاصلة في تلك الجهة المعينة ، تبعا لحصول محلها في تلك الجهة. فإنا إذا رفعنا هذا المعنى من العقل فحينئذ لا يبقى في العقل تفاوت بين الذات وبين الصفة . وحينئذ لا يكون كون أحدهما ذاتا والآخر يجب أن يكون حاصلا في الحيز ، لزم القطع بكونه تعالى حاصلا في الحيز. والحاصل : أنه ثبت أنه لا معنى لا تصاف الذات بالصفة إلا حصول الصفة في الجهة المعينة ، تبعا لحصول محلها في تلك الجهة · ثم إن المعتزلة قالوا : لكنه يمتنع كونه تعالى حاصلا في الحيز ، فيمتنع كونه موصوفا بالصفات · والمجسمة قالوا : لكنه ثبت كونه موصوفا بالصفات ، فوجب الجزم بكونه تعالى حاصلا في الحيز .
And the rational concept of His being an essence subsisting in itself is distinct from the rational concept of His being knowing, powerful. Therefore, we can conceive of His being an essence subsisting in itself while doubting His being, exalted, knowing, powerful, and the known is distinct from the unknown, so it is established that knowledge and power are two attributes subsisting in the essence. And the explanation that everything described by attributes must necessarily be a body occurring in space is that the rational concept of the subsistence of the attribute in the essence is that the attribute occurs in that specific direction, following the occurrence of its locus in that direction. For if we remove this meaning from the intellect, then there remains no distinction in the intellect between the essence and the attribute. Then since one of them is an essence and the other must occur in space, it follows necessarily that He, exalted, occurs in space. The conclusion: It is established that there is no meaning to the attribution of an essence by an attribute except the occurrence of the attribute in the specific direction, following the occurrence of its locus in that direction. Then the Mu'tazila said: But it is impossible for Him, exalted, to occur in space, so it is impossible for Him to be described by attributes. And the Mujassima said: But it is established that He is described by attributes, so it is necessary to affirm that He, exalted, occurs in space.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage defines the rational concept of attributes and argues that if attributes are understood as really distinct from the essence, they must be located in a direction, leading to anthropomorphism. The author begins by distinguishing the rational concept of essence from that of attributes. He uses an example: we can conceive of God's essence without conceiving of His knowledge or power, showing that the concepts are different. Therefore, knowledge and power are real attributes. Then he explains that the only way to understand the relation of an attribute to an essence is that the attribute occurs in the direction of the essence. If that meaning is removed, no distinction remains. Hence, if one insists on real attributes, one must conclude that God is in a direction. This leads to the Mu'tazila denying attributes to avoid spatiality, and the Mujassima affirming direction to preserve attributes. The passage shows the logical extremes.

Key Terms
جهة (direction)
حيز (space)
جسد (body)
مجسمة (Mujassima)
معتزلة (Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming attributes does not necessarily imply corporeality; the Mu'tazila reject attributes to avoid it, while the Mujassima accept direction.
Correction: The passage shows that if one takes the rational meaning of attribute subsistence, it leads to spatiality. The Mu'tazila and Mujassima are consistent in their respective positions based on accepting or rejecting that implication.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the Mu'tazila conclude from the argument about spatiality?
A) God is in space.
B) God has no attributes.
C) God is a body.
D) Attributes are metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
The logical consequence of real attributes is spatial location, which the Mu'tazila reject and the Mujassima accept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the attributes of Allah, do you find yourself more drawn to affirming His transcendence even if it means denying real attributes (like the Mu'tazila) or accepting the logical consequences of those attributes even if it feels uncomfortable (like the Mujassima)? What does your hesitation reveal about your trust in your own intellect versus revelation?
?
The passage presents a rational dilemma: either deny attributes or accept spatial location. Are you willing to let the tension remain unresolved, or do you feel compelled to pick a side? How does your response affect your humility before the unknowable reality of Allah?
?
In your moments of worship, do you ever feel that God is 'somewhere' listening? How does the logical argument of this passage challenge or affirm that feeling? What does your heart choose to believe, and why?
