The Sixth Shubha: Fitra as Evidence for God Being Above
Opponents argue human instinct (fitra) indicates God is above.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponents' argument that human instinct (fitra) supports the belief that God is above the world, and why this argument is considered problematic.

Core Idea
Fitra is cited as proof for God's 'above,' but instinct alone cannot determine divine location.

The opponents of the Ash'ari position on divine direction often appeal to a seemingly universal phenomenon: when people pray or humble themselves, they look up and raise their hands. They argue that this instinctive behavior reflects an innate belief that God is above. In this lesson, we examine the sixth shubha which bases its claim on fitra (instinct). We'll see how they reason from human nature to a theological conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the opponents' argument. Note how they claim that all people instinctively look upward when supplicating.
The passage uses words like 'majbūlūn' (instinctively disposed) and 'fī al-ṣalāt' (in prayer).
2
Identify the key observation
The opponents point to the universal human behavior of directing eyes and hands upward during prayer. They argue this is not taught but innate.
They use 'al-jibilla al-aṣliyya' (original instinct) to emphasize its primordial nature.
3
Analyze the logical structure
The argument moves from observed behavior (upward gaze) to a universal belief (God is above) to a conclusion that this belief is true because it is rooted in fitra.
Consider: Does observable behavior necessarily reflect a correct theological belief? This is where the response will focus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة السادسة : قالوا : جميع العقلاء مجبولون بأصل الخلقة على اعتقاد أن الله تعالى موجود فوق العالم. فإنا نراهم مع اختلاف أديانهم، وتباين مذاهبهم مجبولين عند الدعاء والخضوع والخشوع على رمي الأبصار إلى جهة فوق ، وعلى رفع الأيدي إلى تلك الجهة ، ولو أن الناس تركوا على أصل الخلقة ، ومقتضى الجبلة الأصلية لم يعتقدوا إلا أن إله العالم موجود في جهة فوق . وإذا كان هذا المعنى مما تشهد به فطرة العقل ، وجب أن يكون القول به حقا .
The sixth doubt: They said: All rational people are instinctively disposed by original creation to believe that God is above the world. For we see them, despite differences in religions and doctrines, disposed when supplicating, humbling, and humiliating themselves to direct their eyes upward and to raise their hands to that direction. If people were left to their original creation and the requirement of original instinct, they would not believe except that the God of the world exists in the direction of above. And since this meaning is witnessed by the natural disposition of reason, it is necessary that the statement of it is true.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth shubha (doubt) from opponents who argue that the natural human instinct (fitra) proves God's existence in a direction above. They observe that all people, regardless of religion, when in prayer or humility, instinctively look upward and raise their hands. This, they claim, is a universal, innate belief that God is in the direction of above. Since such a belief is rooted in original human nature (al-jibilla al-asliyya), it must be true.

Key Terms
فطرة
جبلة
جهة فوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because people instinctively look up in prayer, this proves God is literally in a direction above.
Correction: Fitra only indicates a natural inclination to recognize a higher power, but it does not specify physical location. Theologians argue that God is above in a transcendental sense, not spatially.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the sixth shubha?
A) Scriptural texts.
B) Rational arguments from causality.
C) Universal human instinct (fitra).
D) Consensus of scholars.

Key Takeaway
The fitra argument for God's direction is based on observed human behavior, but theologians argue that fitra does not provide knowledge of God's essence or location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray and find yourself instinctively looking upward, do you ever pause to ask whether that instinct truly reveals where God is? What does this lesson force you to reconsider about the assumptions you carry into worship? How will you train yourself to submit to revelation over instinct?
?
This lesson shows that even universal human behavior can lead to a flawed theological conclusion. In what areas of your faith do you rely on 'common sense' or popular belief rather than careful study? Are you willing to let go of comfortable assumptions if they contradict what the Quran and Sunnah teach about Allah's transcendence? What will that cost you?

Proof for Divine Direction from the Qur'an and Scriptures
Author argues from scriptural abundance and silence that direction is affirmed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural argument for affirming direction (jiha) for Allah based on the abundance of directional verses and the absence of any negation in the Qur'an.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's silence on negating direction is as telling as its explicit affirmations.

In this passage, the author makes a compelling scriptural case for divine direction. He points to the overwhelming number of Qur'anic verses that use directional language and notes that not a single verse negates direction. This combination of abundance and absence, he argues, proves that the prophets consistently taught that Allah is in a direction. The argument is not just literal; it uses logical inference from what the scripture emphasizes and what it omits.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the author's flow of argument.
Notice the repeated emphasis on abundance and absence.
2
Identify the two key points: abundance and absence
The author lists many verses affirming direction and then notes the complete absence of any verse negating it. Identify these two prongs.
3
Reflect on the logical inference
The author uses an argument from silence: if negation were true, it would have been mentioned. Consider why this inference is powerful in scriptural reasoning.
This is a classic kalām method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سبع مرات (١) وذكر أيضا : ( وهو القاهر فوق عباده (٣) ، وقوله : « إليه يصعد الكلم الطيب (٣) ، والألفاظ الدالة على النزول والتنزيل كثيرة جدا والآيات المشتملة على لفظ إلى الدالة على انتهاء الغاية خارجة عن الحد ، وليس في القرآن البتة لفظ يدل على نفي الجهة . فلو كان الدين الحق عنده نفي الجهة ، لكان من الواجب أن يذكر ذلك مرة واحدة . فلما أثبت الجهة في آيات لا حصر لها ، ولا حد لها ، ولم يذكر البتة نفي الجهة ، علمنا أنه تعالى كان مصرا على إثبات الجهة . وأما الأخبار المنقولة عن الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم فلا حصر لها ولا حد . وأما التوراة : فمملوءة من هذه المعاني، وهذا يدل على أن الانبياء والرسل عليهم السلام كانوا متفقين على إثبات الجهة الله تعالى
Seven times (1) and He also mentioned: 'And He is the Subjugator over His servants' (3), and His saying: 'To Him ascends the good word' (3). The words indicating descent and sending down are very numerous, and the verses containing the word 'to' indicating the end of the limit are beyond counting. And in the entire Qur'an, there is not a single word indicating the negation of direction. So if the true religion in His view was the negation of direction, it would have been necessary to mention that once. But when He affirmed direction in countless verses, and never mentioned the negation of direction, we know that He — exalted is He — was insistent on affirming direction. As for the reports transmitted from the Messenger (peace be upon him), they are without limit or number. And as for the Torah, it is filled with these meanings. This indicates that the prophets and messengers (peace be upon them) were in agreement on affirming direction for Allah Almighty.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents a scriptural proof for affirming that Allah is in a direction (jiha). The author begins by citing multiple Qur'anic verses that use directional language, such as 'above His servants' and 'to Him ascends the good word,' and notes that these are abundant. He then makes a key argument from silence: if the true religion taught that Allah has no direction, the Qur'an would have explicitly stated that somewhere, but it never does. The absence of any negation, combined with the profusion of affirmations, proves that the prophets consistently taught direction. The author also draws on the Torah and hadith to show that this teaching is universal among the messengers.

Key Terms
جهة (jiha)
نفي الجهة (nafy al-jiha)
إثبات الجهة (ithbat al-jiha)
فوق (fawq)
تنزيل (tanzil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the author's argument is a literalist proof-texting, but it is actually a logical inference based on the pattern of scripture.
Correction: The argument relies on the logical principle that if negation were true, scripture would have mentioned it given the abundance of affirmations.

Checkpoint
What is the core argument the author makes to prove that Allah has a direction?
A) The Qur'an explicitly says Allah is in a direction.
B) The Qur'an has many verses affirming direction but none negating it, so the true teaching is affirmation.
C) The prophets disagreed on this point.
D) The Torah negates direction completely.

Key Takeaway
The scriptural proof for divine direction rests on the combination of abundant affirmations and complete absence of negations in the Qur'an and other scriptures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author says the Qur'an never negates direction, so it must be true. But do you truly submit to this scriptural pattern in your heart, or do you find yourself influenced by modern ideas that reject direction? What would it cost you to accept what the prophets agreed upon?
?
Imagine standing before Allah and He asks you: 'Why did you deny or affirm My direction?' Would your answer be based on the same evidence the prophets presented, or on something else? Are you willing to let the silence of the Qur'an on negation speak as loudly as its affirmations?
?
If you were to be consistent with the way you handle this attribute, how would you treat other attributes like Allah's hand or His face? Is your faith built on the full scriptural witness, or do you selectively accept what is convenient? Challenge yourself to embrace every attribute Allah has affirmed.

Refuting the Ta'wil Objection
Author refutes the claim that directional texts require metaphorical interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument against the claim that directional scriptures can be dismissed through metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil).

Core Idea
If tanzih were the true religion, God would have said so explicitly.

Having presented the proof from direction, the author now anticipates a common objection: that such texts can be interpreted metaphorically (ta'wil). He refutes this by appealing to scriptural emphasis. If divine transcendence (tanzih) were the intended teaching, he argues, it would be clearly and repeatedly stated. The absence of such explicit statements shows that the literal meaning is the intended one.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Some claim that directional verses in scripture can be interpreted metaphorically (ta'wil). Read the passage to see how the author presents this objection.
The objection is the starting point of the argument.
2
Follow the hypothetical
The author sets up a conditional: If tanzih (transcendence) were the true religion, scripture would clearly and explicitly establish it. Notice the logic: if X, then Y.
3
Observe the conclusion
Since no such explicit statement of tanzih exists in any divine book, the author concludes that the literal meaning (direction) is correct. This is a straightforward logical deduction.
The absence of evidence for tanzih is taken as evidence against it.
4
Reflect on the implications
The author asserts that the prophets' doctrine is the literal one. Any claim to the contrary contradicts their message. Think about why this is a powerful refutation.
This argument challenges the need for ta'wil by showing it is not scripturally justified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس لقائل أن يقول : إنا نذكر لهذه الألفاظ تأويلات (٤) ، وذلك لأن الدين الحق لو كان هو التنزيه الذي ذكرتموه ، لكان من الواجب أن يرد في هذه الكتب الإلهية ما يدل على تقرير هذا الأصل العظيم ، وعلى تأكيده بصريح اللفظ ، حتى يصير الحق معلوما ، وحينئذ نصرف بقية الألفاظ إلى التأويل . ولما لم يرد في شيء من الكتب الإلهية البتة ما يدل على التنزيه الذي تذكرونه (١) ورأيناها معلومة بالألفاظ الدالة على كونه تعالى موجودا في الفوق . علمنا : أن مذهب الأنبياء والرسل - عليهم السلام - هو هذا المذهب . وأن الذي تقولونه ، مخالف لأديانهم ومذاهبهم. فهذا تقرير شبهات القوم في هذا الباب
And it is not for anyone to say: 'We mention for these words metaphorical interpretations (4).' That is because if the true religion were the transcendence (tanzih) that you mention, then it would have been necessary for there to appear in these divine books something indicating the establishment of this great principle and its emphasis in explicit wording, so that the truth would become known. Then we would turn the remaining words to metaphorical interpretation. But when nothing appeared in any of the divine books at all indicating the transcendence that you mention (1), and we saw them filled with words indicating that He—exalted is He—exists above, we know that the doctrine of the prophets and messengers (peace be upon them) is this doctrine. And what you say is contrary to their religions and doctrines. This is the exposition of the doubts of the people in this chapter.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage addresses and refutes a potential objection: that the apparent directional texts in scripture can be interpreted metaphorically (ta'wil). The author responds by arguing that if the true religion were divine transcendence (tanzih), God would have explicitly and repeatedly stated that in scripture to establish it clearly. Since no such clear statement occurs, it follows that the literal meaning of the texts is the intended meaning. The prophets affirmed direction; any claim to the contrary contradicts their message. This is presented as a decisive refutation of the metaphorical interpretation approach.

Key Terms
تأويل (ta'wil)
تنزيه (tanzih)
صريح اللفظ (sarih al-lafz)
شبهات (shubuhat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author rejects all forms of ta'wil (metaphorical interpretation) completely.
Correction: The author only rejects ta'wil when it is used to dismiss the apparent meaning of clear, consistent scriptural passages. He does not necessarily reject ta'wil in other contexts.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that directional verses can be interpreted metaphorically?
A) He says metaphorical interpretation is never allowed.
B) He argues that if tanzih were true, it would be clearly stated in scripture, which it is not.
C) He says the prophets never used metaphor.
D) He claims the Qur'an forbids interpretation.

Key Takeaway
The argument against ta'wil rests on the absence of explicit tanzih texts in scripture.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What would it cost you to accept God's words as they are, without adding your own 'but'? This week, choose one such verse and commit to reading it literally, asking Allah to guide you to the truth He intended.
?
The author argues that if tanzih were the truth, scripture would have explicitly stated it. Now examine your own beliefs: are there convictions you hold about God that have no clear scriptural basis, but which you defend through reinterpretation? Will you have the courage to let go of a cherished interpretation when faced with the silence of revelation? Eighth Doubt: Does Vision of Allah Imply Direction?
Argument linking vision of Allah to direction using condition of facing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the eighth doubt and how it creates a dilemma between affirming direction (Mushabbiha) and denying vision (Mu'tazila).

Core Idea
If seeing Allah is possible, He must be in a direction—or is there another way?

The eighth doubt presents a logical argument that seems to force a stark choice: either accept that Allah is in a direction (as the Mushabbiha do) or deny that Allah can be seen at all (as the Mu'tazila do). This passage does not resolve the dilemma but sets it up for critical examination. In this lesson, we will unpack the premises and see why this argument is considered a shubha that requires careful response.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the flow from premise to condition to two opposing conclusions.
The passage is dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the logical chain
Break down the argument: (1) It is established that seeing Allah is permissible. (2) Vision in this world requires facing the object. (3) Therefore, if Allah can be seen, He must be in a direction so that facing is possible.
The premise about facing is a general observation about vision.
3
Analyze the two conclusions
The Mushabbiha accept the conclusion that Allah has direction. The Mu'tazila reject direction, so they reject the permissibility of ru'ya. Reflect on why both conclusions are problematic.
The author presents this as a doubt, not a definitive proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثامنة : ثبت أنه يجوز رؤية الله تعالي ، والرؤية ممتنعة إلا إذا كان الرائي مقابلا للمرئي ، أو في حكم المقابل له ، وذلك يقتضي كون الإله تعالى في الجهة . والحاصل أن جواز الرؤية مشروط بالمقابلة . ثم قالت المشبهة : ثبت جواز الرؤية فوجب القول بكونه تعالى في الجهة . وقالت المعتزلة : يمتنع كونه تعالى في الجهة ، فوجب أن تمتنع رؤيته [ والله أعلم بالصواب ]
The eighth doubt: It is established that it is permissible to see Allah Almighty, and vision is impossible unless the seer is facing the seen, or in the ruling of one facing it. That necessitates that Allah Almighty is in a direction. The conclusion is that the permissibility of vision is conditioned by facing. Then the Mushabbiha said: The permissibility of vision is established, so it is necessary to say that He—exalted is He—is in a direction. And the Mu'tazila said: It is impossible for Him—exalted is He—to be in a direction, so it is necessary that His vision be impossible. [And Allah knows best what is correct.]
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth doubt (shubha) raised by the anthropomorphists (Mushabbiha). It begins with the premise that seeing Allah (ru'ya) is permissible, which is a point of agreement among many Muslims. Then it notes a condition for vision in this world: the seer must face the seen object. If Allah can be seen, then He must be in a direction so that facing is possible. The Mushabbiha conclude: since ru'ya is permissible, direction is necessary. The Mu'tazila, who deny both direction and bodily vision, logically conclude that ru'ya must be impossible. The author presents this as a dilemma without explicitly endorsing either side, ending with 'Allah knows best.'

Key Terms
رؤية (ru'ya)
مقابلة (muqabala)
جهة (jiha)
مشبهة (Mushabbiha)
معتزلة (Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author endorses the Mushabbiha conclusion that Allah has direction.
Correction: The author presents this as a doubt (shubha) to be examined, not as a definitive proof. The concluding phrase 'Allah knows best' indicates the matter is not settled.

Checkpoint
What is the central claim of the eighth doubt (shubha)?
A) That Allah cannot be seen because He has no direction.
B) That if Allah can be seen, He must be in a direction, so those who affirm ru'ya must affirm direction.
C) That the Mu'tazila deny ru'ya because they deny direction.
D) That vision of Allah is impossible for any being.

Key Takeaway
The eighth doubt creates a logical dilemma: either affirm direction (Mushabbiha) or deny vision of Allah (Mu'tazila), leaving the theologian to find a way out.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Eighth Doubt: Does Seeing God Imply Direction?
The eighth doubt links vision to direction, with two opposing conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eighth doubt against the permissibility of seeing God, and the positions of the Musabbiha and Mu'tazila on direction.

Core Idea
The eighth doubt ties vision to direction, creating a dilemma for theologians.

This passage presents a key theological challenge: if God can be seen, then facing seems necessary, which implies God is in a direction. The author then records how two schools, the Musabbiha and Mu'tazila, draw opposite conclusions from the same premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the logical flow: from the permissibility of vision to the requirement of facing, then to the necessity of direction.
Pay attention to how each group responds.
2
Identify the premise and condition
The passage assumes that vision of God is possible (premise) and that vision requires facing (condition). Understand these two key ideas.
3
Compare the two responses
The Musabbiha accept the implication (direction) and affirm it. The Mu'tazila reject direction and therefore reject the vision of God. Notice how they prioritize different principles.
This highlights a deep theological disagreement.
4
Reflect on the dilemma
Ask yourself: Could both premises be false? Is there a way to reconcile vision without direction? This doubt is a starting point for deeper inquiry.
The author leaves the issue unresolved, deferring to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثامنة: ثبت أنه يجوز رؤية الله تعالى، والرؤية ممتنعة إلا إذا كان الرائي مقابلا للمرئي، أو في حكم المقابل له، وذلك يقتضي كون الإله تعالى في الجهة. والحاصل أن جواز الرؤية مشروط بالمقابلة. ثم قالت المشبهة: ثبت جواز الرؤية فوجب القول بكونه تعالى في الجهة. وقالت المعتزلة: يمتنع كونه تعالى في الجهة، فوجب أن تمتنع رؤيته. والله أعلم بالصواب.
The eighth doubt: It is established that it is permissible to see God, exalted, and vision is impossible unless the seer is facing the seen object, or in the ruling of facing it, and that necessitates that God, exalted, be in a direction. The conclusion is that the permissibility of vision is conditional upon facing. Then the Musabbiha (anthropomorphists) said: the permissibility of vision is established, so it is necessary to say that He, exalted, is in a direction. And the Mu'tazila said: it is impossible for Him, exalted, to be in a direction, so it is necessary that His vision be impossible. And God knows best the truth.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth doubt (shubha). It starts with a premise: it is established that it is permissible to see God. Then it adds a principle: vision requires facing (muqabala). From this, it concludes that God must be in a direction (jiha). The argument then notes the disagreement: the Musabbiha (anthropomorphists) use this to affirm direction, while the Mu'tazila reject direction and therefore reject the possibility of seeing God. The author ends by deferring to God's knowledge.

Key Terms
الرؤية
المقابلة
الجهة
المشبهة
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This doubt proves that God is in a direction.
Correction: The passage only presents the doubt as a disputed issue; it does not affirm the conclusion. The author merely reports the two responses and leaves the matter to Allah.

Checkpoint

A) Because vision requires facing.
B) Because the Quran says so.
C) Because God is everywhere.
D) Because of the impossibility of vision.

Key Takeaway
The eighth doubt highlights the tension between affirming vision and affirming direction, leading to divergent theological positions.

Refuting the Fourth Objection: Infinite Regress
Refutation of the fourth objection via infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the fourth objection by demonstrating that it leads to an impossible infinite regress.

Core Idea
If inherence meant spatial co-location, every attribute would need a prior attribute, leading to an endless chain.

The fourth objection claims that an attribute inheres in its subject by being in the same physical location. The author counters by showing that this interpretation creates a logical problem: the subject's own location is itself an attribute, so the argument would require an infinite series of attributes. This lesson unpacks that devastating refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical structure: premise, objection, then refutation.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on the terms 'حيز' (location), 'قائمة' (subsisting), and 'تسلسل' (infinite regress). Understand how they connect.
3
Trace the regress argument
Follow the conditional reasoning: if X, then Y leads to Z (impossible). See why the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة : إنها مبنية على أن معنى حصول الصفة في الموصوف : حصول تلك الصفة في ذلك الحيز، تبعا لحصول تلك الذات فيه وهذا باطل. لأن حصول تلك الذات في هذا الحيز صفة لتلك الذات، قائمة بها، فلو كان معنى حصول الصفة ما ذكرتم، لوجب أن يكون حصوله في الحيز مسبوقا بحصول آخر في ذلك الحيز، وذلك يوجب التسلسل وهو محال
And the answer to the fourth objection: it is based on the meaning that the occurrence of the attribute in the described subject is the occurrence of that attribute in that location, following the occurrence of that essence in it. And this is false. Because the occurrence of that essence in this location is an attribute of that essence, subsisting in it. So if the meaning of the occurrence of the attribute were what you mentioned, then its occurrence in the location would have to be preceded by another occurrence in that location, and that would necessitate an infinite regress, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the fourth objection. The objection is based on a specific understanding of inherence: that an attribute (sifah) inheres in a subject (mawfuf) by being in the same spatial location (hayyiz) as the subject. The author argues that this is false because the subject itself being in that location is itself an attribute of the subject. So if the attribute's inherence means it also must be in that location, then for the attribute to be in that location, it would require the subject's previous occurrence in that location, but that previous occurrence is itself an attribute, and so on, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). Since infinite regress is impossible (muhal), the objection's premise is invalid.

Key Terms
صفة
موصوف
حيز
قائمة
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An attribute inheres in its subject by being in the same physical location.
Correction: The author shows that this would lead to an infinite regress, so the premise is false.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would happen if an attribute inhered in its subject by being in the same location?
A) The attribute would be separate from the subject.
B) It would require an infinite regress of occurrences.
C) The subject would cease to exist.
D) The attribute would become identical with the subject.

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection fails because it would require an infinite chain of attributes, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In your own life, do you ever try to explain your actions or qualities by tracing them back endlessly (e.g., 'I am this way because of my past, which was caused by...') instead of resting in Allah as the First Cause? How can you apply this logical limit to stop that infinite regression and submit to Allah's ultimate causality?

Why Attribute Inherence Does Not Require Spatial Co-Location
The author refutes the fourth objection regarding attribute inherence and spatial location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that an attribute's inherence in its subject implies spatial co-location leads to an infinite regress and is therefore invalid.

Core Idea
Attribute inherence is not spatial co-location; otherwise, it would lead to an infinite regress of locations.

The author now addresses a fourth objection that misunderstands how attributes exist in their subjects. The objector assumes that for an attribute to be 'in' the subject, it must occupy the same physical space. But this leads to an absurd consequence. Let's read the passage and see the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of the argument.
2
Identify the objection and its assumption
The objection assumes that attribute inherence means spatial co-location. Find the phrase that states this assumption.
3
Follow the argument to its conclusion
The author shows that this assumption leads to an infinite regress because the subject's own location is an attribute, requiring another location, and so on.
Trace the logic: if attribute = spatial, then subject's location is attribute, so that attribute needs prior location, ad infinitum.
4
Reflect on the refutation
Ask yourself: Why does the author consider infinite regress impossible? How does this refute the objection?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة : إنها مبنية على أن معنى حصول الصفة في الموصوف : حصول تلك الصفة في ذلك الحيز ، تبعا لحصول تلك الذات فيه وهذا باطل . لأن حصول تلك الذات في هذا الحيز صفة لتلك الذات ، قائمة بها ، فلو كان معنى حصول الصفة ما ذكرتم ، لوجب أن يكون حصوله في الحيز مسبوقا بحصول آخر في ذلك الحيز ، وذلك يوجب التسلسل وهو محال
The answer to the fourth objection: it is based on the idea that the meaning of an attribute's inherence in the subject is the inherence of that attribute in that spatial location, following the subject's inherence in it. This is false. Because the subject's inherence in this location is an attribute of that subject, subsisting in it. So if the meaning of attribute inherence were as you mentioned, then its inherence in the location would have to be preceded by another inherence in that location, necessitating infinite regress, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage answers the fourth objection by refuting the notion that an attribute's existence in its subject implies spatial co-location. The author argues that this leads to infinite regress because the subject's own location is an attribute of it, so the attribute's location would require another prior location, etc.

Correction: Attribute inherence is a metaphysical relation, not spatial co-location. Accepting spatial co-location leads to an infinite regress, as the author shows.

The Second Objection: The Shared Concept as Determinant
Objection: shared concept as determinant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to the argument that only contingency can be the determinant, and why the objector proposes the shared concept between substance and accident as an alternative.

Core Idea
The objector asks: why not the common feature of substance and accident?

This lesson presents the second objection raised against the position that only contingency determines the division into adjacent and separate. The objector argues that since substance and accident share a common feature that distinguishes them from God, that very shared concept could serve as the determinant. Understanding this objection helps clarify the terms of the debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objector is questioning an assumption.
The passage is a single rhetorical question.
2
Identify the key elements

3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this objection matters: it challenges the claim that only contingency can be the determinant.
The author does not answer here, so think about what possible flaw might exist.
4
Connect to the broader argument

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إنه لا شك أن الجوهر والعرض يخالفان ذات الله تعالى ، والأمر الذي به يخالفان ذات الله وصفاته ، مفهوم مشترك بينهما ، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون المقتضى لقبول القسمة إلى المجانب وإلى المباين ، هو ذلك المفهوم المشترك ؟

The feature that makes them different is a common concept shared between them. So why cannot that common concept be the determinant for accepting the division (into adjacent and separate)? The author does not give an answer within this passage; it is left as a question.

Seventh Objection: Existence as Cause Applied to God
Seventh objection: if existence is cause, it applies to God; response denies premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logic of the seventh objection and why the author denies it by distinguishing between necessary knowledge (darura) and inferential reasoning (nazar).

Core Idea
The objection assumes God's existence is like ours; the author says no.

In this passage, we encounter a debate about whether existence as a cause would apply to God. The objector tries to force a conclusion, but the author counters by noting a key assumption about God's existence and then invoking a distinction between what is known directly and what is inferred. Let's unpack the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow: the objector concedes that existence is the cause, then challenges why it doesn't apply to God.
Notice the conditional structure: 'This required conclusion only holds if...'
2
Identify the hidden premise
The objector assumes that God's existence is equivalent to the existence of contingent things. That is, God's existence is an attribute inhering in an essence.
Most Karramiyya do not hold this view.
3
Examine the author's response
The author denies the objection outright and provides a different epistemological basis: the effect is known by necessity (al-darura), while the inference is known by reflection (al-nazar).
This shifts the debate from a logical inference to direct knowledge.
4
Connect to kalam methodology
This passage illustrates a key method in kalam: argumentation often hinges on the nature of God's attributes and the distinction between necessary and inferred knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال السابع : سلمنا أن المؤثر في هذا الحكم [ ليس(۱)] هو الحدوث. وأنه هو الوجود. لكن لم قلتم : يلزم حصول هذا الحكم في حق الله تعالى ؟ وبيانه : أن هذا المطلوب إنما يلزم لو كان وجود الله تعالى مساويا لوجود الممكنات . وهذا إنما يصح إن قلنا : إن وجود الله تعالى صفة قائمة بماهيته والكرامية لا يقول أكثرهم بذلك . قلنا : فهذا ممنوع ، فإنا بينا في كثير من الأشياء : أن الأثر معلوم بالضرورة ، والاستدلال معلوم بالنظر
The seventh question: We grant that the effective cause (al-mu'aththir) in this judgment is not temporality (al-huduth). Rather it is existence (al-wujud). But why do you say that this judgment necessarily applies to God? Its clarification: This required conclusion only holds if the existence of God is equivalent to the existence of contingent things. This is only valid if we say that the existence of God is an attribute inhering in His essence, and most of the Karramiyya do not say that. We say: This is denied, for we have shown in many things that the effect is known by necessity (al-darura), while the inference is known by reflection (al-nazar).
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh objection in a kalam debate. The objector concedes that the effective cause for the judgment (presumably the judgment that something can be either adjacent to or distinct from another) is not temporality but existence. However, they argue that if existence is the cause, then it should apply to God as well, because God exists. The response first notes that this would only follow if God's existence were like the existence of contingent things—i.e., a quality inhering in an essence. The objector reminds that most Karramiyya do not hold that God's existence is an inhering attribute. The author then rejects the objection outright, stating that the effect (the judgment) is known by necessity, while the reasoning (inference) is known by reflection. This implies that the causal link the objector tries to establish is not necessary; the matter is settled by direct intuition, not by a chain of reasoning that can be questioned.

Key Terms
المؤثر (al-mu'aththir)
الحدوث (al-huduth)
الوجود (al-wujud)
الضرورة (al-darura)
النظر (al-nazar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is a definitive proof that existence as cause leads to absurdity if applied to God.
Correction: The objection only works if one assumes God's existence is like contingent existence, which is not granted by the Karramiyya. The author further denies the logic by invoking necessary knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the seventh objection, why would the judgment necessarily apply to God if existence is the cause?
A) Because God is a contingent being.
B) Because God's existence is equivalent to the existence of contingent things.
C) Because temporality is the cause.
D) Because the Karramiyya affirm that God's existence is an attribute inhering in His essence.

Key Takeaway
The author distinguishes between necessary knowledge (darura) and inferential reasoning (nazar) to block the objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever subconsciously assume that God's nature is like yours—limited by time, space, or human categories? Where in your daily life do you impose your own limitations on your understanding of God? How will you actively remind yourself that His existence is fundamentally different?
?
The author says the effect is known by necessity (darura), not by inference (nazar). In your own faith, do you rely more on intellectual proofs or on a direct, undeniable awareness of God's truths? Are you willing to trust that some things about God are known without argument? What would change in your worship if you truly believed that?
?
When you face doubts or objections about God, do you immediately seek a logical argument, or do you first check your assumptions about who God is? This passage warns against equating God with His creation. What false equivalences have you made without realizing it? Will you commit to examining your assumptions before demanding proofs?

Eighth Objection: Can a Cause Be Present Without Its Effect?
Eighth objection against the necessity of effect from cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that a cause may be present yet its effect fails, and how this applies to debates about divine attributes.

Core Idea
A cause does not guarantee its effect if the subject is unreceptive or a condition is missing.

This lesson presents the eighth objection, which challenges the assumption that a cause must always produce its effect. By examining the nature of life in God and creatures, the objector argues that shared attributes do not force shared consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the objection's logic sink in.
The Arabic uses technical terms: al-muqtada, al-mahall, al-shart.
2
Identify the argument structure
Find the key claim: a cause can be present without its effect. Notice the two reasons given: the locus is not receptive, or a condition is absent.
The objection gives an example from life to illustrate this.
3
Analyze the analogy
Consider how being alive makes a creature receptive to pain, pleasure, death, etc., yet God is alive but none of these apply. Reflect on why the objector thinks this supports the main argument.
This analogy is central to the objection's persuasive force.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثامن : إنه قد يحصل المقتضى ، ولكن يتخلف عنه الحكم . إما لأن المحل غير قابل ، وإما لأن الشرط فائت، ألا ترى أن كون الحي حيـا يصحح قبول الشهوة والنفرة والألم واللذة والجهل والموت . ثم إنه تعالى حي ، مع أن شيئا من هذه الأحكام لا يصح عليه ، إما لأن ذاته تعالى غير قابلة لهذه الأحكام ، أو لأن قبول هذه الأحكام موقوف على شرط ، يمتنع حصوله في حق الله تعالى . فلم قلتم : لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر ههنا كذلك ؟ .
The eighth question: It may happen that the prerequisite (al-muqtada) is present, yet the judgment lags behind it—either because the locus (al-mahall) is not receptive, or because the condition is absent. Do you not see that the fact that a living thing is alive makes it receptive to desire, aversion, pain, pleasure, ignorance, and death? Yet God is alive, while none of these judgments apply to Him—either because His essence is not receptive to these judgments, or because the acceptance of these judgments is dependent on a condition that cannot be fulfilled in God's case. So why do you say that it is not permissible for the matter to be similar here?
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth objection as a standalone question. The objector notes that a cause (prerequisite) can be present without the expected judgment taking effect. Two reasons are given: either the subject itself is not receptive, or a necessary condition is missing. An analogy is given: being alive makes a being capable of experiences like desire, pain, death, etc., but God is alive and yet none of these apply to Him. This shows that a shared attribute (life) does not force all its typical consequents. Similarly, if existence is the cause for being subject to division into adjacent or distinct, it does not force that God must be so divisible, because His essence may be non-receptive or a condition may be absent. The objection ends with a challenge: why do you deny that this could be the case here? Note: The author's direct response is not present in this window.

Key Terms
المقتضى (al-muqtada)
المحل (al-mahall)
الشرط (al-shart)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that if a cause is universal (like existence), its effect must also be universal and unavoidable.
Correction: The objection shows that a cause may be present without its effect if the subject (locus) is not receptive or a necessary condition is missing. Thus, universality of the cause does not guarantee universality of the effect.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the eighth objection use to illustrate that a cause may not produce its usual effect?
A) Fire burning wood.
B) Life making one receptive to pain and death, yet God does not experience them.
C) Existence making things divisible.
D) Temporality causing change.

Key Takeaway
The eighth objection uses the example of life to argue that a cause (like existence) may not force its typical effect when the subject is non-receptive or a condition is absent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God as 'alive', do you unconsciously associate it with life as you experience it—desire, pain, change? How does this lesson force you to purify your conception of God?
?
If a cause (like existence) can be present without its effect when the subject is non-receptive, what does that say about your own attempts to limit God by human analogies? Are there areas in your belief where you assume something must be true about God because it's true for creatures?
?
The objector uses the example of life to challenge a theological argument. How often do you examine your own beliefs for hidden assumptions that project creaturely limitations onto God? What concrete step can you take today to correct such an assumption?

Ninth Objection: Existence as Cause and the Hindrance of Particular Essence
Objection and response on existence as cause of division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that existence as cause would force impossible divisions in substances and accidents is answered by appealing to the particularity of essence as a preventing factor.

Core Idea
Existence may cause possibility, but particular essence can block the effect.

This passage presents the ninth objection in a theological debate about the relationship between existence and the possibility of division. The objector argues that if existence is the cause for being divisible into adjacent or distinct, then all existing things would have to be divisible in that way, leading to absurdities. The response, however, introduces the concept of a 'preventive factor' (mani')—the particularity of essence—showing that existence does not necessitate universal application of its effects.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objector's argument: if existence is the cause for division into adjacent (mujanib) and distinct (mubayin), then since existence is equally present in all existents, substances and accidents would have to be divisible in that way.
The key term is 'muqtada' (prerequisite/cause).
2
Identify the reductio
Notice that the objector claims division of substance and accident into those two categories is known to be impossible. This is the absurd consequence meant to refute the premise.
Think about why a substance cannot be both adjacent and distinct to itself in direction.
3
See the defender's reply
The defender says that the possibility holds in view of existence, but actual division is prevented by the particularity of essence (hususiyyat al-mahiyya). This is the core response.
The term 'mani'' (preventive factor) is crucial.
4
Examine the counter-reply
The objector turns this admission against the defender: if particular essence can block the effect, then existence is not a sufficient cause, so the original position is weakened.
This shows the dialectical back-and-forth.
5
Reflect on the nuance
Consider how this argument relates to divine attributes: God's particular essence prevents Him from being subject to division, even though He exists.
This is the broader theological application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال التاسع : [ إن ما ذكرتم إن دل على أن المقتضى لقبـول هـذه القسمة هو الوجود فههنا ما يدل على أنه ليس الأمر كذلك ، فإنه لو كان (٢) ] المقتضى لقبول الانقسام إلى المجانب وإلى المباين هو الوجود . ثم إن الوجود [ حاصل بالسوية . فوجب أن ينقسم الجوهر ، إلى ما يكون مجانبا ، وإلى ما يكون (۱)] مباينا بالجهة . ومعلوم أن انقسام الجوهر إلى هذين القسمين: محال . ولزم أيضاً في العرض أن ينقسم إلى هذين القسمين . ومعلوم أنه محال . فإن قالوا : إن كل جوهر وعرض ، فإنه يصح أن يكون منقسما إلى هذين القسمين، نظرا إلى كونه موجودا. وإنما يمتنع ذلك الإنقسام ، نظرا إلى مانع منع منه ، وهو خصوصية ماهيته. فنقول : فهذا اعتراف بأنه لا يلزم من كون الوجود علة لصحة أمر من الأمور ، أن يصح ذلك الحكم على كل ما كان موصوفا بالوجود ، لاحتمال أن تكون خصوصية تلك الماهية ، ماتعة من ذلك الحكم
The ninth question: [If what you mentioned indicates that the prerequisite for accepting this division is existence, then here is what indicates that it is not so. For if it were (٢)] the prerequisite for accepting division into adjacent and distinct is existence. Then existence [is present equally. So it would be necessary that substance be divided into what is adjacent and what is (۱)] distinct in direction. And it is known that the division of substance into these two categories is impossible. And it also follows that accident would be divided into these two categories. And that is known to be impossible. If they say: Every substance and accident can be divided into these two categories in view of its being existent, and the only reason that division is prevented is a preventive factor, namely the particularity of its essence. Then we say: This is an admission that from existence being the cause of the validity of a thing, it does not follow that that judgment applies to everything described by existence, because it is possible that the particularity of that essence prevents that judgment.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage contains the ninth objection and its response. The objector constructs a reductio: if existence is the cause for the possibility of being divided into adjacent (mujanib) and distinct (mubayin) in direction, then since existence is equally present in substances and accidents, they would all have to be divisible in that way. But it is known that substances and accidents are not so divisible (e.g., a substance cannot be both adjacent and distinct to itself). Therefore the premise is false. The defender responds that the objector has misunderstood: the possibility of such division holds only in view of existence, but it may be prevented by a 'preventive factor' (mani') which is the particularity of the essence (hususiyya al-mahiyya). For example, a substance may be prevented from actually being divided into adjacent and distinct because its essence has a specific nature that blocks it. The objector then replies (via the author's 'فنقول'): this very admission proves that existence being a cause does not force the judgment to apply to everything with existence, because the particular essence can hinder it. Thus the objection is turned back: the defender's own move shows that God's particular essence can prevent Him from being subject to division, without denying that existence is the cause in general.

Key Terms
المقتضى (al-muqtada)
الانقسام (al-inqisam)
المجانب (al-mujanib)
المباين (al-mubayin)
الجوهر (al-jawhar)
العرض (al-'arad)
خصوصية الماهية (hususiyyat al-mahiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is a decisive refutation showing that existence cannot be a cause for division.
Correction: The response shows that existence can be a cause for possibility, but particular essence can prevent the effect, so the objection does not disprove the causal role of existence.

Checkpoint
According to the ninth objection, why would substance and accident have to be divisible if existence is the cause?
A) Because existence is present equally in all things.
B) Because temporality is the cause.
C) Because the particularity of essence prevents division.
D) Because substance and accident are the same.

Key Takeaway
A general cause (existence) may grant a possibility, but the particular nature of an essence can prevent the effect from occurring, a key nuance in kalam reasoning about divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, what 'existence' (blessing, opportunity, truth) is equally available to all, but your particular essence—your habits, biases, or attachments—prevents it from having its full effect? What specific hindrance are you allowing to block the blessing?
?
When you encounter a universal truth that seems to apply to everyone, do you ever use the particularities of your situation (your 'particular essence') as an excuse to avoid acting on it? Recall a recent example and commit to removing that hindrance.
?
If existence alone is not sufficient to cause change, but requires the removal of hindrances, then what 'hindrances' in your heart are preventing you from being truly transformed by the truths you already know? What must you strip away to let the truth do its work?

The Void as Infinite Deity
Enumerates the first attribute: the void's infinity and its proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the void must be infinite in order to be the deity.

Core Idea
The void's infinity is argued by denying the possibility of finite boundaries.

The first attribute claimed for the void is infinity. The proponents argue that if the void were finite, it would lead to an absurdity, thus it must be infinite. Let's examine their reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument for infinity
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the author sets up the premise that the deity must be infinite.
The Arabic phrase 'غير متناهي' means infinite.
2
Understand the two options for a finite void
The argument presents two possibilities if the void were finite: either it has a distinct side outside it, or it does not. Follow the reasoning for each case.
This is a classic disjunctive argument.
3
See why both options are rejected
If a side exists outside, it leads to a contradiction. If no side is distinguished, reason deems it impossible. Therefore the void cannot be finite.
The contradiction arises from something existing while being supposed known in a certain way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قالوا: إن جميع الصفات اللائقة بالإلمية، حاصل فيه. فإحداهما : قولنا : الإله يجب أن يكون غير متناهي، وهذا الفضاء كذلك ، لأنه لو كان متناهيا، لكان إما أن يتميز جانب عن جانب في الخارج منه ، وإما أن لا يتميز ، فإن حصل ذلك الامتياز فهو موجود حال ما فرض معلوما. هذا خلف . وإن لم يحصل ذلك الامتياز ، فقد ذكرنا : أن بديهة العقل تحكم بامتناع ذلك. وثانيها : أن من صفات الإله تعالى أنه مع كـونـه غـير متناهي ، يكون غير قابل للفصل والوصل ، والاجتماع والافتراق . وهذا القضاء كذلك . لأنه مع كونه غير متناهي لا يقبل التفرق والتمزق ، والفصل والوصل ، بل هو باقي أبد الآباد على حالة واحدة. وثالثها : أنه جاء في صفة الله تعالى كونه قريبا من عباده ، كما في جاء في الكتاب الإلهي من قوله : ( وهو معكم أينما كنتم (۳) ) ومن قوله : ( ونحن أقرب إليه من حبل الوريد (٣) والقول : بأن إله العالم هو هذا الفضاء يحقق هذا الكلام .
Then they said: all the attributes befitting divinity are found in it. One of them is our statement: the deity must be infinite, and this void is likewise, because if it were finite, then either a side would be distinguished from a side outside it, or not distinguished. If that distinction occurs, then it exists while supposed known—this is contradictory. And if that distinction does not occur, we have mentioned that primary rational intuition rules its impossibility. The second: among the attributes of God Most High is that while being infinite, He is not subject to separation and union, combination and division. And this void is likewise, because while being infinite, it does not accept separation and tearing, division and union, rather it remains forever in a single state. The third: it came in the description of God Most High that He is close to His servants, as came in the divine Book from His saying: 'And He is with you wherever you are' (Quran 57:4) and His saying: 'And We are closer to him than the jugular vein' (Quran 50:16). And the statement that the god of the world is this void realizes this speech.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage lists three divine attributes that the void is claimed to possess. The first attribute is infinity: the void must be infinite, because if it were finite, it would either have a side beyond it (implying something outside, hence it exists) or not have a distinguished side (which reason deems impossible). The second attribute is indivisibility and immutability: despite its infinity, the void cannot be divided or changed, remaining perpetually in one state. The third attribute is closeness to servants: the proponents of the doctrine argue that identifying God with the void explains the Quranic verses about God's closeness ('He is with you', 'We are closer'), since the void fills all space and thus is always present with everything.

Key Terms
غير متناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The void's infinity is like a mathematical continuum that can be divided into parts.
Correction: The argument assumes the void is a real extended thing; its infinity is about having no boundary, not about divisibility.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, if the void were finite and a side were distinguished outside it, what would be the result?
A) The void would be infinite.
B) It would exist while supposed known, a contradiction.
C) It would be divisible into parts.
D) Reason would accept it as possible.

Key Takeaway
The proponents argue that the void must be infinite because finitude leads to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read an argument that the void must be infinite, just like God. Does this comparison trouble you? Why or why not? Sit with that discomfort and ask yourself: what is the real difference between the void's infinity and God's infinity in your heart?
?
This passage presents a logical proof from a non-believer's perspective. Have you ever examined your own beliefs with such rigorous reasoning? If not, what is holding you back from applying this level of scrutiny to your faith?

The Void as Indivisible and Immutable
Enumerates the second attribute: the void's indivisibility and immutability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the void is claimed to be indivisible and unchanging like the deity.

Core Idea
The void's infinity prevents it from being divided or changed.

The second attribute of the void is that it is indivisible and immutable. Since the void is infinite, it cannot be separated into parts or changed over time. Let's see how the proponents connect this to the divine nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the second attribute passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second attribute. Notice the emphasis on 'not subject to separation and union'.
The term 'الفصل والوصل' means separation and union.
2
Identify the divine attribute
The passage states that God is infinite and yet not divisible. This is a key attribute of the deity.
3
Apply to the void
The void, being infinite, also cannot be divided or changed. It remains in a single state forever.
The void is said to be 'باقي أبد الآباد' (remaining forever).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
According to the second attribute, what characteristic does the void have despite being infinite?
A) It is divisible into parts.
B) It is subject to change over time.
C) It is not subject to separation or union.
D) It has distinct boundaries.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The void remains forever unchanged because it is infinite. You, however, are finite and ever-changing. What finite thing in your life have you treated as though it were infinite, and how has that let you down? Will you today transfer your trust to the truly Infinite One?
?
If infinity prevents division and change, what does that say about the One who is truly infinite? When your faith feels torn by doubts or worldly distractions, are you looking to the infinite God or to something finite? What one attachment do you need to 'cut off' to align with His indivisible unity?
?
The author argues that the void's nature makes it impervious to tearing. Your own soul is finite and vulnerable. Are you clinging to the unchanging void (a created thing) or to the Creator who alone is immutable? What would change in your life if you truly believed God is the only permanent reality?

The Void's Closeness to Servants
Enumerates the third attribute: the void's closeness to servants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how proponents use Quranic verses to argue that the void is close to all, supporting its divinity.

Core Idea
The void fills all space, making it always present with everything.

The third attribute is that the void is close to all beings, just as God is described in the Quran. The proponents cite verses about God's omnipresence to claim that the void, being everywhere, fulfills this divine quality. Let's explore this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the third attribute passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the third attribute. Note the Quranic verses quoted.
The verses are Quran 57:4 and 50:16.
2
Understand the Quranic claim
The Quran states that God is with His servants wherever they are, and closer than the jugular vein.
3
See how the void fits
If the void is the deity, then its spatial omnipresence explains these verses literally.
This completes their case for the void as God.

The Claim That Space Is the Necessary Existent and Its Four Attributes
Presentation of the argument that al-fada' (space) is God, with four attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the infinite void (al-fada') is the necessary existent and how its proponents assign four attributes of divinity to it.

Core Idea
The claim that space is God rests on four attributes: infinity, non-divisibility, nearness, and self-subsistence.

In this passage from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah, the author presents the arguments of those who identify the infinite void with the necessary existent. This is a narration of their claims, not the author's own position. The passage enumerates four attributes that they argue fit both God and space. Understanding this helps us grasp one of the historical challenges to orthodox theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the style: it begins with a premise, then lists four attributes with supporting arguments.
Pay attention to the structure: the passage ends with 'hikaya shubuhat' indicating it is a narration of doubts.
2
Identify the central claim and four attributes
Find the statement that space is wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi, and then list the four attributes: infinity, non-divisibility, nearness, independence.
Each attribute is introduced with 'wa-' (and) or numbers in the text.
3
Understand the objection and response
Read the objection about filth and the response. Note the analogy with God's existence with events in time.
The response argues that space does not change due to its contents, just as God does not change due to temporal events.
4
Reflect on the purpose of this passage
The passage is a presentation of opposing views (hikaya shubuhat). The author will likely refute them later. Consider why the author includes such detail.
This is a common method in kalam: present the strongest version of the opponent's argument before refuting it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفضاء واجب الوجود لذاته، ويثبت : أن ما سواء من الذوات والصفات مفتقر إليه ويثبت : أنه غني عن كل ما سواه ولا معنى للإله الواجب الوجود لذاته ، إلا ذلك . فيثبت : أن إله العالم ليس إلا هذا الفضاء الذي لا نهاية له. ثم قالوا: إن جميع الصفات اللائقة بالإلمية، حاصل فيه. فإحداهما : قولنا : الإله يجب أن يكون غير متناهي، وهذا الفضاء كذلك ، لأنه لو كان متناهيا، لكان إما أن يتميز جانب عن جانب في الخارج منه ، وإما أن لا يتميز ، فإن حصل ذلك الامتياز فهو موجود حال ما فرض معلوما. هذا خلف . وإن لم يحصل ذلك الامتياز ، فقد ذكرنا : أن بديهة العقل تحكم بامتناع ذلك. وثانيها : أن من صفات الإله تعالى أنه مع كونه غير متناهي ، يكون غير قابل للفصل والوصل ، والاجتماع والافتراق . وهذا القضاء كذلك . لأنه مع كونه غير متناهي لا يقبل التفرق والتمزق ، والفصل والوصل ، بل هو باقي أبد الآباد على حالة واحدة. وثالثها : أنه جاء في صفة الله تعالى كونه قريبا من عباده ، كما جاء في الكتاب الإلهي من قوله : ( وهو معكم أينما كنتم ) ومن قوله : ( ونحن أقرب إليه من حبل الوريد ) والقول : بأن إله العالم هو هذا الفضاء يحقق هذا الكلام . وأما قول من يقول : إنه على هذا التقدير تكون ذاته مخالطة للقاذورات . فهو كلام ضعيف . لأن هذا إنما يصير إذا تأثر ذلك الشيء بمخالطة غيره والفضاء لا تتغير ذاته ولا حقيقته بحسب ما يحصل فيه البتة ، ألا ترى أنه لا نزاع في أنه تعالى موجود مع كل الحوادث ، ولم يلزم من كونه تعالى مع هذه الحوادث بسبب الوقت والزمان : نقص في ذاته . فكذلك لا يلزم من كونه تعالى معها بحسب الجهة والمكان : حصول نقص فيه . ورابعها : إن على هذا التقدير يكون غنيا عن المكان والحيز ، لأن من المعلوم بالضرورة : أن هذا الفضاء ليس حاصلا في فضاء آخر ، بل هو شيء قائم بنفسه ، غني عن كل ما سواه فهذا حكاية شبهات هؤلاء الأقوام .
Space is the necessary existent in itself, and it establishes that everything else of essences and attributes is dependent on it. And it establishes that it is independent of everything else, and the meaning of 'the god who is the necessary existent in itself' is nothing but that. So it establishes that the god of the world is nothing but this infinite space. Then they said: All the attributes befitting divinity are present in it. The first of them: our statement: the god must be infinite, and this space is likewise, because if it were finite, then it would either be that a side is distinguished from another side externally from it, or it would not be distinguished. If that distinction occurs, then it exists at the moment it is supposed known. That is a contradiction. And if that distinction does not occur, we have mentioned that the intuition of the intellect judges its impossibility. The second: that among the attributes of God Most High is that He, while being infinite, is not susceptible to separation and connection, combination and disjunction. And this space is likewise, because while being infinite it does not accept splitting and tearing, separation and connection; rather it remains forever in one state. The third: that it came in the attribute of God Most High that He is near to His servants, as came in the Divine Book from His statement: 'And He is with you wherever you are' and from His statement: 'And We are nearer to him than the jugular vein.' And the statement that the god of the world is this space realizes this speech. As for the statement of one who says: on this assumption His essence is intermixed with filth, it is weak speech, because this only occurs when that thing is affected by mixing with another, and space's essence and reality do not change at all according to what occurs in it. Do you not see that there is no dispute that He Most High exists with all events, and there follows from His being Most High with these events by virtue of time and moment no deficiency in His essence. So likewise there does not follow from His being Most High with them by virtue of direction and place any deficiency in Him. And the fourth: that on this assumption He is independent of place and locus, because it is known by necessity that this space is not located in another space, but rather it is a thing subsisting by itself, independent of everything else. This is the narration of the doubts of these peoples.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents the arguments of those who claim that the infinite void (al-fada') is the necessary existent and therefore God. It is a presentation, not a refutation. The passage begins by stating the premise that space is wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi (necessary existent in itself). It then claims that everything else is dependent on it, and that it is independent. Hence, the god of the world is nothing but this infinite space. Then it enumerates four attributes of divinity that they claim apply to space: (1) Infinity: the god must be infinite, and space is infinite because if it were finite it would lead to a contradiction either of distinction or no distinction. (2) Non-divisibility: the god is not subject to separation or connection, and space likewise remains in one state forever. (3) Nearness: the Qur'anic verses about God's nearness are supposedly verified if space is God, because space encompasses all. They also address the objection that if space is God, His essence would be mixed with filth. They argue that space does not change due to what occurs in it, just as God being with events in time does not imply a deficiency. (4) Independence from place and locus: space is not in another space; it is self-subsisting. The passage ends by calling this the 'hikaya shubuhat' (narration of doubts) of these peoples, indicating that these are their arguments being presented for refutation.

Key Terms
الفضاء (al-fada': space/void)
واجب الوجود لذاته (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi: necessary existent in itself)
غير متناهي (ghayr mutanahi: infinite)
بديهة العقل (badihah al-'aql: intuition of the intellect)
قابل للفصل والوصل (qabil lil-fasl wa al-wasl: susceptible to separation and connection)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage presents the author's own theological position that space is God.
Correction: The passage is a narration of the arguments of a group who claim space is God. The author is presenting their views as 'hikaya shubuhat' (narration of doubts) for the purpose of refutation.

Checkpoint
What is the main claim of those whose arguments are narrated in this passage?
A) God is material.
B) The infinite void (al-fada') is the necessary existent and therefore God.
C) Space is created by God.
D) The world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
In this passage, the author narrates the argument that infinite space is the necessary existent, supported by four attributes of divinity: infinity, non-divisibility, nearness, and independence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been tempted to define God based solely on your own reasoning, neglecting revelation? How does this passage warn you against relying on unaided intellect in matters of theology?
?
The opponents in this passage used rational arguments that sounded logical. Can you think of a time when you accepted an argument that seemed sound but contradicted what you know to be true from revelation? How does this lesson challenge you to be more careful in your own reasoning?
?
The passage calls these arguments 'shubuhat' (doubts). What assumptions or biases might cause you to be fooled by seemingly rational falsehoods? How will you guard against that in your study of aqeedah?

The Two Meanings of 'Infinite' and God's Transcendence
Sanusi distinguishes two meanings of 'infinite' and applies the negative sense to God's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two distinct meanings of the term 'non-finite' (ghayr mutanahi) and why the correct meaning for God's essence is the negation of quantity and direction.

Core Idea
God's infinity is not about size but about the absence of limits altogether.

When we say God is infinite, what do we really mean? The term 'infinite' can be misleading, as it might suggest endless extension in space. The great scholar al-Sanusi clarifies that there are two distinct meanings: one is pure negation of any limit, and the other is unbounded extension. In this passage, he explains both and shows that for God's essence, only the negation sense applies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without annotating, then read the English translation. Notice how the author structures his explanation – he first mentions three contexts, then defines two meanings, then applies them.
The passage is dense, so reading twice helps.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'infinite'
Look for the definitions of 'negation' (salb) and 'deviation' (ʻudul). Notice the key difference: negation denies any quantity, while deviation affirms quantity but without a final boundary.
The term 'deviation' (ʻudul) is a technical usage meaning the opposite of negation in this context.
3
Apply to God's essence
The author states that when we say God's essence is non-finite, we mean the negation sense. Why? Because God is transcendent beyond quantity, volume, position, and direction. Understand how this protects tanzih (transcendence).
Contrast with the view of those who attribute corporeality to God – they would use the deviation sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل التاسع في تفسير قولنا : إن الله تعالى غير متناهي اعلم أن هذا اللفظ يطلق في حق الله تعالى تارة بحسب ذاته ، وتارة بحسب دوام وجوده ، وتارة بحسب صفاته ، وقبل الخوض في هذه التفاصيل نقول : إن قولنا للشيء : إنه لا نهاية له قد يذكر بمعنى السلب وقد يذكر بمعنى العدول . أما بمعنى السلب فهو أن يكون المراد : أن الماهية القابلة لمعنى الحد والنهاية مسلوبة عنه . وذلك لأن النهاية عبارة عن طرف مقدار الشيء ومقطعه وإذا كان خاليا عن المقدار وجب خلوه عن طرف المقدار ، لأن عند انتفاء المقدار، يستحيل حصول طرف المقدار وأما بمعنى العدول ، فهو أن يكون الشيء له حجمية ومقدار، إلا أنه لا ينتهي مقداره إلى مقطع وحد ، بل كلما عددته أو قدرته ، فإنك تجد خارجا عنه شيئا آخر منه من غير حاجة إلى التكرير . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إذا قلنا : إن ذات الله تعالى غير متناهي فإنا نريد به معنى السلب . نعني : أنه منزه عن المقدار والحجمية والوضع والحيز ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فالمعنى الذي لأجله يصح وصف الشيء بأن له حدا أو طرفا ، مسلوب عن ذات الله تعالى، فكأن ذاته غير متناهية بهذا التفسير ، وهذا عند من يقول إنه تعالى منزه عن المقدار والجسمية والوضع والحيز ، أما عند من يقول بهذه المذاهب ، فإنه نفس كونه تعالى غير متناهي ، إما في جميع الجهات ، أو في بعض الجهات بالتفسير الثاني . وهذا هو الكلام في تفسير كونه تعالى غير متناهي ، بحسب الذات .
Chapter Nine: On the interpretation of our statement that God Almighty is non-finite (ghayr mutanahi). Know that this expression is applied to God Almighty sometimes with respect to His essence, sometimes with respect to the perpetuity of His existence, and sometimes with respect to His attributes. Before going into these details, we say: Our statement about something that it has no end may be mentioned in the sense of negation (salb) and may be mentioned in the sense of deviation (ʻudul). As for the sense of negation, it is that the intended meaning is that the essence (mahiyya) which is receptive to the meaning of limit and end is negated from it. That is because the end (nihaya) is the extremity of a thing's quantity and its termination; and if it is devoid of quantity, it must be devoid of the extremity of quantity, because when quantity is non-existent, the occurrence of the extremity of quantity is impossible. As for the sense of deviation, it is that the thing has volume and quantity, except that its quantity does not terminate at a final cut-off point; rather, whenever you count it or measure it, you find outside of it another part of it without needing repetition. If you understand this, we say: When we say that the essence of God Almighty is non-finite, we intend by it the sense of negation. We mean that He is transcendent (munazzah) beyond quantity, volume, position, and direction. And if this is the case, then the meaning by which it is valid to describe a thing as having a limit or extremity is negated from the essence of God Almighty, so His essence is non-finite in this interpretation. This is according to whoever says that He is transcendent beyond quantity, corporeality, position, and direction. As for whoever holds these doctrines, then it is the very fact of His being non-finite, either in all directions or in some directions, in the second interpretation. And this is the discussion on the interpretation of His being non-finite with respect to the essence.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the term 'non-finite' (ghayr mutanahi) as applied to God. The author begins by noting that the expression applies to God in three respects: essence, perpetuity of existence, and attributes. Then he distinguishes two meanings of 'having no end': (1) the sense of negation (salb), where the thing lacks the very capacity for limit or end; (2) the sense of deviation (ʻudul), where the thing has quantity but without a final boundary (i.e., infinite in extension). He explains negation: the end is the extremity of quantity; if quantity is absent, the extremity is impossible. The deviation sense: the thing has volume and quantity, but no terminal point; you always find more beyond. Applying this to God: when we say God's essence is non-finite, we mean the negation sense—He is beyond quantity, volume, position, and direction. This interpretation holds for those who affirm God's transcendence (tanzih) over corporeality. For those who attribute corporeality to God (e.g., anthropomorphists), they might understand it in the deviation sense, meaning God is infinite in all or some directions. The author is clearly advocating the negation interpretation, aligning with the Ash'ari/Sunni view of tanzih.

سلب
عدول
نهاية
مقدار
حجمية

حيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that calling God 'infinite' means He has endless extension in space, like an infinitely large body.
Correction: The passage clarifies that for God's essence, 'infinite' means negation of quantity itself, not unbounded quantity. God is beyond any spatial or quantitative attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the author, when we say God's essence is non-finite (ghayr mutanahi), which meaning is intended?
A) The sense of deviation (ʻudul)
B) The sense of negation (salb)
C) Both meanings simultaneously
D) Neither meaning

Key Takeaway
When describing God as non-finite, we intend pure negation of quantity and direction, not endless extension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allahu Akbar' (God is Greatest), do you ever imagine size or magnitude? How does this lesson change that? Commit today to replacing any spatial mental image with the truth that He is beyond quantity altogether.
?
If God has no place and no direction, how can He be 'near' to you personally? Are you content with a closeness that is not physical but real through knowledge and obedience? What will you do today to feel that nearness?
?
The author says God is 'non-finite' in the negation sense—He simply does not have the property of quantity. Does this make Him seem less real or less personal to you? Confront that feeling: is it because you are used to imagining God as a 'big being'? Resolve to worship Him as He has revealed Himself, not as your imagination dictates.
