Weakness of Both Sides' Arguments and Suspension of Judgment
Concludes that deniers' proofs are weak; affirmers' also weak; suspends judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author suspends rational judgment on the vision of Allah and bases his conclusion on prophetic reports and mystical unveilings.

Core Idea
Rational arguments fail; prophecy and unveiling tip the scale toward possibility.

After examining both deniers and affirmers of the vision of Allah, the author finds their rational proofs equally weak. Yet he does not leave the matter unresolved: prophetic reports and mystical experiences provide a basis for strong conjecture. This lesson explores that conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Recognize deniers' weakness
Read the first part where the author declares the deniers' proofs extremely weak. Note the strong language: 'extreme weakness and failure.'
2
Recognize affirmers' weakness
Read how the affirmers' proof (every existent can be seen) is met with strong objections that cannot be refuted. See that both sides are rationally inconclusive.
The author himself raised those objections, so he is impartial.
3
Understand suspension and shift
The author then says the inquiry remains suspended (tawaqquf) because rational arguments are weak. But immediately he introduces an exception: prophets and mystics provide information that strengthens conjecture.
This shows that while reason is insufficient, revelation and mystical insight offer direction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فظهر بهذه البيانات: أن هذه الدلائل التي تمسك بها نفاة الرؤية في غاية الضعف والسقوط. وأما مثبتو الرؤية فقد عولوا على أن قالوا: الله تعالى موجود، وكل موجود فإنه تصح رؤيته، ودليلهم في الإثبات: أن كل موجود تصح رؤيته: قد ذكرناه في أحكام الموجودات، وأوردنا عليه اعتراضات قوية، لا يمكن دفعها البتة. وإذا عرفت ضعف دلائل الفريقين، فنقول: بقي هذا البحث في محل التوقف، إلا أنا رأينا الأنبياء والرسل عليهم السلام يخبرون عن حصول هذه الرؤية، ورأينا أصحاب المكاشفات يخبرون عن وقوع أحوال كأنها جارية مجرى المقدمات لهذه الرؤية، فقوي الظن في جواز وقوعها، وحقائق الأشياء لا يعرفها بتمامها إلا الله الحكيم.
So it became clear from these explanations that these proofs held by the deniers of vision are in extreme weakness and failure. And as for the affirmers of vision, they relied on saying: God Almighty is existent, and every existent can be seen; and their proof in affirmation: that every existent can be seen – we have mentioned it in the rulings of existents, and we have raised against it strong objections that cannot be refuted at all. And when you know the weakness of the proofs of both groups, we say: This inquiry remains in a position of suspension, except that we see the prophets and messengers, peace be upon them, informing about the occurrence of this vision, and we see the people of unveilings (mukāshafāt) informing about the occurrence of states that seem to be like premises for this vision, so the conjecture was strengthened regarding its possibility. And the realities of things are not known in their entirety except by Allah, the Wise.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage provides the author's conclusion after reviewing both sides. He declares the deniers' proofs extremely weak. For the affirmers, their argument (every existent can be seen) faces strong objections from the author himself. Thus, the matter remains suspended (tawaqquf) from a rational standpoint. However, the author finds support from prophetic reports and mystical unveilings that suggest the possibility of vision, leading to a strong conjecture (ghann) that it is possible. Ultimate knowledge belongs to God.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the author definitively affirms vision with certainty.
Correction: The author only says conjecture (ghann) is strengthened, not certainty. He maintains that full knowledge belongs to Allah.

C) Because he has no opinion on the matter.
D) Because the Quran explicitly prohibits discussion.

Key Takeaway
Rational arguments alone are insufficient to settle the vision of Allah; prophetic and mystical testimony strengthens the conjecture of its possibility, but ultimate knowledge rests with Allah.

?
The conclusion says 'the realities of things are not known in their entirety except by Allah.' Do you live with that epistemic humility, or do you assume you can fully comprehend divine matters? What would change if you truly accepted that limitation?

Refuting the Rational Proof for Vision of God
Editor refutes rational proof by citing Quran and falsifying major premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the editor rejects rational proofs for the possibility of seeing God, specifically by critiquing the premise that every existent can be seen.

Core Idea
Not everything that exists can be seen—intellect, humanity, and courage are invisible.

Before examining scriptural arguments, the editor addresses the rational approach. Some claim that since God exists, He must be seeable. But the editor argues this premise is false, using everyday examples of invisible existents. This lesson unpacks that critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the first two sentences
Read the Arabic from 'لا يصح لهم' to 'لا تدركه الأبصار'. Note the editor's immediate appeal to Quranic text as counter-evidence.
2
Identify the major premise
The editor states the major premise is 'every existent can be seen'. Find the examples given to show it is false.
3
Reflect on the examples
Think about why intellect, humanity, and courage are considered existent but not visible. How does this undermine the rationalist claim?
This step connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يصح لهم الإثبات بدليل عقلي. لورود النص بمنع الرؤية في قوله تعالى: «لا تدركه الأبصار» [الأنعام 103] ولأن القضية الكبرى كاذبة فإن العقل موجود، ولا يرى، والإنسانية لا ترى، والشجاعة لا ترى. وهي أشياء موجودة على رأى المؤلف وابن سينا. وقول المؤلف: إن الرؤية تثبت 1- بأخبار الأنبياء 2- ورؤى المتصوفة: قول يدل على منع الرؤية. فإن الرؤى لا تثبت عقائد دينية. وأخبار الأنبياء دلت على منع الرؤية. ففي التوراة في أسفار موسى الخمسة لما طلب موسى عليه السلام من الله أن يراه: قال له: لا تقدر أن ترى وجهي لأن الإنسان لا يراني ويعيش. وهذا النص يمنع الرؤية، لأن الإنسان لا يتحمل الرؤية. وفي سفر أشعياء: وحقاً: أنت إله محتجب يا إله إسرائيل، وفي إنجيل يوحنا: والله لم يره أحد قط، واليهود يقولون: إن الله لا يرى - يضم الياء - كما يقول العقلاء من المسلمين، والنصارى خالفوا النصوص المقدمة عندهم التي تمنع الرؤية، وقالوا: بجواز الرؤية، لأنهم يعتقدون - زورا - أن الله ظهر للناس في بدن المسيح. فالناس لما رأوا المسيح، فإنهم قد رأوا الله - على زعمهم - ودخل زعمهم هذا في عقائد بعض المسلمين، كما دخل زعمهم في كلمة الله التي هي أقنوم العلم عندهم لقد زعموا إن المسيح، قديمة مع الله. فيكون المسيح الذي هو الكلمة إلها قديما - الله تعالى عما يقولون علوا كبيرا - فيكون الكلام زائدا على الذات وفي الأحاديث النبوية: أن عائشة - رضي الله عنها - قالت: من حدثكم أن محمدا قد رأى ربه، فقد أعظم الفرية. ثم قرأت: «لا تدركه الأبصار»، وهذا النص محكم لأن له تفسيرا واحدا هو منع الإدراك. وفي القرآن نصان متشابهان هما: 1- إنهم عن ربهم يومئذ لمحجوبون، 2- إلى ربها ناظرة، والنص الأول يحتمل أنهم محجوبون عن رضا الله ورحمته، لا عن رؤية وجهه، والنص الثاني يحتمل أنهم ينظرون إلى نعم الله وخيراته على أهل الجنة. والدليل إذا تطرق إليه الاحتمال، صار متشابها، ويلزم رده. والحالة هذه إلى المحكم، لبيان مراد الله عز وجل.
It is not valid for them to affirm by a rational proof. Because the text has come prohibiting vision in His statement: 'Vision does not perceive Him' [Quran 6:103], and because the major premise is false, for intellect exists and is not seen, and humanity is not seen, and courage is not seen, and they are existing things according to the opinion of the author and Ibn Sina. And the author's statement that vision is established 1- by reports of prophets 2- and visions of Sufis: a statement that indicates the denial of vision. For visions do not establish religious beliefs. And the reports of prophets indicate the denial of vision. In the Torah in the five books of Moses, when Moses asked God to see Him, He said: 'You cannot see My face, for no man sees Me and lives.' This text prohibits vision because man cannot bear vision. In the book of Isaiah: 'Truly, You are a hidden God, O God of Israel,' and in the Gospel of John: 'No one has ever seen God.' And the Jews say that God is not seen (yughayyu?) as reasonable Muslims say. And the Christians contradicted the texts they have that prohibit vision and said it is permissible because they falsely believe that God appeared to people in the body of Christ. When people saw Christ, they claimed to have seen God, and this claim entered into the beliefs of some Muslims, just as their claim entered about the Word of God which is the hypostasis of knowledge with them. They claimed that Christ is eternal with God, so Christ who is the Word would be an eternal god – exalted is God above what they say greatly – so speech (al-kalām) would be additional to the essence. In the prophetic hadiths, Aisha – may God be pleased with her – said: 'Whoever tells you that Muhammad saw his Lord has fabricated a great lie.' Then she recited 'Vision does not perceive Him.' This text is muhkam because it has one interpretation: denial of perception. In the Quran there are two similar texts: 1- 'Indeed they will be veiled from their Lord on that day,' 2- 'Toward her Lord is looking.' The first text may mean they are veiled from God's pleasure and mercy, not from seeing His face; the second text may mean they look at God's blessings and bounties upon the people of Paradise. When a proof is subject to probability, it becomes mutashābih and must be referred back to the muhkam to clarify the intended meaning of Allah Almighty.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is an editorial commentary critiquing the author's stance. The editor argues that rational proof for vision is invalid because the Quran explicitly says 'Vision does not perceive Him' (6:103). They also claim the major premise 'every existent can be seen' is false, using examples like intellect, humanity, courage. The editor then addresses the author's reliance on prophets and mystics: visionary dreams cannot establish dogma, and prophetic reports actually deny vision (citing Moses, Isaiah, John, and Aisha's hadith). The editor also explains that two Quranic verses about seeing God (veiling and looking) are ambiguous (mutashābih) and must be interpreted according to the clear (muhkam) verse that denies perception. Thus, the editor concludes that vision is definitively denied.

Key Terms
لا تدركه الأبصار
القضية الكبرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if something exists, it must be potentially visible.
Correction: Invisible non-abstract entities like intellect exist but are not seeable, so existence does not entail visibility.

Checkpoint
What is the major premise that the editor claims is false?
A) Vision is impossible for God.
B) Every existent can be seen.
C) Rational proofs are invalid.
D) The Quran prohibits vision.

Key Takeaway
Rational proofs for vision fail because the premise that all existents are seeable is false, and the Quran already denies vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The intellect is real but unseen—do you live as though the unseen realities of your faith (Allah, angels, the Hereafter) are more certain than what your eyes show you? Where do you need to repent of demanding sensory proof?
?
Courage is invisible, yet you admire it in others. If you truly believed that God is beyond vision (as the Quran states), how would your reverence and awe in worship change today?

Critiquing Evidence from Prophetic Reports and Mystical Visions
Editor critiques author's use of prophetic reports and Sufi visions, citing biblical denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the editor rejects prophetic reports and Sufi visions as evidence for seeing God, and how biblical texts and Jewish/Christian views are used to support denial.

Core Idea
Prophetic reports and visions cannot confirm vision; they actually deny it.

The author argued that vision is established through accounts of prophets and Sufi visions. The editor turns this on its head: these sources actually indicate denial. He cites biblical passages where Moses is told no one can see God, and notes that even Jews agree. This lesson traces that argument and its theological consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's claim and editor's response
Read from 'وقول المؤلف' to 'يدل على منع الرؤية'. Understand that the editor reinterprets the evidence as supporting denial.
2
Examine the biblical evidence
Look at the three biblical texts: Moses, Isaiah, John. How does each prohibit vision?
3
Understand the Christian deviation
Read how Christians changed their view due to the incarnation, and how that error affected some Muslims.
The editor links this to the doctrine of the Word and hypostasis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يصح لهم الإثبات بدليل عقلي. لورود النص بمنع الرؤية في قوله تعالى: «لا تدركه الأبصار» [الأنعام 103] ولأن القضية الكبرى كاذبة فإن العقل موجود، ولا يرى، والإنسانية لا ترى، والشجاعة لا ترى. وهي أشياء موجودة على رأى المؤلف وابن سينا. وقول المؤلف: إن الرؤية تثبت 1- بأخبار الأنبياء 2- ورؤى المتصوفة: قول يدل على منع الرؤية. فإن الرؤى لا تثبت عقائد دينية. وأخبار الأنبياء دلت على منع الرؤية. ففي التوراة في أسفار موسى الخمسة لما طلب موسى عليه السلام من الله أن يراه: قال له: لا تقدر أن ترى وجهي لأن الإنسان لا يراني ويعيش. وهذا النص يمنع الرؤية، لأن الإنسان لا يتحمل الرؤية. وفي سفر أشعياء: وحقاً: أنت إله محتجب يا إله إسرائيل، وفي إنجيل يوحنا: والله لم يره أحد قط، واليهود يقولون: إن الله لا يرى - يضم الياء - كما يقول العقلاء من المسلمين، والنصارى خالفوا النصوص المقدمة عندهم التي تمنع الرؤية، وقالوا: بجواز الرؤية، لأنهم يعتقدون - زورا - أن الله ظهر للناس في بدن المسيح. فالناس لما رأوا المسيح، فإنهم قد رأوا الله - على زعمهم - ودخل زعمهم هذا في عقائد بعض المسلمين، كما دخل زعمهم في كلمة الله التي هي أقنوم العلم عندهم لقد زعموا إن المسيح، قديمة مع الله. فيكون المسيح الذي هو الكلمة إلها قديما - الله تعالى عما يقولون علوا كبيرا - فيكون الكلام زائدا على الذات وفي الأحاديث النبوية: أن عائشة - رضي الله عنها - قالت: من حدثكم أن محمدا قد رأى ربه، فقد أعظم الفرية. ثم قرأت: «لا تدركه الأبصار»، وهذا النص محكم لأن له تفسيرا واحدا هو منع الإدراك. وفي القرآن نصان متشابهان هما: 1- إنهم عن ربهم يومئذ لمحجوبون، 2- إلى ربها ناظرة، والنص الأول يحتمل أنهم محجوبون عن رضا الله ورحمته، لا عن رؤية وجهه، والنص الثاني يحتمل أنهم ينظرون إلى نعم الله وخيراته على أهل الجنة. والدليل إذا تطرق إليه الاحتمال، صار متشابها، ويلزم رده. والحالة هذه إلى المحكم، لبيان مراد الله عز وجل.
It is not valid for them to affirm by a rational proof. Because the text has come prohibiting vision in His statement: 'Vision does not perceive Him' [Quran 6:103], and because the major premise is false, for intellect exists and is not seen, and humanity is not seen, and courage is not seen, and they are existing things according to the opinion of the author and Ibn Sina. And the author's statement that vision is established 1- by reports of prophets 2- and visions of Sufis: a statement that indicates the denial of vision. For visions do not establish religious beliefs. And the reports of prophets indicate the denial of vision. In the Torah in the five books of Moses, when Moses asked God to see Him, He said: 'You cannot see My face, for no man sees Me and lives.' This text prohibits vision because man cannot bear vision. In the book of Isaiah: 'Truly, You are a hidden God, O God of Israel,' and in the Gospel of John: 'No one has ever seen God.' And the Jews say that God is not seen (yughayyu?) as reasonable Muslims say. And the Christians contradicted the texts they have that prohibit vision and said it is permissible because they falsely believe that God appeared to people in the body of Christ. When people saw Christ, they claimed to have seen God, and this claim entered into the beliefs of some Muslims, just as their claim entered about the Word of God which is the hypostasis of knowledge with them. They claimed that Christ is eternal with God, so Christ who is the Word would be an eternal god – exalted is God above what they say greatly – so speech (al-kalām) would be additional to the essence. In the prophetic hadiths, Aisha – may God be pleased with her – said: 'Whoever tells you that Muhammad saw his Lord has fabricated a great lie.' Then she recited 'Vision does not perceive Him.' This text is muhkam because it has one interpretation: denial of perception. In the Quran there are two similar texts: 1- 'Indeed they will be veiled from their Lord on that day,' 2- 'Toward her Lord is looking.' The first text may mean they are veiled from God's pleasure and mercy, not from seeing His face; the second text may mean they look at God's blessings and bounties upon the people of Paradise. When a proof is subject to probability, it becomes mutashābih and must be referred back to the muhkam to clarify the intended meaning of Allah Almighty.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is an editorial commentary critiquing the author's stance. The editor argues that rational proof for vision is invalid because the Quran explicitly says 'Vision does not perceive Him' (6:103). They also claim the major premise 'every existent can be seen' is false, using examples like intellect, humanity, courage. The editor then addresses the author's reliance on prophets and mystics: visionary dreams cannot establish dogma, and prophetic reports actually deny vision (citing Moses, Isaiah, John, and Aisha's hadith). The editor also explains that two Quranic verses about seeing God (veiling and looking) are ambiguous (mutashābih) and must be interpreted according to the clear (muhkam) verse that denies perception. Thus, the editor concludes that vision is definitively denied.

Key Terms
رؤى المتصوفة
أخبار الأنبياء
اليهود
النصارى
أقنوم العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that biblical texts support seeing God because of stories like Moses speaking to God.
Correction: The editor shows that those very texts (Moses, Isaiah, John) explicitly prohibit vision.

Checkpoint
According to the editor, what do the prophetic reports from the Torah, Isaiah, and John indicate?
A) That God can be seen by prophets.
B) That vision is impossible.
C) That Sufi visions are valid.
D) That Christians are correct.

Key Takeaway
Prophetic accounts and visions do not prove vision; they consistently point to its impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that visions do not establish religious beliefs, what personal experiences or feelings have you relied on to confirm your faith? Are you willing to measure them against the clear statements of revelation, even if it means letting go of cherished experiences?
?
The editor shows that Christians allowed the vision of God based on a false doctrine. Have you ever accepted a belief about God because it felt comforting or popular, even when the texts seemed to contradict it? What would it cost you to submit to what the texts actually say?
?
The Jews and Muslims agree that God cannot be seen, yet many still desire a sensory encounter with Him. In your worship, do you seek God through His revealed words or through emotional experiences? Which one truly satisfies the soul, and which one are you more tempted by?

Resolving Ambiguous Quranic Verses on Seeing God
Editor uses muhkam/mutashābih principle to interpret Quranic verses on vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the editor uses the Quranic principle of muhkam and mutashābih to interpret verses about seeing God, and why ambiguous verses must be referred back to clear ones.

Core Idea
Ambiguous verses about vision are resolved by the clear verse 'Vision does not perceive Him'.

After dismissing rational and historical arguments, the editor turns to Quranic hermeneutics. Some verses seem to suggest believers will see God, but the editor argues they are ambiguous (mutashābih) and must be interpreted in light of the clear (muhkam) verse that denies perception. This lesson walks through that reasoning, including the hadith of Aisha.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith of Aisha
Start with Aisha's statement and her recitation of 'Vision does not perceive Him'. Why does she call it a great lie?
2
Understand muhkam and mutashābih
The editor explains that 'Vision does not perceive Him' is muhkam (clear) because it has one meaning. Then two other verses are presented as mutashābih (ambiguous). List them.
3
Apply the hermeneutical rule
The editor says ambiguous proofs must be referred back to the clear one. How does that settle the question of vision?
This step connects to the learning objective.

Why Knowing God's Attributes Does Not Mean Knowing His Essence
Lists the third and fourth types of knowledge of God and proves they do not yield essence-knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the four types of knowledge about God (existence, necessity, negations, and relations) do not amount to knowledge of His essence, and why the essence remains unknown.

Core Idea
Knowing God's attributes reveals what He does and does not possess, but not what He is.

This passage from al-Maṭālib al-ʿĀliyah tackles a deep question: can humans know God's essence? The author argues that although we can know many things about God—that He exists, that He is eternal, what He is not, and how He relates—none of these give us direct knowledge of His inner reality. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of knowledge
Read the first part of the passage to see what the third and fourth types are. The first two are mentioned only in passing—focus on the two listed here.
The third type is negations (sulub), the fourth is relations (idafat).
2
Understand the second premise
The author explains why each of the four types does not give knowledge of the essence. Work through each argument: existence, necessity, negations, relations.
Notice the logic: if existence is an attribute, then knowing it is not knowing the essence itself.
3
Grasp the third premise
The author asserts that introspection shows we have no other knowledge of God beyond these four. Confirm your understanding by reflecting on your own thoughts.
This premise is crucial for the conclusion.
4
Assemble the conclusion
Combine the premises: we have only four types of knowledge, and none of them is knowledge of the essence. Therefore, knowledge of the essence is impossible.
The conclusion is stated explicitly at the end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثالث : وهو معرفة السلوب . فهي مثل علمنا بأنه تعالى ليس بمتحيز ، ولا في مكان ، ولا حال ، ولا في محل ، ولا ملون ، ولا يكيف ، ولا مركب ، ولا مبعض
وأما الرابع : وهو معرفة الإضافات فهو مثل علمنا بأنه يصح منه الفعل والترك. وهذه الصحة إضافة مخصوصة ، ومثل علمنا بكونه عالما ، والعلم عبارة عن إضافة مخصوصة بين العالم وبين المعلوم. فهذه الأنواع الأربعة من المعارف حاصلة للعقول البشرية .
المقدمة الثانية : إن العلم بهذه المعلومات ليس علما بالذات المخصوصة ، التي هي ذات الله تعالى . أما العلم بالوجود فليس هذا العلم علما بذات الله لانا قد دللنا على أن وجود الله صفة قائمة بذات الله تعالى ، وأما العلم بكونه واجب الوجود لذاته ، ويكونه دائم الوجود، فذلك أيضا ليس علما بالذات المخصوصة ، لأنا بينا أن العلم بالوجوب علم بكيفية مخصوصة من كيفيات ذلك الموجود ، وذلك العلم [ بالدوام، علم بكيفية مخصوصة من كيفيات ذلك الوجود ، ولما ثبت أن العلم (١)] بالوجود ، ليس نفس العلم بتلك الذات المخصوصة ، فالعلم بكيفيات ذلك الوجود أولى أن لا يكون نفس العلم بتلك الذات المخصوصة . وأما العلم بتلك السلوب ، فهو ليس نفس العلم بتلك الذات المخصوصة . لأنا إذا قلنا : إنه ليس بجوهر ، ولا بعرض ، فالمفهوم منه : سلب الجوهرية والعرضية . وذاته المخصوصة ، ليست عبارة عن عين هذه السلوب ، والعلم به ضروري . وأما العلم بتلك الإضافات ، فليس هو نفس العلم بتلك الذات المخصوصة ، لأن الإضافات الحاصلة بين الشيئين ، مغاير لهما معا . فالعلم بتلك الإضافة لا يكون نفس العلم بتلك الذات المخصوصة . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن هذه العلوم الأربعة ليست عبارة عن العلم بتلك الذات المخصوصة . وأما بيان أن العلم بهذه المعلومات الأربعة ، لا يوجب العلم بالذات المخصوصة ،
فهو ظاهر . لأنا عند العلم بهذه المقدمات الأربعة ، لا نجد من أنفسنا إلا أنه شيء ما . لا نعرف أنه ما هو . ؟ إلا أنه موصوف بهذه الأنواع الأربعة من الصفات . وذلك يدل على أن العلم بهذه المعلومات، لا يوجب العلم بالذات المخصوصة
وأما المقدمة الثالثة : فهي في بيان أنها لا تعرف من الله تعالى إلا هذه الأنواع الأربعة من المعلومات . وهي (١) من رجع إلى نفسه واعتبر حال عقله وفهمه ، علم بالضرورة أنه ليس عنده [ من المعارف الإلهية إلا هذه الأنواع الأربعة ، ولما ثبت أنه ليس عنده (٢) ] إلا هذه المعارف الأربعة ، وثبت أنها ليست نفس العلم بذات الله تعالى ، ولا أنها توجب العلم بذات الله ، ظهر حينئذ أن العلم بالذات المخصوصة الله تعالى غير حاصل عندنا البتة
As for the third (type): it is knowledge of negations (sulub). That is like our knowledge that He, exalted, is not spatial, nor in a place, nor a mode, nor in a substrate, nor colored, nor qualified, nor composed, nor divided.
As for the fourth (type): it is knowledge of relations (idafat). That is like our knowledge that action and abstention are possible for Him. This possibility is a specific relation. And like our knowledge of Him being knowing, and knowledge is a specific relation between the knower and the known. So these four types of cognitions are attained by human minds.
The second premise: Knowledge of these objects of knowledge is not knowledge of the specific essence, which is the essence of God Almighty. As for knowledge of existence, this knowledge is not knowledge of God's essence, because we have proven that God's existence is an attribute subsisting in God's essence. And as for knowledge that He is necessary of existence in Himself and that He is everlasting in existence, that also is not knowledge of the specific essence, because we have explained that knowledge of necessity is knowledge of a specific modality among the modalities of that existent, and that knowledge of perpetuity is knowledge of a specific modality among the modalities of that existence. And since it is established that knowledge of existence is not itself knowledge of that specific essence, knowledge of the modalities of that existence is even more deserving of not being knowledge of that specific essence. And as for knowledge of those negations, it is not itself knowledge of that specific essence, because when we say: He is not a substance nor an accident, what is understood from it is the negation of substance-ness and accident-ness. And His specific essence is not identical to these negations; this is self-evident. And as for knowledge of those relations, it is not itself knowledge of that specific essence, because relations occurring between two things are other than both of them. So knowledge of that relation is not itself knowledge of that specific essence. So it is established by what we have mentioned that these four knowledges are not knowledge of that specific essence. And as for the explanation that knowledge of these four objects of knowledge does not necessitate knowledge of the specific essence, it is obvious. Because when we know these four premises, we find within ourselves nothing but that it is something (shay' ma). We do not know what it is, except that it is described by these four types of attributes. And that indicates that knowledge of these objects of knowledge does not necessitate knowledge of the specific essence.
As for the third premise: It is an exposition that nothing is known of God Almighty except these four types of information. Whoever reflects on himself and considers the state of his mind and understanding knows necessarily that he has nothing of divine cognitions except these four types. And since it is established that he has nothing except these four cognitions, and it is established that they are not themselves knowledge of God's essence, nor do they necessitate knowledge of God's essence, then it becomes clear that knowledge of the specific essence of God Almighty is not attained by us at all.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is the first of two arguments demonstrating that human beings cannot attain knowledge of God's essence. The author begins by listing the third and fourth types of knowledge about God (the first two were presumably discussed earlier): the third is knowledge of negations (sulub)—attributes that deny imperfections—and the fourth is knowledge of relations (idafat), such as God's ability to act or abstain and His knowledge. He states that these four types are attainable by human minds.

Then he presents the second premise (the first premise, that these four types exist, is implied). The second premise is that knowledge of these four items is not knowledge of God's specific essence. He argues for each: knowledge of existence is not the essence because existence is an attribute; knowledge of necessity and perpetuity are knowledge of modalities, not the essence; knowledge of negations are merely negative concepts, not the essence; knowledge of relations are different from the relata. Therefore none of these four is identical to knowledge of the essence. Moreover, having all four does not necessitate knowing the essence; we only find in ourselves that it is 'something' described by these attributes, not what it is.

The third premise asserts that we have no other knowledge of God besides these four. By introspection, one sees that human minds contain only these four types of divine cognitions. Combining the second and third premises, the author concludes that knowledge of God's essence is utterly unattainable. The argument is carefully structured: it identifies the types of knowledge we have, shows each is not the essence, and then shows we have no alternative access.

Key Terms
السلوب
الإضافات
الذات المخصوصة
المقدمة الثانية
المقدمة الثالثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowing God's attributes (existence, power, knowledge, etc.) gives direct knowledge of His essence.
Correction: The author argues that each type of attribute is either a mode, a negation, or a relation, none of which is identical to the essence itself. Therefore, knowing these attributes does not equate to knowing the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does knowledge of God's existence not constitute knowledge of His essence?
A) Because existence is an attribute, not the essence itself.
B) Because existence is unknowable.
C) Because we only know existence through negations.
D) Because existence is a relation.

Key Takeaway
The essence of God is not known; only four types of attributes—existence, necessity, negations, and relations—are known, which do not reveal the essence itself.

?

?

Second Proof: Attributes Require Knowledge of the Essence
Al-Razi's second proof that essence must be known for attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that affirming divine attributes necessitates prior knowledge of the essence, and the role of the impossibility of infinite regress.

Core Idea
The subject of any divine attribute must be known, otherwise infinite regress follows.

Al-Razi presents a second argument that to attribute any quality to God, we must already have some knowledge of His essence. The proof relies on the logical structure of judgments and the impossibility of an infinite chain of attributable attributes. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the starting premise
Al-Razi begins with a logical principle: every judgment (tasdiq) is preceded by a conceptualization (tasawwur) of its subject and predicate. This means that when we make a claim like 'God is knowing,' we must have some concept of 'God' and 'knowing'.
2
Identify the two possibilities for the subject
When attributing attributes (sifat) to the essence (dhat), the subject of those attributes can be either the essence itself (min haythu hiya hiya) or the essence through other attributes. Read the Arabic to see how Al-Razi sets up this dichotomy.
The phrase 'من حيث هي هي' means 'insofar as it is itself', i.e., without any added condition.
3
Trace the first alternative
If the subject is the essence itself, then the subject of the predicates (attributes) is that specific essence, and it must be known (ma'luma). This directly shows that the essence is known.
4
Trace the second alternative
If the subject is the essence through other attributes, then we must explain how those attributes are attributed. This leads either to infinite regress (tasalsul) or to stopping at an attribute that is directly attributed to the essence itself. Both paths ultimately require the essence to be known.
Infinite regress is impossible because it would require an endless chain of attributes without a foundation.
5
Conclude the argument
Since both possibilities force us to accept that the essence itself must be known, the proof is complete. The essence is known prior to any attribute.
This proof does not tell us what the essence is, only that it must be known in some way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إن كل تصديق فإنه مسبوق بتصور الموضوع والمحمول فإذا أثبتنا الصفات لتلك الذات، فالذي يجعل موضوعا لهذه الصفات، إما أن يكون هو تلك الذات من حيث هي هي، وإما أن يكون هو تلك الذات بحسب صفات أخرى، فإن كان الأول فحينئذ يكون الموضوع لتلك المحمولات، التي هي الصفات، ليس إلا تلك الذات المخصوصة من حيث هي هي أن تكون معلومة، وإن كان الثاني وهو أن موضوع هذه الصفات تلك الذات بحسب صفات أخرى، فحينئذ ينتقل الكلام إلى كيفية استناد تلك الصفات . فإن أسندناها إلى صفات أخرى، لزم التسلسل وهو محال، أو الانتهاء إلى صفة نثبتها لتلك الذات من حيث هي هي، وحينئذ يلزم كون تلك الذات معلومة متصورة من حيث إن موضوع القضية يجب كونه معلوما .
The second proof: Every judgment is preceded by a conceptualization of its subject and predicate. So when we affirm attributes (sifat) for that essence (dhat), that which is made the subject for these attributes is either that essence itself (min haythu hiya hiya) or that essence through other attributes. If it is the first, then the subject for those predicates, which are the attributes, is nothing but that specific essence itself, which must be known. If it is the second, i.e., that the subject of these attributes is that essence through other attributes, then the discussion shifts to how those attributes are attributed. If we attribute them to other attributes, this leads to infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible; or we end at an attribute that we affirm for that essence itself, and then it necessitates that that essence itself be known and conceptualized (mutasawwara), since the subject of the judgment must be known.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof that the essence must be known. The proof is based on the structure of attributing attributes to a subject. Whenever we say 'God is knowing' or 'God is powerful,' we make a judgment with a subject (God) and a predicate (knowing). The subject of such judgments must be known. Now, the subject is either the essence in itself (without any attribute) or the essence under some attribute. If it is the essence in itself, then the essence itself is known (because the subject must be known). If it is the essence under some attribute, then we must examine how that attribute is attributed. Either we go back to another attribute ad infinitum (impossible), or we eventually reach an attribute that is directly attributed to the essence itself, which again requires the essence to be known. Thus, in either case, the essence must be known. This proof uses the impossibility of infinite regress (tasalsul) to force the conclusion.

Key Terms
ذات
صفات
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that attributes can be known without knowing the essence.
Correction: The argument shows that any attribution of an attribute ultimately requires the essence itself to be known, otherwise infinite regress occurs.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, what happens if we try to avoid the essence being known by attributing attributes via other attributes?
A) It leads to an infinite regress.
B) It is acceptable.
C) It proves that attributes are unnecessary.
D) It shows that the essence is unknown.

Key Takeaway
The essence of Allah must be known in itself before any attribute can be affirmed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite Allah's names and attributes, do you ever pause to consider that your knowledge of these attributes logically depends on a prior knowledge of His essence? How can you cultivate that direct awareness of His essence rather than just cataloguing His attributes?
?
Al-Razi's argument forces you to confront infinite regress in your own thinking. Are there areas in your faith where you rely on a chain of reasons ('I believe because...') without ever arriving at a direct knowledge of Allah? What would it take to move from secondary proofs to immediate consciousness of the essence?
?
This proof shows that the subject of every attribute must be known. Have you ever affirmed something about Allah without truly having a concept of who He is in Himself? What might change if you made knowing the essence itself your primary spiritual goal, not just knowing about Him?

Razi's Answer: Negative Concepts of God's Essence
Razi answers the third objection by defining divine essence knowledge as negative concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses negative concepts to explain our knowledge of God's essence without implying composition.

Core Idea
Negative concepts protect divine simplicity by being external to quiddity.

Razi faces an objection: if we know God's essence, does that imply composition? He responds by clarifying that the knowledge we have is through negative concepts, which do not add real parts to the essence. This short but crucial passage shows how Islamic theologians defend divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice the key terms: مفهوم سلبي, سلوب, ماهية.
2
Understand the concept
Razi defines knowing the essence as understanding it as independent and not needing others. Recognize that this is a negative definition.
3
See the implication
Negative concepts are external to quiddity, so they don't cause composition. This also answers another objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب من الثالث : إنا إذا علمنا الذات ، فالمراد منه كونه مستقلا بنفسه ، غير محتاج إلى غيره، وهذا مفهوم سلبي ، والسلوب خارجة عن الماهيات . وهذا هو بعينه الجواب عن الوجه الرابع .
The answer to the third: When we know the essence, what is meant by it is its being independent by itself, not needing anything else, and this is a negative concept, and negative concepts are external to quiddities. This is exactly the answer to the fourth point.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage provides Razi's response to a third objection, likely concerning the nature of the knowledge we have of God's essence. Razi clarifies that when we say we know the essence, what is meant by 'essence' here is not a positive attribute but a negative concept: being independent (mustaqill bi nafsihi) and not needing anything else. Negative concepts (sulub) are not part of the quiddity (mahiyyah) itself; they are external to it. This avoids the problem of composition because a negative attribute does not add a real component to the essence. Razi notes that this same answer resolves the fourth objection as well, likely a similar concern.

Key Terms

سلوب
ماهية
مستقل بنفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That knowing God's essence means we grasp a positive attribute that adds to His essence.
Correction: Razi says our knowledge is a negative concept, not a positive attribute, so no composition arises.

Checkpoint

A) Grasping a positive attribute
B) Understanding it as independent and not needing anything else
C) Knowing its quiddity directly
D) Having a mental image of God

Key Takeaway
Razi explains that our knowledge of God is through negative concepts, which are external to quiddity and do not imply composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah, do you catch yourself describing Him with positive attributes that limit Him, like 'He is powerful' while imagining power like ours? How can you train your mind to instead affirm His transcendence through negations like 'He is not weak'? Commit to pausing each time you make a positive statement and think of its negative counterpart.
?
This lesson shows that our knowledge of Allah is through concepts like 'not needing anything else'. Look at your own life: where do you feel 'needy' or dependent? How does reflecting on Allah's absolute independence free you from clinging to created things? Make a concrete choice to let go of one worldly attachment this week.
?
If knowing Allah means grasping negative concepts, what does that say about the limitations of your intellect? Are you willing to humbly accept that your deepest knowledge is what He is not, rather than what He is? Write down one area where you have tried to 'define' Allah, and then replace it with a negation from this passage.

Why God Cannot Be Described with Light
Refutes objection about light as a divine attribute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribute of light cannot be attributed to God, based on the principle that God must be described with perfections and free from deficiencies.

Core Idea
God must be perfect; light implies deficiency.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a specific objection: since light is often seen as noble, why can't God be described with it? He responds by reaffirming the foundational rule that God must be free from all deficiency. Let's examine the objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic of the objection carefully. Notice how the objector assumes light could be compatible with transcendence.
2
Read the response
The response is concise. Focus on the principle: God must be described with perfection and free from deficiency.
3
Reflect on the principle
Consider why light is considered a deficiency. The passage does not elaborate, but the principle is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صفات النقص . وإذا كان كذلك ، فلم لا يجوز أن تكون ذات الله موصوفة بصفة النور مع كونه منزها عن الجسمية والجهة ؟ لما ثبت أنه يجب كونه تعالى موصوفا بصفات الكمال والمدح، ويجب كونه منزها عن صفات النقص والثاني : هب أنا سلمنا أنه ليس شيء منها صفة كمال ومدح ، لكن لم لا يجوز أن يكون موصوفا ببعضها دون البعض ؟ قوله : « إنه ليس اتصاف ذاته بعضها أولى من اتصافها بالبواقي ، فنقول : قد بينا في علم المنطق أن قول القائل ليس هذا ، أولى من ذلك مقدمة ضعيفة . فإنه إن كان المراد عدم الأولوية في أذهاننا وفي عقولنا ، بمعنى أنا لا نعرف دليلا يدل على أن هذا أولى بالوقوع . فهذا مسلم ، إلا أن هذا لا يفيد إلا الوقف والشك ، وإن كان المراد بعدم الأولوية أنه يمتنع كون أحد الجانبين أولى في نفس الأمر ، فهذا ممنوع . فلم لا يجوز اتصاف ذاته ببعض هذه الأضداد أولى ، وإن كنا لا نهتدي ولا نعرف المعنى الموجب لتلك الأولوية ؟
Attributes of deficiency. And if it is so, then why is it not permissible for the essence of God to be described with the attribute of light while He is transcendent over corporeality and direction? Because it is established that He, exalted, must be described with attributes of perfection and praise, and must be transcendent over attributes of deficiency. And the second: Suppose we grant that none of them is an attribute of perfection and praise, but why is it not permissible for Him to be described with some of them without others? He said: 'It is not that His essence's description with some is more appropriate than its description with the rest.' So we say: We have explained in logic that the statement 'This is not more appropriate than that' is a weak premise. For if what is meant is the absence of priority in our minds and intellects, meaning that we do not know a proof indicating that this is more likely to occur, then this is granted, except that this only yields suspension and doubt. And if what is meant by the absence of priority is that it is impossible for one side to be more appropriate in reality, then this is denied. So why is it not permissible that His essence's description with some of these opposites is more appropriate, even if we do not attain nor know the cause necessitating that priority?
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage refutes two objections against the necessity of God being free from all deficiency attributes. The first objection asks why God cannot be described with the attribute of light (النور) while still being transcendent, since light is often seen as noble. The author responds by stating the foundational principle: God must be described with all attributes of perfection and praised qualities, and must be transcendent over all attributes of deficiency; light, if considered a deficiency, cannot be attributed to God. The second objection concedes that none of the attributes under discussion are perfections, but asks why God cannot be described with some of them and not others. The author refutes this by analyzing the claim of 'no priority' (عدم الأولوية). He distinguishes between two meanings: (1) lack of priority in our knowledge, which only leads to agnosticism, and (2) impossibility of priority in reality, which is denied. It is possible that in reality some attributes are more appropriate for denial, even if we do not know the reason. Thus the objections fail.

Key Terms
صفات النقص
صفات الكمال
النور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the attribute of light is noble and hence could be attributed to God even if He is transcendent.
Correction: The author establishes that God must be free from all deficiency attributes; light is a deficiency, so it cannot be attributed to God.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it not permissible for God to be described with the attribute of light?
A) Because light is not a praised attribute.
B) Because God must be described with perfections and free from deficiencies.
C) Because light implies direction.
D) Because the author does not provide a reason.

Key Takeaway
God must be described with all perfections and be free from all deficiencies; light, being a deficiency, cannot be attributed to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah in your mind, do you ever unconsciously picture Him using concepts from the physical world, like light or direction? How does this lesson expose that as a deficiency in your understanding? What specific step will you take today to purify your mental image of Him?
?
If you truly believe that Allah must be free from all deficiencies, why do you still sometimes complain about His decrees or doubt His wisdom? Reflect on a recent moment of discontent—how did it contradict the perfection of His attributes?
?
The principle here is that Allah must be described only by what He has revealed about Himself. In what areas of your life do you speak about Allah based on your own assumptions rather than revelation? Will you commit to learning and checking your words against the Quran and Sunnah from now on?

The Flaw in the Argument of No Priority
Refutes objection about lack of priority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between subjective and objective priority and why the claim of 'no priority' does not justify describing God with deficiency attributes.

Core Idea
Our ignorance does not make all options equally possible.

The second objection concedes that the attributes in question are not perfections, but argues that God could still be described with some of them. The refutation hinges on the meaning of 'no priority'. We'll see how Sanusi distinguishes between subjective ignorance and objective reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector says: even if none are perfections, why can't God be described with some? He claims there is no priority.
2
Understand the premise
The objector's premise is that there is no reason to prefer one option over another.
3
Analyze the two meanings
The author distinguishes between subjective lack of knowledge and objective impossibility. Note the logical distinction.
4
Conclude
The objection fails because the premise is ambiguous. Real priority may exist even if we are ignorant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صفات النقص . وإذا كان كذلك ، فلم لا يجوز أن تكون ذات الله موصوفة بصفة النور مع كونه منزها عن الجسمية والجهة ؟ لما ثبت أنه يجب كونه تعالى موصوفا بصفات الكمال والمدح، ويجب كونه منزها عن صفات النقص والثاني : هب أنا سلمنا أنه ليس شيء منها صفة كمال ومدح ، لكن لم لا يجوز أن يكون موصوفا ببعضها دون البعض ؟ قوله : « إنه ليس اتصاف ذاته بعضها أولى من اتصافها بالبواقي ، فنقول : قد بينا في علم المنطق أن قول القائل ليس هذا ، أولى من ذلك مقدمة ضعيفة . فإنه إن كان المراد عدم الأولوية في أذهاننا وفي عقولنا ، بمعنى أنا لا نعرف دليلا يدل على أن هذا أولى بالوقوع . فهذا مسلم ، إلا أن هذا لا يفيد إلا الوقف والشك ، وإن كان المراد بعدم الأولوية أنه يمتنع كون أحد الجانبين أولى في نفس الأمر ، فهذا ممنوع . فلم لا يجوز اتصاف ذاته ببعض هذه الأضداد أولى ، وإن كنا لا نهتدي ولا نعرف المعنى الموجب لتلك الأولوية ؟
Attributes of deficiency. And if it is so, then why is it not permissible for the essence of God to be described with the attribute of light while He is transcendent over corporeality and direction? Because it is established that He, exalted, must be described with attributes of perfection and praise, and must be transcendent over attributes of deficiency. And the second: Suppose we grant that none of them is an attribute of perfection and praise, but why is it not permissible for Him to be described with some of them without others? He said: 'It is not that His essence's description with some is more appropriate than its description with the rest.' So we say: We have explained in logic that the statement 'This is not more appropriate than that' is a weak premise. For if what is meant is the absence of priority in our minds and intellects, meaning that we do not know a proof indicating that this is more likely to occur, then this is granted, except that this only yields suspension and doubt. And if what is meant by the absence of priority is that it is impossible for one side to be more appropriate in reality, then this is denied. So why is it not permissible that His essence's description with some of these opposites is more appropriate, even if we do not attain nor know the cause necessitating that priority?
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage refutes two objections against the necessity of God being free from all deficiency attributes. The first objection asks why God cannot be described with the attribute of light (النور) while still being transcendent, since light is often seen as noble. The author responds by stating the foundational principle: God must be described with all attributes of perfection and praised qualities, and must be transcendent over all attributes of deficiency; light, if considered a deficiency, cannot be attributed to God. The second objection concedes that none of the attributes under discussion are perfections, but asks why God cannot be described with some of them and not others. The author refutes this by analyzing the claim of 'no priority' (عدم الأولوية). He distinguishes between two meanings: (1) lack of priority in our knowledge, which only leads to agnosticism, and (2) impossibility of priority in reality, which is denied. Key Terms
عدم الأولوية
الوقف والشك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if we cannot prove priority, then both options are equally possible.
Correction: The author clarifies that our ignorance does not negate real priority; it is possible that in reality some attributes are more fitting to deny.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response to the claim that there is no priority between describing God with some deficiency attributes and not others?
A) He accepts it and concludes that it does not matter.
B) He distinguishes between subjective and objective priority, and denies objective priority.
C) He argues that priority is impossible because God is one.
D) He says that the question has no answer.

Key Takeaway
Our ignorance of why some deficiency attributes are more fitting to deny does not mean that all are equally possible; real priority can exist without our knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a theological uncertainty—like not knowing why God does something—do you unconsciously assume that because you see no reason, there is none? How does this lesson's distinction between epistemic and real priority confront that tendency? Will you commit to stopping that assumption today?
?

?

Is there a statement about Allah you have made recently that assumed your lack of knowledge proved equality of possibilities? Repent of it now and resolve to speak only from revelation.

Arguing that God Cannot Be a Locus for Accidents
Opening the fifteenth chapter: demonstrating that God cannot be a locus for other things, with first research on Ash'ari position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author argues that the Ash'ari position necessarily implies that God is a locus for accidents, contrary to their own claims.

Core Idea
The Ash'aris unwittingly make God a locus for change.

The author begins the fifteenth chapter by addressing a challenging question: can God be a locus for accidents? While only the Karamiyya openly admit this, the author contends that many other schools, especially the Ash'aris, are logically committed to it. This lesson unpacks the two arguments he uses to show that Ash'ari theology leads to God being a locus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and grasp the claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's assertion that the Ash'aris, despite denying it, are logically forced to accept that God is a locus for accidents.
The author gives two arguments to support this.
2
Examine the first argument
Focus on the argument about God's power to create a specific body. Before creation, God has power; after creation, that power relation ceases because re-creation is impossible. This implies a change in God's attributes.
Think about why this might entail God being a locus for accidents.
3
Examine the second argument
Consider the argument about God's command. In pre-eternity, God does not command Zayd because Zayd doesn't exist. Once Zayd exists, God commands. The command (a demand) arises anew, implying an attribute that occurs in time.
This is another example of change in God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصيلات الخامس عشر في بيان أنه يمتنع كونه تعالى محلاً لغيره ههنا أبحاث البحث الأول : أنه هل يعقل أن يكون محلا للحوادث ؟ قالوا : إن هذا قول لم يقل به أحد إلا الكرامية . وأنا أقول : إن هذا قول قال به أكثر أرباب أهل المذاهب . أما الأشعرية : فإنهم يدعون الفرار من هذا القول ، إلا أنه لازم عليهم من وجوه : الأول : إنه تعالى كان قادرا على إيجاد الجسم المعين من الأزل إلى الأبد ، فإذا حلق ذلك الجسم المعين ، يمتنع أن يقال : إنه بقى قادرا على إيجاده ، لأن إيجاد الوجود محال . والمحال لا قدرة عليه ، فتعلق قادرينه بإيجاد ذلك الجسم قد زال وفني والثاني : إنه في الأزل يمتنع أن يقال: إنه كان يطلب من زيد إقامة الصلاة، وإيتاء الزكاة في الحال . ثم إن عند دخول زيد في الوجود ، يصير مطالبا له بإقامة الصلاة في الحال، وإيتاء الزكاة. وهذا الطلب إلزام ، والإلزام الحاصل ، ما كان حاصلا ثم حصل ، وهذا يقتضي حدوث الصفة في ذات الله
The fifteenth chapter: Demonstrating that it is impossible for Him, exalted, to be a locus (mahall) for other than Him. Here there are researches. The first research: Is it conceivable that He is a locus for accidents (hawadith)? They said: This is a statement that no one has made except the Karamiyya. And I say: This is a statement made by most of the leaders of the schools. As for the Ash'aris, they claim to flee from this statement, but it is necessarily incumbent upon them from some aspects: The first: He, exalted, was capable (qadir) of bringing the specific body into existence from pre-eternity to eternity. When He creates that specific body, it is impossible to say that He remains capable of bringing it into existence, because bringing into existence what exists is impossible, and the impossible is not within power. So the relation of His power to bringing that body into existence has ceased and perished. The second: In pre-eternity, it is impossible to say that He was demanding from Zayd the performance of prayer and payment of zakah at the present moment. Then, when Zayd comes into existence, He becomes demanding of him the performance of prayer and payment of zakah at the present moment. This demand is an obligation (ilzam), and the obligation that occurs was not occurring and then occurs, and this necessitates the origination (huduth) of the attribute in God's essence.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage opens the fifteenth chapter, which aims to prove that God cannot be a locus (mahall) for anything else. It begins by noting that there are several researches under this chapter. The first research asks whether it is conceivable that God is a locus for accidents (hawadith). The author remarks that only the Karamiyya sect explicitly held this view, but he asserts that in fact most school leaders implicitly hold it. He then focuses on the Ash'aris, who deny this, but argues that it is necessarily entailed by their own principles from two aspects. The first aspect: God's power to create a specific body from pre-eternity to eternity. When He actually creates that body, He cannot remain powerful over bringing it into existence because bringing into existence what already exists is impossible. Thus, the relation of His power to that act ceases, implying a change in God's attributes. The second aspect: In pre-eternity, God could not have been commanding Zayd to perform prayer and pay zakah because Zayd did not exist. But when Zayd comes into existence, God becomes commanding. This command is an obligation that was not present and then becomes present, which would entail the origination (huduth) of an attribute in God. The argument is that these imply that God is a locus for temporal events (mahall li'l-hawadith), which the Ash'aris deny, but their own positions lead to it.

قادر (qadir)

Correction: The author argues that most schools, especially the Ash'aris, are logically forced into this position by their own principles.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari claim to avoid making God a locus for accidents is undermined by their own principles regarding divine power and command.

God Cannot Be a Locus: The Claim and the Schools
Author corrects a common attribution and sets up the first argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's claim that the majority of schools inadvertently hold that God is a locus for accidents, and that the Ash'aris are not exempt from this implication.

Core Idea
The author turns the tables: it's not just the Karramiyya who say God is a locus.

This lesson begins the fifteenth chapter by stating its central thesis: God cannot be a locus for anything. The author first challenges a common attribution, arguing that the view that God is a locus of accidents is not limited to the Karramiyya but is actually held by most schools, including the Ash'aris, even if they deny it explicitly. We'll see how the author sets the stage for the first argument.

The First Objection: God's Power Cannot Cease
First proof that Ash'ari position entails God being a locus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first wajh against the Ash'aris: that God's power to create a specific body must cease after creation, implying a change in His attribute.

Core Idea
Creating a body removes God's power to create it — a change in His attribute.

In this lesson, we examine the first wajh (aspect) that the author uses to show that the Ash'ari position necessarily implies that God is a locus of accidents. The argument focuses on God's power: if God from eternity has the power to create a specific body, then when He creates that body, that power must cease because creating an already-existent thing is impossible. This cessation implies a change in God's attribute of power, which the Ash'aris reject. Let's unpack the logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the full argument
Read the Arabic passage for the first wajh along with its translation. Note the logical flow.
The argument is short but dense.
2
Identify the premise
The argument starts with 'God was powerful over bringing into existence the specific body from pre-eternity to eternity.' This is the premise.
This is an assumption the Ash'aris would accept.
3
Follow the consequence
After creation, the power to create that body becomes impossible (muhaal). Why? Because bringing an existent into existence is contradictory.
The key is the concept of 'إيجاد الوجود محال'.
4
Understand the implication
Since the power relation ceased, God's attribute of power changed. If the Ash'aris deny change in God, they are forced into a contradiction.
This is the core of the wajh.

And the impossible cannot be within power. So the attachment of His power to bringing that body into existence has ceased and perished.

This passage is an introductory argument within the fifteenth chapter. The author first states the chapter's topic: proving that God cannot be a locus for anything. Second Ash'ari Objection: Command and Accidental Attributes
Second wajh against Ash'aris: God's command to Zayd implies a new attribute in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second Ash'ari objection regarding God's command and why the attempted escape via relational changes still implies temporal attributes in God.

Core Idea
God's command to a newly created creature cannot be eternal; it implies a new attribute in God.

This lesson examines the second major objection raised by the Ash'aris against the Maturidi position. The argument focuses on divine command: before creation, God cannot be said to command a non-existent being. Once the being exists, God's command becomes actual, implying a change in God's attributes. The Ash'ari attempt to escape by referring to relations (nisab) and attachments (ta'alliqat) is shown to be self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the flow: argument, possible objection, and refutation.
The argument is in two halves: first the problem, then the objection and response.
2
Identify the logical dilemma
The author presents a dilemma: either relations (nisab) have existence in reality or they do not. Examine each horn.
If they do not exist, then God is not actually commanding. If they do, they are accidents in God's essence.
3
Connect to the learning objective
Understand why this objection is considered a refutation of the Ash'ari position on divine attributes.
This argument reinforces the Maturidi view that God's attributes are not temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : إنه في الأزل يمتنع أن يقال: إنه كان يطلب من زيد إقامة الصلاة، وإيتاء الزكاة في الحال . ثم إن عند دخول زيد في الوجود ، يصير مطالبا لـه بإقامة الصلاة في الحال، وإيتاء الزكاة. وهذا الطلب إلزام ، والإلزام الحاصل ، ما كان حاصلا ثم حصل ، وهذا يقتضي حدوث الصفة في ذات الله تعالى . ولو قال قائل : إن كونه مطالبا لزيد في الحال بإقامة الصلاة وإيتاء الزكاة تعلق خاص ونسبة خاصة، والحادث هو النسب والتعلقات ، لا الصفات . فنقول : هذه النسب والتعلقات ، هل لها وجود في نفس الأمر أو ليس كذلك ؟ والثاني يقتضي نفي كونه تعالى مطالبا في الحال بإقامة الصلاة ، وإيتاء الزكاة وأما الأول فيقتضي حدوث الصفة في ذات الله
The second: He, exalted, in pre-eternity it is impossible to say that He was demanding from Zayd the performance of prayer and the giving of zakah immediately. Then, when Zayd comes into existence, He becomes demanding of him the performance of prayer immediately and the giving of zakah. And this demand is an obligation, and the obligation that occurs was not occurring then occurred, and this entails the origination of an attribute in the essence of God, exalted. And if someone says: His being demanding of Zayd immediately the performance of prayer and the giving of zakah is a specific relation and a specific nexus, and what occurs are the relations and nexuses, not the attributes. Then we say: Do these relations and nexuses have existence in reality or not? The second entails denying that He, exalted, is demanding immediately the performance of prayer and the giving of zakah; as for the first, it entails the origination of an attribute in the essence of God.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents the second wajh against the Ash'aris. The argument is about divine command. In pre-eternity, before Zayd exists, it is meaningless to say God commands Zayd to pray now. But once Zayd exists, God becomes a commander to Zayd. This command is a real obligation (ilzam) that was not there before and then occurs, implying a new attribute in God. The author then presents a possible objection from the Ash'aris: that the change is only in relations (nisab) and attachments (ta'alliqat), not in God's essential attributes. The author counters by asking whether these relations have real existence (wujud fi nafs al-amr) or not. If they do not, then God is not actually commanding; if they do, then the relations themselves are accidents occurring in God's essence, which is the same problem. Thus, the Ash'aris cannot escape implying temporal attributes in God.

Key Terms
الطلب
الإلزام
النسب والتعلقات
حدوث الصفة
نفس الأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The change in God's command is only a change in relations, not in God's attributes, so God remains unchanged.
Correction: If relations have real existence, they are accidents in God's essence. If they do not, then God is not actually commanding—contradicting the clear teaching that God commands.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue about the Ash'ari objection that only relations change?
A) Relations have no existence, so the objection stands.
B) If relations have existence, they are accidents in God's essence.
C) Relations are not real, so God's command is eternal.
D) The objection is valid and accepted by the author.

Key Takeaway
The attempt to explain God's command through changing relations fails because if relations are real, they introduce accidents into God's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Third Ash'ari Objection: Divine Hearing and Seeing
Third wajh: hearing/seeing temporal objects implies new attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari position on divine hearing and seeing of temporal objects leads to an objection that implies temporal attributes in God's essence.

Core Idea
God's hearing of a sound begins when the sound begins — implying new attributes.

This passage presents the third objection against the Ash'ari view on divine attributes. It focuses on hearing (sam') and seeing (basar) as they relate to temporal objects. The objection argues that if God hears a specific sound only when it occurs, then a new attribute or state arises in God at that moment, which conflicts with the doctrine of eternal, unchanging attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Note the concise style: it states the impossibility, then the consequence.
2
Identify the two cases
The passage mentions two scenarios: hearing a voice and seeing an image. Notice that the reasoning is identical for both.
The pattern is: X cannot occur before existence, so being X occurs at the moment of existence.
3
Reflect on the implication
The objection concludes that this entails new attributes in God's essence. Ask yourself: why is this a problem for the Ash'ari? Connect to the broader discussion of divine attributes.
This implication challenges the Ash'ari claim that all attributes are eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث : وهو أنه تعالى يمتنع أن يسمع صوت زيد قبل وجوده ، ويرى صورة زيد قبل وجودها ، فكونه سامعا لذلك الصوت إنما حدث عند حدوث ذلك الصوت ، وكونه رائيا لتلك الصورة إنما حدث عند حدوث تلك الصورة وهذا يقتضي حدوث هذه الصفات في ذات الله تعالى
And the third: That He, exalted, cannot hear the voice of Zayd before its existence, nor see the image of Zayd before its existence. So His being hearing of that voice only occurs at the occurrence of that voice, and His being seeing of that image only occurs at the occurrence of that image, and this entails the origination of these attributes in the essence of God, exalted.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This short passage is the third wajh against the Ash'aris. It concerns God's attributes of hearing (sam') and seeing (basar) as they relate to temporal objects. The argument is straightforward: God cannot hear Zayd's voice before the voice exists; He becomes 'hearing that voice' only when the voice occurs. Similarly for seeing. Since the attribute of being hearing of a specific sound arises at the moment the sound occurs, this implies that a new attribute (or state) comes to be in God's essence. The Ash'aris typically maintain that God's attributes are eternal and unchanging, but this argument suggests that relational aspects of those attributes (hearing this particular sound) are temporal, which again commits them to admitting accidents in God's essence.

Key Terms
السمع
البصر
حدوث الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that God's hearing itself changes or becomes temporal.
Correction: The argument does not say that the attribute of hearing changes; it says that the specific relation of being hearing of a particular sound is temporal, not the attribute itself.

Checkpoint
What does the third wajh argue about God's hearing of Zayd's voice?
A) God hears all voices eternally.
B) God becomes hearing of that voice only when the voice occurs.
C) God's hearing is unaffected by the voice.
D) The voice is heard in God's essence eternally.

Key Takeaway
This objection argues that God's relational attributes of hearing and seeing specific temporal things arise at the moment of those things, implying that not all of God's attributes are eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's hearing of your prayer arises at the very moment you pray, does that make your prayer feel more immediate and urgent? Will you now pray with the certainty that God is hearing you right now, not from eternity past?
?
Reflect on a moment you sinned and felt unseen. According to this lesson, God's seeing of that sin occurred exactly when you committed it. How does that truth compel you to repent sincerely and never take His sight for granted?
?
The objection challenges the idea that all of God's attributes are eternal. Does this shake your confidence in God's perfection? Or does it deepen your awe that He relates to your temporal existence in real time? What will you do with that awe today?

Mu'tazila Views on Accidents in God's Essence
Overview of Mu’tazila views on temporal attributes in God’s essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the differences among Mu'tazila thinkers regarding whether temporal events and attributes occur in God's essence.

Core Idea
Some Mu‘tazilites admitted temporal changes in God’s attributes and knowledge.

In this passage, the author cites specific Mu‘tazila figures to support his broader argument that even schools which deny anthropomorphism and change in God often end up admitting some form of temporal occurrence in the divine essence. Abu Ali al-Jubba’i and his son Abu Hashim held that certain attributes like will and generosity occur in God’s essence, while Abu al-Husayn al-Basri went further, claiming that God’s very knowledge changes with the objects of knowledge. By examining these views, we see the internal diversity within Mu‘tazila thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the names and the distinct positions.
2
Identify the two positions
Find where the passage presents the view of Abu Ali and Abu Hashim, and then the view of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri. What do they claim occurs in God’s essence?
The first group mentions attributes (willing, generosity, hearing, seeing) ; the second focuses on knowledge.
3
Compare the two positions
How does Abu al-Husayn’s view differ from the earlier view? What does he add that is more radical?
He explicitly says God's knowledge changes, not just attributes.
4
Reflect on the author's purpose
Why does the author present these views? Is he criticizing them or using them as evidence? Consider the commentary.
The author is not criticizing; he is showing that even Mu'tazila admit accidents in God's essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعتزلة : فقد ذهب أبو علي وأبو هاشم إلى أنه يحدث في ذاته صفة المريدية والكرامية، ويحدث في ذاته كونه سامعا مبصرا لهذه الأصوات الحادثة، ولهذه الألوان الحادثة . وأما أبو الحسين البصري فقد صرح بأن علم الله يتغير عند تغير المعلومات، وأن تلك العلوم إنما تحدث في ذات الله تعالى

And as for Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, he explicitly stated that God's knowledge changes with the change of known things, and that those knowledges occur in the essence of God, exalted.

Commentary
This passage surveys the Mu'tazilite position. The author notes that both Abu Ali al-Jubba'i and his son Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i held that attributes like will (irada) and generosity (karama) occur temporally in God's essence, as well as the relational attributes of being hearing and seeing of particular temporal objects. Then Abu al-Husayn al-Basri went further, stating that even God's knowledge changes when the objects of knowledge change (e.g., knowing that Zayd exists only after he exists), and that these knowledges occur temporally in God's essence. By citing these prominent Mu'tazilites, the author strengthens his earlier claim that most schools of thought admit accidents in God's essence, even those who theoretically deny it.

Mu'tazila thinkers, despite their rationalism, admitted temporal occurrences in God's essence, with Abu al-Husayn al-Basri even allowing change in God's knowledge.
