Philosophers' Unwitting Admission of Accidents in God
Sanusi explains how philosophers inadvertently admit accidents in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers' realist ontology of relations leads to the conclusion that temporal attributes occur in God's essence.

Core Idea
Even those who deny accidents may admit them through relations.

The philosophers (falasifa) are known for denying that God is a locus of accidents. Yet, as this passage reveals, their commitment to the reality of relations forces them to concede that some attributes in God are temporal. Let's unpack how their own logic traps them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall argument. Notice the key terms: idafat, qabliyya, ma'iyya, ba'diyya.
The passage is a self-contained argument.
2
Identify the three relations
Identify the three temporal relations: priority (qabliyya), withness (ma'iyya), and posteriority (ba'diyya). Understand that they are relative to a temporal event.
These relations are central to the argument.
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow the reasoning: philosophers assert relations are real existents. God's relation 'withness' to a temporal event occurs at the event's time. Therefore, a real relation occurs in God's essence, i.e., an accident.
The conclusion is that philosophers inadvertently admit accidents.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this is a powerful critique: it shows that even the most rationalist school cannot avoid the implication that God has temporal attributes if they hold relations are real.
This demonstrates the consistency of the Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفلاسفة : فإنهم مع كونهم أبعد الناس عن هذا المذهب ، قد قالوا به من حيث لم يشعروا به. وبيانه : وهو أن الإضافات صفات موجودة في الأعيان عندهم ، ولا شك أن الباري تعالى موجود مع كل حادث يحدث ويدخل في الوجود . ولا شك أنه كان موجودا قبل حدوث ذلك الحادث ، وسيبقى موجودا بعد فناء ذلك الحادث، وهذه القبلية والمعية والبعدية ، إضافات حادثة في ذات الله تعالى ، وإذا كانت الإضافات موجودات في الأعيان ، كان هذا قولا بحدوث المعاني والصفات ، في ذات الله تعالى
And as for the philosophers: Even though they are the farthest people from this doctrine, they have stated it without realizing it. Its explanation is that relations are attributes existing in external entities according to them. There is no doubt that the Creator, exalted, exists with every accident that occurs and enters into existence. There is no doubt that He existed before the occurrence of that accident, and will remain existing after the annihilation of that accident. And these priority, withness, and posteriority are relations occurring in the essence of God, exalted. And if relations are existent in external entities, then this is a statement asserting the occurrence of meanings and attributes in the essence of God, exalted.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage deals with the philosophers (falasifa). The author notes that although they are the furthest from believing that God is a locus of accidents, they inadvertently hold the same position. The reasoning is that philosophers consider relations (idafat) to be real existents in the external world (a'yan). Now, consider any temporal event: God existed before it, He exists with it, and He will exist after it. These three relations—priorness (qabliyya), withness (ma'iyya), and posteriority (ba'diyya)—are all relations that attach to God. Since God did not have the relation of withness before the event came to be, that relation occurs at the moment of the event. If relations are real, then a real attribute (relation) occurs in God's essence. Thus, even the philosophers end up admitting accidents in God's essence, despite their denial.

Key Terms
الفلاسفة
الإضافات
القبلية
المعية
البعدية
الأعيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers explicitly affirm that God has temporal attributes.
Correction: No, they deny it, but the author shows that their realist ontology of relations forces the conclusion, even if they do not realize it.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how do the philosophers inadvertently admit accidents in God's essence?
A) They deny all attributes to God.
B) They hold that relations are real, and temporal relations like 'withness' occur in God.
C) They claim God's essence changes over time.
D) They believe God creates accidents in His own essence.

Key Takeaway
Even the most rationalist philosophers cannot avoid attributing temporal relations to God if they hold that relations are real entities in the external world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God being 'with you' in a moment of struggle or 'having been with you in the past,' do you realize you are attributing a temporal relation to God? How does this lesson challenge the way you imagine God's relationship to time? What must you change in your heart to think of God as unchanging yet present?
?
The philosophers unwittingly fell into error because they insisted their rational framework was correct. Is there a belief about God that you hold so firmly that you would reject any scriptural evidence against it? What step will you take today to submit your reasoning to revelation?
?
This passage shows that even the most careful thinkers can 'state something about God without realizing it.' In your own speech about God, where might you be inadvertently implying change or composition? Commit to one specific phrase about God you will correct starting today.

The Objection of Temporal Relations Refuted
Refutes an objection about temporal relations affecting attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that only relations are temporal leads to unacceptable consequences, and how the author refutes it by examining the reality of relations.

Core Idea
Relations cannot be temporal without affecting the attributes themselves.

Some object that divine attributes like commanding are not temporal; only the relation to creatures changes. But this objection itself leads to contradictions. In this passage, the author exposes the flaw by asking: do these relations have real existence or not? The answer forces the objector into an untenable position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objector's claim: that God's demanding prayer and zakat is a specific relation, and only relations are temporal, not the attributes.
This is a common objection against the eternity of attributes.
2
Follow the author's dilemma
The author asks whether these relations have real existence or not. This sets up two possibilities.
A third point is added later about hearing and seeing.
3
Analyze the first two horns
If relations have no real existence, then God is not actually demanding anything now. If they have real existence, then the attribute (being a demander) becomes temporal.
Both options contradict the objector's intention.
4
Consider the third point
The author adds: God cannot hear or see something before it exists, so His hearing and seeing occur only when the object exists. This makes the attributes of hearing and seeing temporal.
This further shows the objector's reasoning leads to temporality.
5
Conclude the refutation
The objection fails because it leads to either denying actual divine acts or making attributes temporal.
Thus the original position (attributes are eternal) is preserved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال قائل : إن كونه مطالبا لزيد في الحال بإقامة الصلاة وإيتاء الزكاة تعلق خاص ونسبة خاصة، والحادث هو النسب والتعلقات ، لا الصفات . فنقول : هذه النسب والتعلقات ، هل لها وجود في نفس الأمر أو ليس كذلك ؟ والثاني يقتضي نفي كونه تعالى مطالبا في الحال بإقامة الصلاة ، وإيتاء الزكاة وأما الأول فيقتضي حدوث الصفة في ذات الله. والثالث : وهو أنه تعالى يمتنع أن يسمع صوت زيد قبل وجوده ، يرى صورة زيد قبل وجودها ، فكونه سامعاً لذلك الصوت ، إنما حدث عند حدوث ذلك الصوت ، وكونه رائياً لتلك الصورة إنما حدث عند حدوث تلك الصورة وهذا يقتضي حدوث هذه الصفات في ذات الله تعالى
And if someone says: 'His being a demander of Zaid at the moment to perform prayer and give zakat is a specific relation and a specific attribution, and what is temporal is the relations and the attributions, not the attributes.' So we say: These relations and attributions, do they have existence in reality or not? The second entails the negation of His being a demander at the moment to perform prayer and give zakat. As for the first, it entails the temporality of the attribute in God's essence. And the third: that He, exalted, cannot hear Zaid's voice before its existence, nor see Zaid's form before its existence. So His being a hearer of that voice only occurs at the occurrence of that voice, and His being a seer of that form only occurs at the occurrence of that form. And this entails the temporality of these attributes in God's essence.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection that only the relations are temporal, not the attributes. The author presents the objector's claim: that God's demanding prayer and zakat from Zaid is a specific relation, and only the relation changes, not the attribute. The author then counters by asking: these relations and attributions, do they have real existence or not? He then presents three possibilities. (1) If they have no real existence, that would deny that God is actually demanding these actions. (2) If they have real existence, then the attribute (of being a demander) becomes temporal. (3) If God cannot hear or see something before it exists, then His hearing and seeing occur only when the sound or form comes into existence, implying that these attributes arise temporally in God. Thus, the objector's own reasoning leads to the conclusion that attributes become temporal in God's essence.

Key Terms
نسبة
تعلق
حادث
صفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only relations change, attributes remain eternal.
Correction: Even if relations change, the attribute (e.g., being a demander) would become temporal if the relation has real existence. The author shows that the objection leads to either denying the divine act or making attributes temporal.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the third possibility (that God cannot hear a sound before its existence) lead to?
A) It proves that God's hearing is eternal.
B) It entails the temporality of the attribute of hearing in God's essence.
C) It negates that God is a hearer altogether.
D) It shows that sounds have no real existence.

Key Takeaway
The objection that only relations are temporal fails because it leads to either denying God's actual commands or attributing temporality to His attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat God's commands as if they are temporary demands that apply only in certain circumstances, rather than real, eternal attributes of God? How would your life change if you truly believed that His being a demander of prayer and zakat is an unchanging reality, not a mere relation?

The objector argued that relations are temporal and separate from attributes. Are there areas of your faith where you separate God's attributes from His actions—believing that He is merciful in theory but not living as if His mercy is active in your daily struggles?
?
If God's hearing of your prayers occurs only at the moment you pray, would you pray differently? The lesson refutes that, affirming that His hearing is eternal. Does this certainty affect the urgency or sincerity of your supplications? Commit to praying with this conviction today.

All Groups Affirm Temporal Attributes in God
Surveys Mu'tazila and philosopher views and concludes all sects affirm temporal attributes in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how even the Mu'tazila and philosophers, despite their differing positions, ultimately affirm the temporality of certain attributes in God's essence.

Core Idea
All theological groups, knowingly or unknowingly, affirm temporal attributes in God.

This passage surveys the views of Mu'tazilite theologians and philosophers on whether attributes are temporal in God. The author shows that even those who explicitly deny change in God end up, through their own reasoning, implying that some attributes occur in time. This reveals a hidden consensus across seemingly opposing schools.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of arguments.
Notice the author uses the term 'حدث' (temporal occurrence) for attributes.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila positions
Find the three distinct Mu'tazilite figures mentioned: Abu Ali, Abu Hashim, and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri. Note how each one affirms temporality in God.
Abu Ali and Abu Hashim attribute temporality to will and perception; Abu al-Husayn extends it to knowledge.
3
Examine the philosophers' position
Understand how the philosophers' doctrine of relations as real attributes leads to temporal attributes in God. The author argues they say it 'without realizing it'.
Focus on the three relations: anteriority, accompaniment, posteriority.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the author's final claim: all groups affirm the temporality of attributes in God's essence. Ask yourself why this is significant.
This challenges the notion that only certain sects hold this view.
5
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question and try the activity to solidify your grasp.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعتزلة : فقد ذهب أبو علي وأبو هاشم إلى أنه يحدث في ذاته صفة المريدية والكرامية، ويحدث في ذاته كونه سامعاً مبصراً لهذه الأصوات الحادثة، ولهذه الألوان الحادثة . وأما أبو الحسين البصري فقد صرح بأن علم الله يتغير عند تغير المعلومات، وأن تلك العلوم إنما تحدث في ذات الله تعالى . وأما الفلاسفة : فإنهم مع كونهم أبعد الناس عن هذا المذهب ، قد قالوا به من حيث لم يشعروا به. وبيانه : وهو أن الإضافات صفات موجودة في الأعيان عندهم ، ولا شك أن الباري تعالى موجود مع كل حادث يحدث ويدخل في الوجود . ولا شك أنه كان موجودا قبل حدوث ذلك الحادث ، وسيبقى موجودا بعد فناء ذلك الحادث، وهذه القبلية والمعية والبعدية ، إضافات حادثة في ذات الله تعالى ، وإذا كانت الإضافات موجودات في الأعيان ، كان هذا قولا بحدوث المعاني والصفات ، وفي ذات الله تعالى . فيثبت بهذا البحث الذي ذكرناه : أن القول بحدوث الصفات في ذات الله قول قال به جميع الفرق .
As for the Mu'tazila: Abu Ali and Abu Hashim held that in His essence occurs the attribute of will (al-muridiyya), and in His essence occurs His being a hearer and seer of these temporal sounds and these temporal colors. As for Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, he explicitly stated that God's knowledge changes with the change of known things, and that these knowledges only occur in God's essence. As for the philosophers: although they are the furthest people from this doctrine, they have said it without realizing it. Its explanation: that relations are attributes existing in essences according to them, and there is no doubt that the Creator, exalted, exists with every temporal thing that occurs and enters into existence. And there is no doubt that He existed before the occurrence of that temporal thing, and will remain after the annihilation of that temporal thing. And these anteriority, accompaniment, and posteriority are temporal relations in God's essence. And if relations are existents in essences, then this is a statement of the temporality of meanings and attributes in God's essence. So it is established by this discussion we have mentioned: that the statement of the temporality of attributes in God's essence is a statement held by all groups.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage surveys the views of different groups on temporal attributes in God. It begins with the Mu'tazilites: Abu Ali al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i held that attributes like 'will' and 'hearing' and 'seeing' occur temporally in God's essence when the objects of these attributes come into existence. Then Abu al-Husayn al-Basri even held that God's knowledge itself changes with changing known things, implying new knowledges occur in God. Next, the philosophers (falasifa) are examined. Although they seem far from this doctrine, they unwittingly hold it because they consider relations to be real attributes existing in entities. God exists with every temporal event; He is before, with, and after that event. These relations are temporal in God. Since they consider relations as real attributes, this amounts to saying that new attributes occur in God. The passage concludes that all groups, despite their differences, end up affirming the temporality of some attributes in God's essence.

Correction: The passage shows that Mu'tazila and philosophers also affirm temporal attributes in God, though in different ways. The conclusion states all groups hold this view.

Threefold Classification of Attributes
Classifies attributes into three kinds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold classification of attributes: real without relations, real with relations, and pure relations.

Core Idea
Attributes divide into three kinds: intrinsic, connected, and purely relational.

This lesson introduces a foundational classification of attributes that is crucial for understanding later discussions about divine attributes. The author presents three categories: attributes that are real and have no relation to anything else, attributes that are real but involve relations, and attributes that are nothing but relations. This classification helps clarify which attributes can be temporal or eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice how the author begins with 'اعلم أن' (Know that) to assert the classification.
2
Identify the first category
Locate the first kind: real attributes devoid of relations. Note the examples given: color, taste, smell, existence, life.
These are intrinsic properties not depending on anything external.
3
Understand the second category
Find the second kind: real attributes that involve relations. The example is knowledge, which according to some has a connection to the known object.
Here the attribute itself is real, but it entails a relation.
4
Examine the third category
Study the third kind: pure relations and proportions. Examples: Zaid being to the right or left of someone, or becoming an uncle when his brother has a child.
These attributes are not real properties; they are purely relational and can change without any intrinsic change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن الصفات على ثلاثة أقسام : أحدها : الصفات الحقيقية العارية عن الإضافات ، مثل اللون والطعم والرائحة . ومثل : الوجود والحياة . وثانيها : الصفات الحقيقية الموصوفة بالإضافات والنسب وذلك مثل : العلم عند من يقول : إنه صفة لها تعلق بالمعلوم. فإن على هذا القول : العلم صفة حقيقية وحصل بين تلك الصفة وبين المعلوم نسبة خاصة ، وتعلق خاص . وثالثها : الصفات التي هي محض النسب والإضافات مثل : كون زيد يمينا لعمرو ، أو يسارا له ، مثل ما إذا تزوج أخو زيد بامرأة ، وحصل له ولد ، فإن زيدا يصير عماً لذلك الولد ، بعد أن كان عاريا عن هذه الصفة ، وهذا يقتضي حدوث هذه الإضافات المختصة في ذات زيد .
Know that the attributes are of three kinds: First: the real attributes devoid of relations, such as color, taste, and smell, and such as existence and life. Second: the real attributes qualified by relations and proportions, such as knowledge according to the one who says that it is an attribute that has a connection to the known. For on this view, knowledge is a real attribute, and between that attribute and the known there occurs a specific proportion and a specific connection. Third: the attributes that are pure proportions and relations, such as Zaid being to the right of 'Amr or to his left, like when Zaid's brother marries a woman and has a child, then Zaid becomes an uncle to that child after he was devoid of this attribute. And this entails the temporality of these specific relations in Zaid's essence.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a classification of attributes into three categories. The author introduces it with 'Know that attributes are of three kinds.' The first category is real attributes that are not related to anything else, like color, taste, smell, existence, and life. These are intrinsic and do not involve any relation. The second category is real attributes that do involve relations or proportions to other things. An example is knowledge, which, according to some, is a real attribute that has a specific relation to the known object. The third category is attributes that are pure relations and attributions, not rooted in a real property. Examples include Zaid being to the right or left of someone, or becoming an uncle when his brother has a child. These relations arise temporally when the relevant situation changes. This classification sets the stage for the subsequent discussion about which types of attributes can be temporal in God.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think all attributes are either real or relational, but the author distinguishes two types of real attributes based on whether they include relations.
Correction: Real attributes can be either devoid of relations (first kind) or qualified by relations (second kind). The third kind is purely relational, not real.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Attributes are of three types: real without relations, real with relations, and pure relations.

If it is merely relational, what does that imply about the state of your soul right now? Commit to one concrete act today that turns that relation into a real quality.
?
The attribute of knowledge is described as real yet tied to the known object. Consider your knowledge of God—is it a real, living conviction that changes you, or is it just information you possess? What specific belief about God must you stop merely knowing and start embodying? Write it down and recite it in your next prayer.
?
If 'existence' is a real attribute devoid of relations, then your very existence is a gift directly from God, not dependent on any circumstance. When you feel insignificant or defined by your roles (parent, worker, etc.), remember that your existence itself is a real attribute from Him. The Precise Locus of Disagreement on Temporal Attributes
States that relational attributes are agreed to be temporal, and real attributes are the point of disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise point of disagreement among theologians regarding temporal attributes in God.

Core Idea
The real debate is about real attributes, not relational ones.

After explaining the classification of attributes, the author now pinpoints exactly where scholars disagree. He shows that everyone agrees relational attributes can be temporal, but the real dispute is whether real attributes can also be temporal in God's essence. This lesson helps you focus on the core issue.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the two parts: what is agreed upon and what is disputed.
The passage is short but dense; each sentence carries weight.
2
Identify the agreed part
The author states that the temporality of relational attributes is a matter that must be acknowledged and cannot be denied. Understand why relational attributes (like being to the right) are not controversial.
Relational attributes do not affect the essence; they are merely relative.
3
Locate the point of disagreement
The author says 'here is the point of disagreement' and presents two opposing views on the temporality of real attributes. Some allow it; some forbid it. This is the real locus of debate.
The phrase 'ههنا محل الخلاف' is key.

This passage clarifies the exact point of disagreement among theologians regarding temporal attributes. However, the dispute is about the real attributes, whether they can be temporal in God's essence. The First Proof: Receptivity to Accidents Implies Temporality
Begins the first proof that God's essence cannot be receptive to temporal accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the first proof used by kalam theologians to deny that temporal accidents can subsist in God's essence.

Core Idea
Receptivity to temporal accidents necessarily makes a being temporal.

This passage introduces one of the most famous arguments in kalam: that anything that can receive temporal accidents must itself be temporal. The argument is a simple syllogism, but its implications for the nature of God are profound. In this lesson, we will break down the proof step by step and understand why it is considered decisive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that the proof is presented as a conditional syllogism with two premises and a conclusion.
The passage is incomplete; the full conclusion is implied.
2
Identify the two premises and the conclusion
Find the statements: 'Every receptive entity is not free from accidents' and 'What is not free from accidents is temporal.' The conclusion is that every receptive entity is temporal.
3
Understand the logical flow
See how the first premise is defended: the essence that accepts accidents cannot separate from that receptivity, thus it always has some accident and is temporal.
4
Consider the missing part
The passage cuts off; the complete argument would conclude that God, being eternal, cannot be receptive to accidents.
5
Reflect on the significance
Why does this argument matter? It establishes that God's essence cannot undergo change, preserving divine immutability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج القائلون بامتناع قيام الحوادث بذات الله تعالى بوجوه : منه الحجة الأولى : أن نقول : كل ما كان قابلا للحوادث ، فإنه لا يخلو عنها ، وما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث . أما قولنا : إن كل ما كان قابلا للحوادث ، فإنه لا يخلو عنها . فالدليل عليه : أن نقول : إن تلك الذات التي لا يمكن خلوها عن قابليه الحوادث [ حادثة . فيلزم أن يقال : كل ما كان قابلا للحوادث ، فإنه لا ينفك عن الحوادث ] وأما بيان أن كل ذات تقبل الحوادث فإنها لا تنفك عن تلك القابلية
And those who say that it is impossible for temporal accidents to subsist in God's essence argued with several proofs. Among them is the first proof: that we say: Everything that is receptive to temporal accidents is not free from them, and whatever is not free from temporal accidents is temporal. As for our statement: 'Everything that is receptive to temporal accidents is not free from them' — the proof for it is: that we say: That essence which cannot be free from the receptivity of temporal accidents [is temporal. So it follows that everything receptive to temporal accidents is not free from them.] And as for the clarification that every essence that accepts temporal accidents does not separate from that receptivity...
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof used by those who deny that temporal accidents can occur in God's essence. The proof is structured as a conditional syllogism: (1) Any entity that can receive temporal accidents (i.e., is 'receptive') is never devoid of them. (2) Whatever is never devoid of temporal accidents is itself temporal. The conclusion is that any entity that can receive temporal accidents is temporal. Therefore, if God were receptive to temporal accidents, He would be temporal, which is impossible. The author then provides a proof for the first premise: an essence that cannot be free from the receptivity of accidents is itself temporal. However, the passage cuts off before completing the clarification. The argument relies on the principle that a substrate that can receive accidents must always have some accidents, and thus cannot be eternal, because an eternal being would not be subject to change.

Key Terms
قابل للحوادث
لا يخلو عن الحوادث
حادث
حجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is circular because it assumes that having accidents makes something temporal, which is exactly what it tries to prove.
Correction: The argument does not assume circularity; it uses two separate premises: (1) receptivity implies non-emptiness, and (2) non-emptiness implies temporality. These are distinct kalam principles based on the nature of change and origination.

Checkpoint
What is the first premise of the first proof?
A) Everything that is receptive to accidents is immutable.
B) Everything that is receptive to accidents is not free from them.
C) Everything that is temporal is receptive to accidents.
D) God is not receptive to accidents.

Key Takeaway
The first proof establishes that any being receptive to change must itself be subject to change, and thus cannot be eternal; therefore, God's essence cannot be a substrate for temporal accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If only that which never changes is truly eternal, and only the Eternal is worthy of your ultimate trust, then in what changing thing have you placed your trust that you need to withdraw today?
?
This proof shows that any being subject to change cannot be God. How does your own experience of constant change remind you that you are not your own lord, and how will you let that awareness drive you to constant dependence on the Unchanging One?
?
The argument equates receptivity to accidents with temporality. In your spiritual life, are you receptive to the 'accidents' of trials and blessings as opportunities to turn to the Eternal? Commit to one specific way this week you will use a change in your circumstances to remember your contingency and seek God.

Classification of Attributes into Three Kinds
The author classifies attributes into three kinds and identifies the locus of disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of attributes—real without relations, real with relations, and purely relational—and how this classification frames the theological dispute about temporal attributes in God.

Core Idea
Attributes are either real, real-with-relations, or purely relational.

The author begins by establishing a crucial classification of attributes. Before we can discuss whether attributes can change or occur in God, we need a clear taxonomy. This passage provides that, and then points to the heart of the theological disagreement: whether temporal relational attributes can be ascribed to God's essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of attributes
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview. Then read the translation. Notice the three types of attributes the author presents.
The passage uses examples like color, knowledge, and being an uncle.
2
Understand each type with its examples
Identify each of the three types: real attributes without relations (e.g., color, taste, existence), real attributes with relations (e.g., knowledge as having a connection to the known), and purely relational attributes (e.g., being to the right or becoming an uncle).
3
Notice the transition to the dispute
The author asserts that relational attributes are undeniably temporal. This is a key premise. Then he states the actual disagreement: some allow such temporal attributes in God, others forbid it.
The phrase 'ههنا محل الخلاف' marks the pivot.
4
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this classification matters. It separates what everyone agrees is created (relational attributes) from what might be debated (whether God can have temporal relational attributes).

Source Text

The author then explains that the temporality of relational attributes is undeniable, but the real dispute is whether such temporal attributes can occur in God's essence. The Second Proof: Originated Attribute Dilemma
The author argues that any originated attribute in God must be either a perfection or not, leading to impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why any originated attribute in God leads to an impossibility, whether it is perfect or not.

Core Idea
An originated attribute in God is impossible in both cases.

This passage presents the second proof against originated attributes in God. The author uses a simple dichotomy: any supposed originated attribute must be either a perfection or not. Both options are shown to be impossible, either because it would imply prior deficiency or because it contradicts the consensus that all divine attributes are perfect.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the dichotomy and the two branches.
2
Analyze the first case
If the attribute is perfection, consider: before it originated, God lacked it. Lack of perfection is deficiency, which is impossible for God.
3
Analyze the second case
If the attribute is not perfection, then affirming it for God contradicts the consensus that all divine attributes are perfect. Hence impossible.
4
See the logical conclusion
Both branches lead to impossibility, so the supposition of an originated attribute in God is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية في المسألة: إن الصفة التي حدثت في ذات الله تعالى، إما أن تكون من صفات الكمال ، وإما أن لا تكون من صفات الكمال ، فإن كانت من صفات الكمال ، كانت تلك الذات قبل حدوث تلك الصفة فيها خالية عن صفة الكمال ، والخلو عن صفة الكمال نقصان فيلزم كون تلك الذات ناقصة . والنقصان على الله محال . وإن كانت تلك الصفة ليست من صفات الكمال ، كان إثباتها في حق الله تعالى حالا لحصول الاتفاق على أن صفات الله تعالى بأسرها يجب أن تكون من صفات الكمال والمدح .
The second proof regarding the issue: The attribute that originated in the essence of God, exalted is He, is either from the attributes of perfection, or it is not from the attributes of perfection. If it is from the attributes of perfection, then that essence before the occurrence of that attribute in it is devoid of the attribute of perfection, and being devoid of the attribute of perfection is deficiency, so it follows that that essence is deficient. And deficiency is impossible for God. And if that attribute is not from the attributes of perfection, then its affirmation with respect to God, exalted is He, is impossible because of the agreement that all of God's attributes, exalted is He, must be attributes of perfection and praise.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against the possibility of an originated attribute in God. The author uses a simple dichotomy: any hypothetical originated attribute in God must be either a perfection or a non-perfection. If it is a perfection, then God would have lacked that perfection before it occurred, implying deficiency in His essence, which is impossible. If it is not a perfection, then affirming it for God would contradict the consensus that all of God's attributes must be perfect and praiseworthy. In either case, the supposition leads to an impossibility, so God cannot have originated attributes.

Key Terms
صفات الكمال (sifāt al-kamāl) - attributes of perfection
نقصان (nuqṣān) - deficiency
محال (muḥāl) - impossible

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the proof only covers the case of perfection and ignores non-perfection.
Correction: The proof also covers non-perfection by appealing to the consensus that all divine attributes are perfect, making non-perfect attributes impossible.

Checkpoint

B) God would have to create it.
C) It would be eternal.
D) It would be a contingent attribute.

Key Takeaway
Any attribute that arises in God, whether perfect or not, is impossible; therefore, God's attributes are eternal and unchanging.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Third Proof: The Essence Would Depend on an External Cause for the Attribute
A logical proof against originated attributes in God's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that originated attributes cannot subsist in God's essence because doing so would entail dependence on an external cause, contradicting His necessary existence.

Core Idea
If the divine essence is insufficient to determine an attribute, an external cause is required, making God contingent.

In this lesson, we examine the third proof presented by the author to demonstrate that God's essence cannot be the locus of originated attributes. The proof argues by analyzing the relationship between the essence and any conceivable attribute: either the essence is sufficient to cause the attribute or its absence, or it is not. The third scenario leads to an unacceptable conclusion that would make the Necessary Being dependent on something else, which is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
The intellect considers any attribute, and the divine essence is either sufficient to necessitate its existence, sufficient to necessitate its non-existence, or sufficient for neither. This sets up three exhaustive cases.
Notice that the proof covers every possibility.
2
Examine the first two cases
If the essence is sufficient for existence, the attribute is perpetual like the essence. If sufficient for non-existence, the absence is perpetual. Both cases yield eternal attributes (or their negation).
In these two cases, no external cause is needed.
3
Focus on the third case
If the essence is not sufficient for either, then the state (existence or non-existence) of the attribute must be determined by an external cause because the essence cannot be in both states simultaneously.
The proof then shows that this leads to dependence.
4
Follow the logical chain to impossibility
The essence is dependent on one of the two states, each state is dependent on an external cause, so the essence is dependent on an external cause. That which depends on another is contingent, making the Necessary Being contingent—an impossibility.
This contradiction proves the original assumption false.
5
Conclude about God's attributes
Therefore, God's essence cannot be the locus of originated attributes. Just as His essence is eternal, His attributes are eternal, without beginning or end.
This affirms the doctrine of eternal attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : كل صفة يشير العقل إليها ، فإما أن تكون ذات الله كافية في استلزام حصولها ، أو كانت كافية في استلزام عدمها ، أو لا تكون كافية في واحد منهما ، فإن كان الأول لزم دوام وجود تلك الصفة بسبب دوام تلك الذات ، وإن كان الثاني لزم دوام عدم تلك الصفة بسبب دوام تلك الذات وإن كان الثالث فحينئذ لا تكون تلك الذات كافية لا في وجود تلك الصفة ولا في عدمها . معلوم : أن تلك الذات لا تنفك عن وجود تلك الصفة وعن عدمها . ونقول : الذات موقوفة على حصول أحد هذين القسمين ، إما وجود تلك الصفة وإما عدمها ، لكن وجودها وعدمها لما لم يكف فيه تلك الذات ، فلا بد فيه من سبب منفصل فتكون تلك الذات موقوفة على أحد هذين القسمين ، وكل واحد منهما موقوف على سبب منفصل، والموقوف على الموقوف على الشيء ، يجب كونه موقوفا على الشيء ، فيلزم كون ذات الله تعالى موقوفة على سبب منفصل ، والموقوف على الغير ممكن لذاته ، فواجب الوجود لذاته ممكن الوجود لذاته ، وذلك محال . فيثبت : أن ذات الله تعالى لا تقبل الصفات الحادثة ، وأنه كما أن ذاته دائم لم يزل ولا يزال ، فكذلك صفاته دائمة لم تزل ولا تزال .
The third proof: Every attribute to which the intellect points is such that either the essence of God is sufficient to necessitate its existence, or it is sufficient to necessitate its non-existence, or it is not sufficient for either. If the first, then the attribute must be perpetual due to the perpetuity of that essence. If the second, then the non-existence of the attribute must be perpetual due to the perpetuity of that essence. If the third, then that essence is not sufficient for either the existence of that attribute or its non-existence. It is known that that essence cannot be free from either the existence of that attribute or its non-existence. And we say: the essence is dependent on the occurrence of one of these two divisions, either the existence of that attribute or its non-existence. But since its existence and non-existence are not sufficiently determined by that essence, there must be an external cause. Thus that essence becomes dependent on one of these two divisions, and each of them is dependent on an external cause. And that which is dependent on that which is dependent on something must itself be dependent on that something. So it follows that the essence of God, exalted is He, is dependent on an external cause. And that which is dependent on another is possible in itself. So that which is necessary of existence in itself would be possible of existence in itself, and that is impossible. Therefore it is established that the essence of God, exalted is He, does not accept originated attributes, and that just as His essence is eternal, without beginning and without end, so too His attributes are eternal, without beginning and without end.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This third proof examines every conceivable attribute that the mind might posit for God. The author divides the possibilities: either the divine essence is sufficient to necessitate the attribute, or to necessitate its absence, or it is not sufficient for either. If it is sufficient for existence, the attribute must be perpetual because the essence is perpetual. If it is sufficient for non-existence, the absence is perpetual. If it is not sufficient, then the essence is not the cause of the attribute's presence or absence. Since the essence must be in one of the two states (either having the attribute or not), but cannot determine which, it becomes dependent on an external cause. This leads to a chain of dependencies, ultimately meaning that God's essence would be dependent on something external, implying that He is contingent (mumkin) in Himself, which contradicts His being necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd). Therefore, God cannot be subject to originated attributes; His attributes, like His essence, are eternal.

Key Terms
كافية (kāfiya) - sufficient
استلزام (istilzām) - necessitation
سبب منفصل (sabab munfaṣil) - external cause
موقوف (mawqūf) - dependent
واجب الوجود (wājib al-wujūd) - necessary of existence
ممكن (mumkin) - possible/contingent

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that this proof denies that God can cause attributes in creatures or that God's attributes are separate entities.
Correction: The proof only addresses attributes subsisting in God's essence. It does not deny that God can cause attributes in creatures, nor does it claim God's attributes are separate from His essence; rather, it affirms that if any attribute were to inhere in His essence, it would have to be eternal, not originated.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, if the divine essence is not sufficient for either the existence or non-existence of an attribute, what follows?
A) The attribute is impossible.
B) The essence must be dependent on an external cause.
C) The attribute is eternal.
D) The non-existence is necessary.

Key Takeaway
The third proof establishes that God's essence cannot be the locus of originated attributes because that would imply dependence on an external cause, contradicting His necessary existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes like His anger or mercy, do you unconsciously imagine them fluctuating based on your actions? How does the truth that they are eternal and unchanging challenge your sense of security or fear before Him?
?
This proof demands that you not conceive of God as having 'moods' or emotional reactions. But when you sin, do you feel that God's attitude toward you changes? How can you reconcile that feeling with the fact that His attributes—including His forgiveness and justice—are eternal and never originate?
?
If God's essence is sufficient for all His attributes, then nothing external—including your prayer—can cause a new attribute in Him. Does that make your worship feel pointless? Or does it transform worship into something deeper, a response to what He already is eternally?

The Second Proof: Perfection vs. Imperfection
Second proof: a temporal attribute would be either a perfection or not, both impossible for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof against temporal attributes in God, which uses a dichotomy of perfection and deficiency to show impossibility.

Core Idea
Temporal attributes require either deficiency or imperfection, both impossible for God.

This lesson presents the second proof from the author, a clear disjunctive argument. The key move is to consider whether a temporal attribute would be a perfection or not. Both possibilities lead to an impossibility, so such attributes cannot be ascribed to God. Pay close attention to how the argument covers all cases.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: it starts with a dichotomy, then examines each branch.
The argument is concise but powerful.
2
Understand the first branch
If the attribute is a perfection, then before it occurred God lacked it — that lack is deficiency, which is impossible for God.
Deficiency (nuqṣān) is the opposite of perfection.
3
Understand the second branch
If the attribute is not a perfection, then it cannot be a divine attribute because all divine attributes must be perfections.
This is based on consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية في المسألة: إن الصفة التي حدثت في ذات الله تعالى، إما أن تكون من صفات الكمال ، وإما أن لا تكون من صفات الكمال ، فإن كانت من صفات الكمال ، كانت تلك الذات قبل حدوث تلك الصفة فيها خالية عن صفة الكمال ، والخلو عن صفة الكمال نقصان فيلزم كون تلك الذات ناقصة . والنقصان على الله محال . وإن كانت تلك الصفة ليست من صفات الكمال ، كان إثباتها في حق الله تعالى محالا، لحصول الاتفاق على أن صفات الله تعالى بأسرها يجب أن تكون من صفات الكمال والمدح .
The second proof in the issue: The attribute that occurs in the essence of God, exalted, must either be from the attributes of perfection (ṣifāt al-kamāl) or not be from the attributes of perfection. If it is from the attributes of perfection, then that essence before the occurrence of that attribute in it was devoid of the attribute of perfection, and being devoid of the attribute of perfection is deficiency, so that essence would be deficient. And deficiency is impossible for God. And if that attribute is not from the attributes of perfection, then its affirmation with respect to God, exalted, is impossible, due to the consensus that all of God's attributes must be attributes of perfection and praise.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof, which is a disjunctive argument. The assumption is that a temporal attribute in God would either be a perfection (kamāl) or not. If it is a perfection, then before it occurred, God would have been devoid of that perfection, which is a deficiency (nuqṣān). Deficiency is impossible for God. If it is not a perfection, then ascribing it to God is impossible because there is consensus that all divine attributes must be perfections and praiseworthy. Therefore, temporal attributes cannot be ascribed to God.

Key Terms
صفات الكمال
نقصان
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second proof only applies to attributes that are obviously perfections, not to all temporal attributes.
Correction: The proof is exhaustive because the dichotomy of perfection vs. non-perfection covers every possible attribute. If it's not a perfection, it's automatically excluded.

Checkpoint
What is the main dichotomy in the second proof?
A) The attribute is either essential or accidental.
B) The attribute is either a perfection or not a perfection.
C) The attribute is either eternal or temporal.
D) The attribute is either necessary or contingent.

Key Takeaway
Temporal attributes are impossible because they force either a deficiency in God or an imperfection, both of which are incompatible with divine perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you find yourself believing that God could gain a new attribute over time, does that mean you believe He was once less than perfect? Examine your own heart: are you comfortable with the thought that your Lord might have been incomplete at some point? What does that imply about your understanding of His absolute perfection? Commit to affirming that God is perfect and does not change.
?
You have learned that any temporal attribute in God would force either deficiency or imperfection. When you reflect on your own life, do you ever attribute to God human-like changes in His nature, even unintentionally? How does this proof challenge your assumptions? Will you actively guard against such thoughts and affirm God's timeless perfection?
?
The proof relies on the consensus that all divine attributes must be perfections. Have you ever considered an attribute of God that you thought might be less than perfect, such as His wrath or justice? How does this lesson correct that misconception? Take a moment to realign your heart with the truth that every divine attribute is one of perfection and praise.

The Argument from God's Knowledge of Changing Particulars
First argument for temporal attributes from knowledge of particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument for temporal attributes based on God's knowledge of particulars and its implication.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of changing things requires change in His essence.

This lesson presents the first of three proofs cited by those who affirm temporal attributes in God. It argues that since God knows particulars that change, His knowledge must also change, implying a temporal event in the Divine essence. Understanding this argument helps clarify a major point of contention in classical kalām.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the argument is structured: a premise (God knows particulars) leads to a conclusion (knowledge changes, hence temporal).
The passage is part of a larger discussion; focus on this specific inference.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument assumes that knowledge must change when the known object changes. Consider why this premise might be accepted or challenged.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that temporal knowledges occur in God's essence. Ask yourself: does this necessarily follow? What alternatives might exist?
This argument is often debated; the lesson aims to present it clearly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القائلون بحدوث الصفات فقد بنوا كلامهم على مسائل : أحدها : أنه تعالى عالم بالجزئيات ، والعلم يجب تغيره عند تغير المعلوم ، وهذا يقتضي حدوث العلوم في ذات الله. وثانيها : أنه تعالى مريد وثبت أن القصد إلى إحداث الشيء ، لا يحصل إلا حال الإحداث ، وهذا يقتضي حدوث الإرادة لكن الإرادة والمريدية صفة الله فيلزم حدوث هذه الصفة في ذات الله . وثالثها : إنه تعالى سميع بصير ، وسماع الكلام قبل حدوثه محال ، وإبصار الصورة قبل حدوثها محال فيثبت : أن ذلك السماع إنما حدث عند حدوث ذلك الصوت ، وأن ذلك الإبصار إنما حدث عند حدوث ذلك المرئي. وأما الدلائل الدالة على أنه لا يحدث في ذات الله تعالى شيء ، فهي بأسرها مشكلة الصفات الإضافية . فإنا قد بينا : أنه لابد من الاعتراف بحدوثها ، مع أن الدلائل التي ذكرتموها قائمة فيه . [ وهذا آخر هذه المسألة ، والله أعلم (۱) ]
As for those who affirm the temporal origination of attributes, they have based their discourse on issues: First: He, exalted, is knower of particulars, and knowledge must change when the known changes, and this necessitates the temporal origination of knowledges in the essence of God. Second: He, exalted, is willing, and it is established that intention to bring something into existence only occurs at the time of bringing it into existence, and this necessitates the temporal origination of will, but will and being willing are attributes of God, so the temporal origination of this attribute in the essence of God follows. Third: He, exalted, is hearing and seeing. Hearing speech before its occurrence is impossible, and seeing a form before its occurrence is impossible. So it is established that that hearing only occurs at the occurrence of that sound, and that that seeing only occurs at the occurrence of that visible object. As for the proofs that nothing occurs in the essence of God, exalted, they are entirely problematic with respect to relational attributes (al-ṣifāt al-iḍāfiyya). For we have already shown that it is necessary to acknowledge their occurrence, while the proofs that you have mentioned stand concerning it. [This is the end of this issue, and God knows best.]
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents the case of those who affirm temporal attributes (al-qa'ilun bi-huduth al-sifat). They base their argument on three issues: (1) God is knower of particulars; knowledge must change when the known changes, implying temporal knowledge in God. (2) God is willing; the intention to create something occurs only at the time of creation, implying a temporal will. (3) God is hearing and seeing; hearing a sound before it occurs is impossible, so hearing occurs at the moment of the sound, and similarly for seeing. The author then remarks: The proofs that nothing occurs in God's essence are entirely problematic with regard to relational attributes (sifat idafiyya). For we have shown that we must acknowledge their temporal occurrence, while the proofs you (the opponents of temporal attributes) have presented still stand concerning it. This is a concluding note that the issue revolves around relational attributes, and the previous proofs might not apply to them.

Key Terms
علم بالجزئيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the first argument claim about God's knowledge?
A) It is eternal and unchanging.
B) It must change with changing particulars.
C) It is limited to universals.
D) It does not involve particulars.

Key Takeaway
The first argument for temporal attributes reasons that God's knowledge of changing particulars necessitates change in the divine essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your life takes an unexpected turn, do you believe God's knowledge of you shifts in that moment? If so, does that make God feel more intimately aware of your circumstances, or does it challenge your belief in His unchanging nature? How will you sit with this tension in your next prayer?
?
If God's knowledge of your changing states means new ‘knowledges’ arise in His essence, does that make you feel more personally accountable for each moment? How can you live today knowing that every change in your condition is reflected in divine knowledge?

The Argument from God's Will and Temporal Intention
Second argument for temporal attributes from will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument for temporal attributes based on God's will and the timing of intention.

Core Idea
God's will to create occurs only at the moment of creation, making it temporal.

This lesson covers the second of three arguments for temporal attributes. It focuses on God's attribute of will (irada). The argument holds that intention to bring something into existence can only occur contemporaneously with the act, implying a temporal will in God. This challenges the classical view of an eternal will.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that the argument includes two steps: first, that intention occurs only at the time of bringing into existence; second, that this implies temporal origination of will in God.
2
Analyze the logic
Identify the connection between 'intention' (qasd) and 'huduth' (temporal origination). Why does the argument conclude that will itself becomes temporal?
3
Consider the implications
If God's will is temporal, then His attribute of 'being willing' (al-muridiyya) becomes incidental. Reflect on how this might challenge divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

A) Eternally before creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God’s intention to create only happens at the moment of creation, what does that mean for your daily prayers? Are you speaking to a God whose will is already fixed, or one who responds to your present moment with fresh intention? How does that change the urgency with which you plead with Him?
?
This argument says God’s will is temporal—it occurs in time. How does that affect your understanding of your own sin and repentance? If God’s will to forgive is not eternally static but temporally directed at your specific moment of turning, does that not compel you to turn now rather than later?
?
The passage says intention to bring something into existence occurs only at the time of bringing it into existence. Reflect: In what area of your life are you waiting for God to act, assuming His will is already settled? What if His will to act in your situation is dependent on the moment you actually lift your hands to Him? Would your diligence in supplication change?

The Argument from Hearing/Seeing and Relational Attributes
Third argument from hearing/seeing and conclusion on relational attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument for temporal attributes from hearing and seeing, and the concluding point about relational attributes.

Core Idea
God's hearing and seeing occur only when their objects exist, implying temporal attributes.

This lesson presents the third argument for temporal attributes, which focuses on God's hearing (sam') and seeing (basar). It argues that these perceptions are impossible before the existence of their objects, so they must occur temporally. The lesson also covers the author's concluding note that the proofs against temporal attributes fail regarding relational attributes (sifat idafiyya). This highlights a nuanced position in the debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the third argument and the concluding remark. Note how the argument parallels the previous ones: a premise about impossibility leads to a conclusion of temporal occurrence.
2
Understand the concluding note
The author acknowledges that the proofs against temporal attributes are problematic for relational attributes. Consider why relational attributes might be a special case.
3
Reflect on the overall debate
This lesson ends the discussion of the three arguments. Think about how these arguments collectively challenge the doctrine of eternal attributes.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument applies to all attributes equally.
Correction: The author distinguishes relational attributes (sifat idafiyya) as a category where temporal occurrence is admitted.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the proofs against temporal attributes regarding relational attributes?
A) They are completely valid and refute the arguments.
B) They are problematic and temporal origination must be acknowledged.
C) They are irrelevant.
D) They apply only to God's essence.

Key Takeaway
The third argument and concluding note show that hearing and seeing are temporal, and that relational attributes present a special challenge to the doctrine of immutable divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's hearing of your prayer occurs only when you pray, are you consistent in your supplication? Does this truth change how you approach du'a? Commit to a specific time this week to call upon Him with the awareness that He hears at that moment.
?
The author concedes that relational attributes are temporal. How does that affect your understanding of God's constant attributes? Are you comfortable with a God whose hearing 'occurs' in time? Examine your heart: do you truly believe God hears your specific situation now, or do you intellectually accept it? Resolve to act on that belief today.
?
The argument shows that God's seeing is tied to the visible object. If God sees your actions only when they happen, does this motivate you to be more conscious of His sight in every moment? Identify one action you will perform today with the explicit intention that God is seeing it at that instant.

The Objection to Change in God and Its Refutation
Al-Razi refutes an objection against change in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi refutes the objection that change in God is impossible by showing it is either self-referential or irrelevant.

Core Idea
The objection to change in God fails because it either begs the question or misses the point.

This passage presents an objection against the possibility of change in God and Al-Razi's refutation. The objector argues that if change means a new attribute arising in God, it is self-referential; if it means alteration of the essence, it is not necessary. Al-Razi dismisses this as weak reasoning. Understanding this dialectic is essential before exploring arguments for temporal attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the gist of the argument.
Pay attention to the two conditional phrases beginning with 'إن عنيتم'.
2
Identify the two interpretations of 'change'
The objector offers two meanings of 'change': (1) occurrence of a new attribute after non-existence, (2) alteration of the essence itself. Note how Al-Razi responds to each.
The first interpretation leads to a self-referential claim; the second is unnecessary.
3
Understand why the objection is weak
Al-Razi concludes that the objection is weak because it either imposes a self-referential condition or fails to follow logically. Reflect on why this refutation is important for the discussion of divine attributes.
This refutation clears the way for the positive arguments for temporal attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوع التغير وذلك محال بالاتفاق ، فوجب أن يكون حدوث تلك الصفة في ذات الله تعالى محالا . ولقائل أن يقول : إن عنيتم بهذا التغير حدوث صفة في ذات الله تعالى ، بعد عدمها . فهذا يفيد إلزام الشيء على نفسه ، وذلك لا يفيد ، وإن عنيتم به وقوع التبدل في نفس تلك الذات المخصوصة ، فمعلوم أن هذا غير لازم . فيثبت أن هذا الكلام ضعيف . وأما القائلون بحدوث الصفات فقد بنوا كلامهم على مسائل : أحدها : أنه تعالى عالم بالجزئيات ، والعلم يجب تغيره عند تغير المعلوم ، وهذا يقتضي حدوث العلوم في ذات الله. وثانيها : أنه تعالى مريد وثبت أن القصد إلى إحداث الشيء ، لا يحصل إلا حال الإحداث ، وهذا يقتضي حدوث الإرادة لكن الإرادة والمريدية صفة الله فيلزم حدوث هذه الصفة في ذات الله . وثالثها : إنه تعالى سميع بصير ، وسماع الكلام قبل حدوثه محال ، وإبصار الصورة قبل حدوثها محال فيثبت : أن ذلك السماع إنما حدث عند حدوث ذلك الصوت ، وأن ذلك الإبصار إنما حدث عند حدوث ذلك المرئي. وأما الدلائل الدالة على أنه لا يحدث في ذات الله تعالى شيء ، فهي بأسرها مشكلة الصفات الإضافية . فإنا قد بينا : أنه لابد من الاعتراف بحدوثها ، مع أن الدلائل التي ذكرتموها قائمة فيه . وهذا آخر هذه المسألة ، والله أعلم (۱) (۱) من (و)
The occurrence of change, and that is impossible by consensus, so it must be that the occurrence of that attribute in the essence of God, may He be exalted, is impossible. And someone may say: If you mean by this change the occurrence of an attribute in the essence of God, after its non-existence, then this entails imposing the thing upon itself, and that is not beneficial; and if you mean by it the occurrence of alteration in that specific essence itself, then it is known that this is not necessary. So it is established that this speech is weak. As for those who affirm the temporality of attributes, they have based their speech on issues: One: that He, exalted, is knower of particulars, and knowledge must change when the known changes, and this necessitates the temporality of knowledges in the essence of God. Second: that He, exalted, is willer, and it is established that the intention to bring about a thing does not occur except at the moment of its bringing about, and this necessitates the temporality of will, but will and being-willing are an attribute of God, so it necessitates the temporality of this attribute in the essence of God. Third: that He, exalted, is hearing and seeing, and hearing speech before its occurrence is impossible, and seeing an image before its occurrence is impossible, so it is established that that hearing only occurred at the occurrence of that sound, and that seeing only occurred at the occurrence of that visible thing. As for the proofs that indicate that nothing occurs in the essence of God, exalted, they are all problematic with respect to relational attributes. For we have explained that it is necessary to acknowledge their temporality, even though the proofs that you have mentioned are valid concerning it. And this is the end of this issue, and God knows best. (1) From (w).
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection and a presentation of three arguments by those who claim attributes are temporal, with a concluding remark about relational attributes. The author first states that change in God is impossible by consensus. Then an objector says that if by 'change' you mean the occurrence of a new attribute in God's essence after its non-existence, that is merely self-referential and proves nothing; if you mean a transformation of the essence itself, that is not necessary. Thus the objection fails. Then the author presents the three arguments of those who affirm temporal attributes: (1) God's knowledge of particulars requires that His knowledge change as the known changes, implying that multiple knowledges occur in God, which are temporal. (2) God's will as a purposeful inclination occurs only at the moment of creation, implying that the attribute of will is temporal. (3) God's hearing of speech only occurs at the moment the sound exists, and His seeing of an image only at the moment it exists, so these are temporal. Finally, the author notes that the proofs claiming nothing occurs in God's essence are all problematic concerning relational attributes (al-sifat al-idafiyya), and they have already explained that these relational attributes must be acknowledged as temporal, even though the proofs against occurrence in God are valid in other respects. This concludes the issue.

Key Terms
تغير
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objector's argument is a strong refutation of temporal attributes.
Correction: Al-Razi shows the objector's argument is weak because it either imposes a self-referential condition or does not logically follow.

Checkpoint
Why does Al-Razi consider the objector's argument weak?
A) Because change in God is impossible by consensus.
B) Because the objector's interpretations lead to either a self-referential claim or an unnecessary implication.
C) Because the objector denies the occurrence of attributes.
D) Because Al-Razi has a better argument.

Key Takeaway
The objection that change in God is impossible is refuted because it either imposes a self-referential requirement or does not logically follow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Has there been a belief you held so firmly that you refused to examine the objections against it? What would it take for you to honestly consider a challenge to that belief, as this lesson models?
?
When you think of God's attributes like mercy or anger, do you find it difficult to reconcile them with His changelessness? How does the distinction in this passage between the essence and the occurrence of attributes help you think differently?
?
If God's essence does not change, what does that mean for the trustworthiness of His promises and warnings in the Quran? Are you living as though that changelessness guarantees His word?

Three Arguments for Temporality of Attributes
Al-Razi enumerates three arguments for temporal attributes and notes the problem of relational attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three compelling arguments for the temporality of God's attributes: knowledge, will, hearing/seeing.

Core Idea
God's knowledge, will, hearing, and seeing are relational and thus temporal.

Having refuted the objection, Al-Razi now presents the positive arguments of those who affirm temporal attributes. These are based on the relational nature of God's knowledge of particulars, His will to create, and His perception of temporal events. Each argument demands that God's attributes arise at the moment of their object's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the three arguments
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Identify the three distinct arguments: knowledge, will, and hearing/seeing.
Each argument begins with 'أحدها', 'وثانيها', 'وثالثها'.
2
Understand the reasoning for each
For each argument, note the premise about the attribute's relation to its object and the conclusion that the attribute must be temporal.
Knowledge changes with the known; will occurs at the moment of creation; hearing/seeing occur at the moment of sound/image.
3
Reflect on the concluding remark
Al-Razi notes that proofs against temporal attributes are problematic for relational attributes (al-sifat al-idafiyya). Consider why relational attributes are a special case.
Relational attributes depend on created things, so they cannot be eternally unchanging.

?

?

?

Intellectual Pleasures: The Philosophers' Proof
Razi introduces intellectual pleasure and presents the philosophers' proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument for intellectual pleasure in Allah and the point of contention with the theologians.

Core Idea
Allah's own knowledge of His perfection produces joy—so says the philosopher.

This passage marks a major fault line between philosophers and theologians: can Allah experience intellectual delight? The philosophers say yes, and they back it with a simple but profound proof. As you read, pay attention to how they derive divine pleasure from divine self-knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms: لذات عقلية, بهجة, التذاذ.
The passage is incomplete—the theologian's rebuttal is on a missing page.
2
Identify the three components
Find where Razi defines intellectual pleasure, states the disagreement, and gives the philosophers' proof. Each is a distinct part.
3
Analyze the proof's logic
The proof is: Allah knows His perfect attributes → this knowledge necessitates joy → therefore joy exists in Allah. Walk through each step.
This is a causal argument from knowledge to affect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما اللذات العقلية : فهي مثل الالتذاذ بحصول صفات الكمال والجلال له . وهذا النوع من الالتذاذ قد أطبقت الفلاسفة على إثباته في حق الله تعالى . والمتكلمون أطبقوا على إنكاره . أما الفلاسفة فقد احتجوا على إثباته بأنه تعالى عالم بكونه موصوفا بصفات الكمال والجلال ، وهذا العلم يوجب البهجة والالتذاذ ، فوجب أن يحصل هذا النوع من البهجة في حق الله تعالى
As for intellectual pleasures: they are like the delight in the attainment of attributes of perfection and majesty for Him. This type of delight the philosophers have unanimously affirmed in the case of Allah Almighty, while the theologians have unanimously denied it. As for the philosophers, they have argued for its affirmation by [saying] that He Almighty knows that He is described with attributes of perfection and majesty, and this knowledge necessitates joy and delight; so this type of joy must occur in the case of Allah Almighty.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of intellectual pleasures (al-ladhdhāt al-ʿaqliyya)—pleasures of the mind, such as delight in one's own perfections. Razi notes the complete disagreement: the philosophers (falāsifa) affirm such pleasure in Allah, while the theologians (mutakallimūn) deny it entirely. Then he presents the philosophers' proof: Allah knows His own perfect attributes, and such knowledge necessarily produces joy (bahjah) and delight (iltidhādh). Therefore, this joy must exist in Allah. The passage breaks off abruptly at this point; the theologian's response is not included in the available text. This incomplete presentation serves as a setup for the refutation that presumably follows in the missing portion.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that intellectual pleasure in Allah is uncontroversial or self-evident, but in fact the theologians reject it entirely.
Correction: The philosophers affirm intellectual pleasure in Allah, but the theologians deny it. The full debate requires considering both sides; the available passage only gives the philosophers' case.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the philosophers' argument for intellectual pleasure in Allah?
A) Allah has a physical body that experiences pleasure.
B) Allah's knowledge of His own perfect attributes necessitates joy and delight.
C) Allah receives pleasure from external objects.
D) The theologians have no argument against it.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that Allah's knowledge of His own perfection necessitates intellectual delight, but this view is rejected by theologians.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sincerely reflect that Allah possesses infinite perfection and majesty, does your heart feel any genuine delight? If not, what might that reveal about the depth of your knowledge of Him? Commit today to meditate on one of His attributes until it stirs your heart.
?
The philosophers argued that knowledge of perfection necessarily brings joy. If you know Allah's attributes intellectually but feel no joy in worship, is your knowledge merely theoretical? Ask yourself: What stands between me and a heart that exults in knowing my Lord? Take one concrete step this week to move from knowing about Allah to knowing Him.
?
The theologians denied that Allah experiences pleasure like us, but we are commanded to seek nearness to Him. Should pleasure be your goal in worship, or is it a byproduct? Examine your intention: When you pray, do you seek the joy of connection or merely fulfill an obligation? Pledge to make one prayer today purely for the sake of drawing close to Him, and notice what arises.
