Intellectual Pleasure in God: Philosophers' Proof and Objection
Razi defines intellectual pleasure, presents the philosophers' proof, and raises an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument for intellectual pleasure in God and the theological objection based on the difference in essences.

Core Idea
God's pleasure is not simply human pleasure scaled up — the essences differ.

This passage from Razi's work presents a key debate between philosophers and theologians on whether God experiences intellectual pleasure. The philosophers argue from God's self-awareness of His perfections, while the objection challenges the analogy between human and divine perfections. We'll follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Define intellectual pleasure
Read the opening definition of intellectual pleasure in the source. Note that it is pleasure from awareness of perfections and majesty.
This sets the stage for the debate.
2
Identify the two premises
The philosophers' proof rests on two premises: God has perfections, and God knows them. Find these in the text.
Both premises are argued from God's attributes and self-knowledge.
3
Understand the inductive reasoning
The philosophers use induction from human experience to claim that knowledge of one's perfections produces pleasure, then extrapolate to God.
The argument depends on analogy between human and divine.
4
Analyze the objection
The objection states that human perfections differ in essence from God's, so the analogy fails. A judgment on one essence does not transfer to another.
This is a methodological critique of the analogical argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما اللذات العقلية : فهي مثل الالتذاذ بحصول صفات الكمال والجلال له . وهذا النوع من الالتذاذ قد أطبقت الفلاسفة على إثباته في حق الله تعالى . والمتكلمون أطبقوا على إنكاره . أما الفلاسفة فقد احتجوا على إثباته بأنه تعالى عالم بكونه موصوفا بصفات الكمال والجلال ، وهذا العلم يوجب البهجة والالتذاذ ، فوجب أن يحصل هذا النوع من البهجة في حق الله تعالى . أما أنه موصوفا بصفات الكمال والجلال فهذا بناء على مقدمتين : إحداهما : كونه تعالى موصوفا بصفات الجلال والكمال . والثانية : أنه تعالى عالم بهذه الأحوال . أما عن صفات الجلال والكمال فالأمر فيه ظاهر ، لأن أعظم صفات الكمال والجلال، وجوب الوجود في الذات وفي الصفات ، وكمال العلم وكمال القدرة وكمال الفردانية في هذه الصفات . وكل ذلك حاصل في حق الله تعالى. وأما أنه تعالى عالم بهذه الأحوال فلأنه تعالى لما ثبت أنه عالم بذاته وبجميع صفاته ، وجب كونه عالما بهذه الأحوال . وأما أن كونه عالما بكونه موصوفا بصفات الكمال والجلال ، يوجب الابتهاج واللذة والسرور ، فالاستقراء يدل عليه ، لأنا متى علمنا من أنفسنا هذه الأحوال ، حصل أنواع من البهجة والفرح والسرور. فإذا كانت الكمالات في حق الله تعالى لا نسبة لها إلى كمالات البشر ، وعلم الله لا نسبة له إلى علم البشر في القوة والظهور ، وجب القطع بأنه لا نسبة لتلك البهجة والفرح إلى ما يحصل للبشر من هذا النوع . فهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب ولقائل أن يقول : كمالاتنا مخالفة بالماهية ، لكمالات الله تعالى فوجب أن يكون علمنا بكمالاتنا ، مخالفا لعلم الله تعالى بكمالات النفس ، ولا يلزم من ثبوت حكم ما في ماهية ثبوت مثل ذلك الحكم فيما يخالف تلك الماهية فهذا محصول الكلام في هذا الباب
As for intellectual pleasures (al-ladhdhat al-‘aqliyya): they are like taking pleasure in the attainment of attributes of perfection and majesty (sifat al-kamal wa al-jalal) for Him. This type of pleasure-taking has been unanimously affirmed by the philosophers (al-falasifa) in respect of God Most High, while the theologians (al-mutakallimun) have unanimously denied it. As for the philosophers, they have argued for its affirmation by [saying] that He Most High is knowing of His being described with attributes of perfection and majesty, and this knowledge necessitates delight (al-bahja) and pleasure, so it is necessary that this type of delight occurs in respect of God Most High. As for His being described with attributes of perfection and majesty, this is based on two premises: one of them is that He Most High is described with attributes of majesty and perfection, and the second is that He Most High is knowing of these states. As for the attributes of majesty and perfection, the matter is clear, for the greatest attributes of perfection and majesty are the necessity of existence (wujub al-wujud) in the essence and in the attributes, and the perfection of knowledge, perfection of power, and perfection of uniqueness (al-fardaniyya) in these attributes. All of that is present in respect of God Most High. As for His being knowing of these states, it is because when it is established that He Most High knows His essence and all His attributes, it necessarily follows that He is knowing of these states. As for His being knowing of His being described with attributes of perfection and majesty necessitating joy (al-ibtihaj), pleasure (al-ladhdha), and delight (al-surur), induction (al-istiqra’) indicates it, for whenever we from our own selves know these states, types of joy, happiness, and delight occur. And if the perfections in respect of God Most High bear no proportion to human perfections, and God’s knowledge bears no proportion to human knowledge in strength and manifestation, then it must be concluded that those joy and happiness bear no proportion to what occurs for humans of this type. This is the complete discussion of this chapter. And one may say: our perfections differ in essence (mukhalifa bi al-mahiyya) from God’s perfections, so it is necessary that our knowledge of our perfections differs from God’s knowledge of His essence, and it does not follow from the establishment of a judgment in one essence the establishment of a similar judgment in that which differs from that essence. This is the gist of the discussion in this chapter.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage discusses intellectual pleasure (al-ladhdhat al-‘aqliyya), which is pleasure derived from awareness of perfections and majesty. Razi states that philosophers have unanimously affirmed such pleasure for God while theologians have unanimously denied it. The philosophers' proof is built on two premises: (1) God possesses attributes of majesty and perfection, and (2) God is aware of these attributes. The first premise is clear: God has necessary existence, perfect knowledge, perfect power, and perfect uniqueness. The second follows from God's knowledge of His essence and attributes. Then they claim that this knowledge necessitates joy, pleasure, and delight, citing induction from human experience: when we become aware of our own perfections, we feel joy. Since God's perfections and knowledge are incomparably greater than ours, His joy and pleasure must also be incomparably greater. After presenting this argument, Razi introduces an objection (liqa'ilin an yaqula): human perfections differ in essence from God's, so our knowledge of our perfections differs from God's knowledge of Himself. Consequently, the judgment that knowledge of one's perfections yields pleasure in our case does not necessarily apply to God, because a ruling established for one essence does not transfer to a different essence. The passage ends with this objection.

Key Terms
اللذات العقلية
صفات الكمال والجلال
الابتهاج
الفلاسفة
المتكلمون
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the objection completely refutes the philosophical position.
Correction: The objection raises a methodological concern about analogy across essences, but it does not definitively disprove the philosophers' claim; it only shows that the argument from analogy is problematic.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the objection against the philosophers' proof for intellectual pleasure in God?
A) God's knowledge is not like human knowledge in strength
B) Human perfections differ in essence from God's, so the analogy fails
C) Pleasure is a sensory attribute, not an intellectual one
D) The philosophers have no proof at all

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' proof for intellectual pleasure in God relies on analogy with human experience, but the objection argues that the difference in essence invalidates the analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you delight in your own accomplishments or virtues, do you subconsciously assume that God's pleasure is simply a magnified version of that feeling? How does the objection about differing essences challenge that assumption in your heart today?
?
If intellectual pleasure for God is entirely unlike human joy, then your spiritual practices—prayer, dhikr, study—are not meant to produce a feeling that mirrors God's. What are you truly seeking in worship, and how must your intention shift?
?
The philosophers' argument uses an analogy that fails. Where in your life have you relied on an analogy between your standards and God's to justify complacency or to measure His love? Will you let the objection dismantle that false comfort right now?

The Two-Premise Proof of Divine Delight
The author states the two premises and concludes with divine delight, supported by induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-premise argument that God's knowledge of His perfections necessarily produces delight, and how induction from human experience supports this conclusion.

Core Idea
God's perfections and His knowledge of them necessitate infinite delight.

This lesson introduces a foundational argument in Islamic theology: that God's self-knowledge entails perfect delight. The author presents two premises—God's perfect attributes and His complete knowledge of them—and uses induction from human experience to illustrate the necessary connection. Understanding this proof is key to appreciating the nature of divine joy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Read the passage and locate the two premises: that God is described with attributes of perfection, and that God knows these attributes.
These are explicitly stated as the building blocks of the argument.
2
Understand the induction
Notice how the author uses human experience (when we know our own perfections, we feel joy) to support the conclusion for God, while emphasizing the infinite difference in proportion.
The induction is not an analogy of essence but a demonstration of a necessary connection.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why the author insists that God's delight is beyond proportion with human joy, and how this safeguards divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موصوفا بصفات الكمال والجلال. فهذا بناء على مقدمتين: إحداهما: كونه تعالى موصوفا بصفات الجلال والكمال. والثانية: أنه تعالى عالم بهذه الأحوال. أما عن صفات الجلال والكمال فالأمر فيه ظاهر، لأن أعظم صفات الكمال والجلال وجوب الوجود في الذات وفي الصفات، وكمال العلم وكمال القدرة وكمال الفردانية في هذه الصفات. وكل ذلك حاصل في حق الله تعالى. وأما أنه تعالى عالم بهذه الأحوال فلأنه تعالى لما ثبت أنه عالم بذاته وبجميع صفاته، وجب كونه عالما بهذه الأحوال. وأما أن كونه عالما بكونه موصوفا بصفات الكمال والجلال، يوجب الابتهاج واللذة والسرور، فالاستقراء يدل عليه، لأنا متى علمنا من أنفسنا هذه الأحوال، حصل أنواع من البهجة والفرح والسرور. فإذا كانت الكمالات في حق الله تعالى لا نسبة لها إلى كمالات البشر، وعلم الله لا نسبة له إلى علم البشر في القوة والظهور، وجب القطع بأنه لا نسبة لتلك البهجة والفرح إلى ما يحصل للبشر من هذا النوع. فهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب. ولقائل أن يقول: كمالاتنا مخالفة بالماهية، لكمالات الله تعالى فوجب أن يكون علمنا بكمالاتنا، مخالفا لعلم الله تعالى بكمالات النفس، ولا يلزم من ثبوت حكم ما في ماهية ثبوت مثل ذلك الحكم فيما يخالف تلك الماهية. فهذا محصول الكلام في هذا الباب.
[He is] described with attributes of perfection and majesty. This is based on two premises: one: that He, exalted, is described with attributes of majesty and perfection. And the second: that He, exalted, is knower of these states. As for the attributes of majesty and perfection, the matter is apparent, because the greatest attributes of perfection and majesty are the necessity of existence in essence and in attributes, and perfection of knowledge, perfection of power, and perfection of uniqueness in these attributes. And all that is realized with respect to God, exalted. And as for that He, exalted, is knower of these states, it is because when it is established that He, exalted, is knower of His essence and all His attributes, it follows that He is knower of these states. And as for that His being knower that He is described with attributes of perfection and majesty necessitates delight, pleasure, and joy, induction indicates it, because whenever we ourselves know these states in ourselves, various types of happiness, joy, and pleasure occur. Since the perfections with respect to God, exalted, have no proportion to human perfections, and God's knowledge has no proportion to human knowledge in strength and clarity, it must be concluded that those happiness and joy have no proportion to what occurs for humans of this kind. This completes the discussion in this section. And an objector may say: Our perfections are different in essence from God's perfections, so it follows that our knowledge of our perfections is different from God's knowledge of His essence, and the establishment of a judgment in one essence does not necessitate the establishment of a similar judgment in what differs from that essence. So this is the gist of the discussion in this section.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents a proof followed by an objection and its refutation. The author sets up a two-premise argument (مقدمتان). The first premise: God is described with attributes of majesty and perfection, which is obvious since He possesses necessary existence, perfect knowledge, perfect power, and perfect uniqueness. The second premise: God knows these attributes because He knows His essence and all His attributes. From these two premises, the conclusion follows: God's knowledge of His own perfections yields delight, pleasure, and joy. The author supports this by induction (الاستقراء) from human experience: when we become aware of our own perfections, we feel happiness. Since God's perfections and knowledge are infinitely greater, His delight is proportionately greater. Then an objection is raised: human perfections differ in essence (مخالفة بالماهية) from God's perfections, so one cannot analogize from human experience to God. The response is implied: the argument does not rely on analogy of essence but on the general principle that knowledge of perfection produces delight, a necessary correlative; the difference in essence does not break the connection because the judgment follows from the attribute of perfection itself, not from the specific essence. The objection is answered implicitly by reaffirming the original proof.

Key Terms
مقدمتان
كمال
جلال
ابتهاج
لذة
سرور
استقراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that attributing delight to God implies anthropomorphism or emotional change.
Correction: The author distinguishes divine delight from human emotions by emphasizing the infinite proportion of God's perfections, showing it is a necessary consequence of self-knowledge, not an emotional reaction.

Checkpoint
What is the second premise of the proof?
A) God is the creator of all things.
B) God is knower of these states (His perfections).
C) Human perfections are similar to God's perfections.
D) God's essence is unknown.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of His own perfections necessarily produces infinite delight, as shown by two premises and inductive reasoning from human experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God’s knowledge of His own perfections produces infinite delight? If so, why is your heart not constantly filled with awe and love for Him when you contemplate His attributes? What would change in your daily life if you carried this awareness with you every moment?
?
This lesson shows that even our small, imperfect knowledge of our own good qualities brings us joy. How much more is God’s joy in His perfections? Yet, do you find yourself more often seeking delight in created things than in the Creator who is infinitely more delightful? What step can you take today to shift your heart’s desire toward Him?
?
The proof here begins with the premise that God has perfect attributes. Have you ever doubted any of God’s attributes—His power, knowledge, or uniqueness? How does this doubt affect your reverence for Him? Commit to memorizing one attribute today and reflecting on its perfection until it stirs genuine delight in your soul.

Objection and Implicit Answer to the Proof
The objector raises a challenge based on essential difference, and the answer is implied.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that human and divine perfections differ in essence, and how the proof remains valid despite this difference.

Core Idea
The objection that essences differ does not break the necessary connection between self-knowledge and delight.

In this lesson, we examine a challenge to the previous proof: the objector argues that since human perfections differ in essence from God's, the analogy fails. The author's implicit answer reaffirms that the judgment follows from the attribute of perfection itself, not from the particular essence. This lesson sharpens our understanding of how rational argumentation in theology handles objections based on essential difference.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Pay attention to the objector's reasoning: our perfections differ in essence from God's, so judgments cannot be transferred.
The objector is trying to break the analogy used in the proof.
2
Understand the basis of the objection
The objector claims that because essences are different, a judgment valid for one essence cannot be assumed valid for another.
This is a general logical principle: quid quid est in uno non est in altero.
3
Reflect on the implicit answer
The author does not explicitly refute but implies that the argument does not rely on analogy of essences but on the necessary relationship between self-knowledge and delight, regardless of essence.
The proof is based on the attribute of perfection, not on a contingent similarity.

?

Three Proofs That Necessity Is Not the Essence
Three arguments demonstrating that necessity is not the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three arguments that demonstrate that the attribute of necessary existence is not identical to the divine essence.

Core Idea
Necessity is a distinct attribute, not the essence itself.

This passage from al-Matālib al-Āliyah presents three proofs that necessary existence (wujūb) is not identical to the essence (māhiyya) of the Necessary Being. These arguments rely on logical distinctions, conceptual knowledge, and the principle of contradiction. Understanding them helps clarify that God's attributes are not redundant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: three distinct proofs introduced by 'First', 'Second', 'Third'.
The proofs are concise; each has a clear premise and conclusion.
2
Analyze each proof
For each proof, identify the key premise and how it leads to the conclusion that necessity is not the essence. The first uses impossibility of self-description, the second uses knowledge of necessity, the third uses contradiction.
The third proof is particularly subtle—compare the contradictories.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why is it important that necessity is distinct from the essence? How does this relate to the broader discussion of divine attributes?
This distinction prevents the attributes from being mere redundancies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها : إنا نصف تلك الماهية بالوجوب . ووصف الشيء بنفسه محال . لأنا ندرك تفرقة بديهية بين قولنا : هذه الذات ، وبين قولنا : هذه الذات واجبة الوجود . وثانيها : إنه لو كان الوجوب هو نفس تلك الماهية ، لكان كل من عرف الوجوب بالذات عرف تلك الماهية المخصوصة ، ولما لم يكن الأمر كذلك ، علمنا أن الوجوب ليس نفس الماهية . وثالثها : إن نقيض قولنا : واجب لذاته : أنه ليس بواجب ، ولا يناقضه قولنا : إنه ليس تلك الذات الفلانية ، ولو كان الوجوب نفس تلك الذات لكان نقيض أحدهما ، عين نقيض الآخر
First: We describe that essence with necessity. And describing a thing by itself is impossible. For we perceive an intuitive distinction between our saying 'this essence' and our saying 'this essence is necessary of existence'. Second: If necessity were that same essence, then everyone who knew necessity by itself would know that specific essence. And since it is not the case, we know that necessity is not the essence itself. Third: The contradictory of our saying 'necessary by itself' is that it is not necessary, and our saying 'it is not that specific essence' does not contradict it. And if necessity were that same essence, then the contradictory of one would be exactly the contradictory of the other.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs that necessity (wujūb) is not identical to the essence (māhiyya) of the Necessary Being (Wājib bi al-dhāt). The first proof points out that we describe the essence as necessary, but describing a thing by itself is impossible; we perceive an intuitive difference between the essence and its being necessary. The second proof argues that if necessity were the essence, then anyone who knew necessity would know the essence, which is not the case. The third proof uses contradiction: the contradictory of 'necessary by itself' is 'not necessary', not 'not that specific essence'; if necessity were the essence, their contradictories would be identical, but they are not.

Key Terms
وجوب
ماهية
واجب بالذات
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, what is the contradictory of 'necessary by itself'?
A) Not that specific essence
B) Not necessary
C) Possible
D) Non-existent

Key Takeaway
The three proofs establish that the attribute of necessary existence is not identical to the divine essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is necessary', do you feel you have grasped His essence, or is that just an attribute? How does this lesson humble your claim to know God?
?
If merely understanding necessity does not give you knowledge of God's essence, what does that say about your own attempts to define Him? Are you content with knowing His attributes without claiming to know His essence?
?
The third proof shows that negating necessity is not the same as negating the essence. How does this affect your understanding of what it means to deny God? Are you careful not to confuse rejection of an attribute with rejection of the essence itself?

Proof That Necessity Is Not a Part of the Essence
A brief argument that necessity cannot be a part of the essence of the Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that necessity cannot be a part of the essence of the Necessary Being, and how this relates to the concept of contingency.

Core Idea
If necessity were part of the essence, the Necessary Being would be contingent.

This passage presents a concise proof that the attribute of necessity (wujub) cannot be a constituent part of the essence of the Necessary Being. The argument relies on a fundamental principle in Islamic theology: anything composed of parts is contingent. The lesson unpacks this reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the original argument
Start by reading the Arabic and English translation to get the gist of the proof.
Notice the concise style of the argument.
2
Identify the premise
The argument states that everything with a part is possible. Recognize this as the key premise.
This premise is assumed as known.
3
Apply the premise
If the Necessary Being had a part, it would be possible, which contradicts its necessity. Therefore, necessity cannot be a part of the essence.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters for understanding divine simplicity and the nature of God's attributes.
This argument supports the idea that God's attributes are not distinct parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بيان أنه لا يجوز أن يكون الوجوب جزءا للماهية. لأنكم لما ذكرتم أن كل ماله جزء فهو : ممكن ، فلو كان الواجب بالذات له جزء ، لكان ممكنا بالذات
As for the demonstration that it is not permissible for necessity to be a part of the essence: Because you have mentioned that everything that has a part is possible; so if the Necessary by itself had a part, it would be possible by itself.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage gives a brief proof that necessity cannot be a part (juz') of the essence. The premise is that anything composed of parts is contingent (mumkin). If the Necessary Being had a part, it would be composite and thus contingent, but the Necessary Being is necessary, not contingent. Therefore, necessity cannot be a part of the essence.

Key Terms
جزء
ممكن
واجب بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that necessity (wujub) is a separate part or component that makes up the essence of God.
Correction: This argument shows that if necessity were a part, the essence would be composite and thus contingent, which is impossible for the Necessary Being.

Checkpoint
Why can't necessity be a part of the essence of the Necessary Being?
A) Because a part is always external
B) Because having a part would make it possible
C) Because necessity is not an attribute
D) Because the essence is simple

Key Takeaway
This argument demonstrates that necessity cannot be a part of the essence because composition implies contingency, which is incompatible with necessary existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen that composition implies contingency. If you believe God is absolutely necessary, how do you reconcile that with any mental picture you might have of God having 'parts' or 'components'? Are you willing to let go of a comfortable but anthropomorphic image of God to affirm His true simplicity?
?
This argument forces you to choose: either necessity is part of God's essence (making Him contingent) or it is not. Which one do you truly accept in your heart? Have you ever unknowingly treated God's attributes as if they were separate parts added to His essence?
?
If necessity were a part of the essence, the Necessary Being would be possible—and thus not truly necessary. How does this realization affect your sense of security in God's absolute independence? Today, can you commit to worshipping a God whose necessity is not a part but His very reality?

Two Proofs That Necessity Is Not External
Two arguments that necessity cannot be external to the essence, concluding that it is not a positive attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why necessity (wujūb) cannot be an attribute external to the essence of the Necessary Being, and why it is not a positive attribute at all.

Core Idea
Necessity cannot be an external attribute—it is neither the essence nor an added property.

Theologians debate whether the attribute of necessity is something added to the essence or identical with it. This passage presents two strong arguments showing that necessity cannot be external, which leads to the conclusion that it is not a positive attribute. By studying these proofs, you'll grasp a key principle in Islamic theology about the simplicity of the Divine essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Try to follow the logical structure.
2
Examine the first proof
The first proof has two parts: (a) an external attribute would make the Necessary Being possible in itself, a contradiction; (b) it leads to infinite regress. Understand why each part is impossible.
The proof uses the concepts of 'possible by itself' and 'necessary by another'.
3
Examine the second proof
The second proof shows that if necessity were an external attribute, the essence and the attribute would depend on each other, causing a vicious circle.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes from all arguments that necessity cannot be identical to the essence, nor part of it, nor external—so it cannot be a positive attribute at all. Reflect on what this means for divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بيان أنه لا يجوز أن يكون خارجا عن الماهية . فلوجهين : الأول : إن كل صفة خارجة عن الماهية فهي مفتقرة إلى تلك الماهية ، والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته ، وواجب بغيره ، فيلزم أن يكون الواجب بالذات ممكنا لذاته ويلزم منه الجمع بين النقيضين ، وهو محال . وأيضا : فلما كان الوجوب بالذات واجبا بغيره، كان وجوب ذلك الغير سابقا على وجوب هذا الأثر ، فيلزم أن يحصل قبل ذلك الوجوب ، وجوب آخر إلى غير النهاية . وذلك محال . والثاني : إن الصفة مفتقرة إلى الذات، محتاجة إليها ، فلو حكمنا بأن وجوب تلك الذات إنما كان بسبب هذه الصفة لزم كون كل واحد منهما مفتقرا ، إلى الآخر وهو محال ، فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أنه يمتنع كون الوجوب بالذات نفس الماهية ، ولا جزءا منها ، ولا صفة خارجة عنها فيمتنع كون الوجوب صفة ثبوتية
As for the demonstration that it is not permissible for it to be external to the essence: There are two aspects. First: Every attribute external to the essence is dependent on that essence, and what is dependent on another is possible by itself and necessary by another; thus the Necessary by itself would be possible by itself, leading to a combination of contradictories, which is impossible. Also, since necessity by itself would be necessary by another, the necessity of that other would precede the necessity of this effect, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. Second: The attribute is dependent on the essence, in need of it; so if we judge that the necessity of that essence is due to this attribute, then each would be dependent on the other, which is impossible. Thus it is established that it is impossible for necessity by itself to be the essence itself, nor a part of it, nor an attribute external to it; therefore it is impossible for necessity to be a positive attribute.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs that necessity cannot be external (khārij) to the essence, followed by a concluding summary. The first proof argues that an external attribute is dependent on the essence; what is dependent is contingent in itself and necessary through another. If the Necessary Being's necessity were an external attribute, the Being would be necessary through that attribute, making it contingent in itself—a contradiction. Moreover, the necessity of the attribute would depend on a prior necessity, leading to infinite regress. The second proof notes that an attribute depends on its subject; if the essence's necessity were due to the attribute, each would depend on the other in a vicious circle. The conclusion from all the arguments on this page is that necessity is neither identical to the essence, nor a part of it, nor an external attribute; therefore, it is not a positive attribute (ṣifa thubūtiyya) at all.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think necessity is simply another attribute of God, like power or knowledge, added to His essence.
Correction: The passage proves that necessity cannot be an attribute at all (neither identical nor external), because it would entail contradictions, infinite regress, or circularity.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what would happen if necessity were an external attribute?
A) The Necessary Being would be possible by itself
B) The essence would be identical to necessity
C) There would be two necessities
D) Nothing would change

Key Takeaway
Necessity cannot be a positive attribute; it is a unique status that belongs to the essence itself without addition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Your entire existence is contingent and dependent on the Necessary Being. What one area of your life are you living as if you are self-sufficient, and how will you surrender that illusion today?
?
The proof shows that attaching necessity to anything other than the essence leads to contradiction. What created 'necessities' (jobs, relationships, health) have you falsely elevated to the status of absolute, and what step will you take to demote them?
?

Third Proof: Necessity as Positive Attribute Leads to Regress
Argument against necessity as positive attribute via regress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why necessity (wujūb) cannot be a positive attribute because it would lead to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
If necessity were a positive attribute, it would cause an infinite regress.

In this third proof, al-Matalib al-Aliyah argues that necessity (wujūb) cannot be a positive (thubūtī) attribute. The reasoning shows that if it were, it would be an existent distinct from the essence, leading to a composition and then to a regress that cannot be resolved. Pay close attention to the two alternatives presented for how the existence would qualify the essence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the assumption
The argument begins by supposing that necessity is something established (thābit), i.e., a positive attribute. What does that imply?
This is the hypothesis to be disproven.
2
Follow the consequences
If necessity is a positive attribute, it would be like other existents: it has an essence and an existence distinct from each other. Why would its existence be other than its essence?
This sets up the composition problem.
3
Examine the two alternatives
How does the existence attach to the essence? Either by possibility (impossible for a necessary being) or by necessity (creates an infinite regress). Consider why both are impossible.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Conclude the impossibility
Since both alternatives are impossible, the original assumption is false: necessity cannot be a positive attribute.
The conclusion is decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لو كان الوجوب أمرا ثابتا لكان مساويا لسائر الموجودات في كونه موجودا ، ومخالفا لها في ماهيته المعينة ، فيكون وجوده غير ماهيته فاتصاف ماهیته بوجوده إن كان على سبيل الإمكان بالذات، وهو محال . وإن كان على سبيل الوجوب ، لزم أن يكون وجوب لوجود زائداً عليه . ولزم التسلسل . وهو محال .
Third: If necessity were something established, it would be equal to other existents in being existent, and different from them in its specific essence; so its existence would be other than its essence. Then the qualification of its essence by its existence: if it is by way of possibility in itself, that is impossible; and if it is by way of necessity, then there would be a necessity for existence additional to it, and a regress would follow, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof that necessity cannot be a positive (thubūtī) attribute. If necessity were a real, positive thing, it would be an existent distinct from the essence. Like other existents, it would have its own essence and existence. Its existence would then be other than its essence. Now, that essence would be qualified by existence either contingently (making the essence possible in itself, which contradicts its necessity) or necessarily. If necessarily, then the necessity of that existence would itself require a further existence, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). Both alternatives are impossible, so necessity cannot be a positive attribute.

موجود

تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessity is a positive attribute like any other, such as knowledge or power.
Correction: If it were, it would be an existent distinct from the essence and lead to an infinite regress, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
In the argument, if the existence attaches to the essence by way of necessity, what follows?
A) The essence becomes possible
B) A regress occurs
C) The attribute is negated
D) The essence becomes simple

Key Takeaway
Necessity cannot be a positive attribute because it would create an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The argument breaks down because essence and existence would be separate. Do you live as if your own existence is separate from Allah's sustaining will? How might acknowledging that your existence is entirely dependent on Him, not a separate property, reshape your gratitude and reliance?

The Fourth Proof: Necessity as Positive Attribute Leads to Contradiction
Reductio proof that necessity by itself cannot be a positive attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof that necessity by itself cannot be a positive attribute because it would be a composite essence, entailing possibility and thus contradiction.

Core Idea
Necessity by itself, if positive, would be composite, thus possible, contradicting necessity.

This lesson examines a sophisticated reductio ad absurdum argument from classical kalām. The argument assumes, for the sake of proof, that necessity by itself (wujūb bi al-dhāt) is a positive attribute. It then analyzes the concept of necessity, dividing it into necessity by self and necessity by another, and shows that the shared concept and the specific differentia must be distinct. This leads to the conclusion that necessity by itself would be a composite essence, and every composite essence is possible, which contradicts the very meaning of necessity by itself. The argument is a powerful demonstration of why divine attributes cannot be positive additions to the divine essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source passage once carefully. Then read the literal English translation. Let the logical flow of the argument settle in your mind before analyzing the details.
Notice the conditional structure: 'If necessity by itself were positive... then it would be possible, which is impossible.'
2
Identify the division of necessity
The passage divides necessity into two kinds: necessity by itself (wujūb bi al-dhāt) and necessity by another (wujūb bi al-ghayr). Understand that 'necessity' is a general concept shared by both, while 'by itself' is a specific differentia.
The argument relies on the distinction between the shared meaning (ishtirāk) and the distinguishing meaning (imtiyāz).
3
Trace the composition argument
Follow the reasoning: since the shared concept (necessity) is different from the distinguishing concept (by itself), necessity by itself is a composite of genus and differentia. Every composite essence is contingent (mumkin) because it depends on its parts.
This step is crucial: composition implies dependence, and dependence implies possibility.
4
Understand the contradiction
The conclusion is that if necessity by itself existed as a positive attribute, it would be possible. But necessity by itself is defined as that which cannot be possible. Thus, we would have a contradiction: the same thing would be necessarily necessary and possibly necessary, which is absurd.
The argument shows that the initial assumption leads to a logical impossibility, so the assumption must be false.
5
Reflect on the broader implication
This proof demonstrates that divine attributes cannot be real, positive entities distinct from the essence. They must be either identical to the essence or merely negative concepts.
This is a foundational argument for the Ashʿarī position on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : لو كان الوجوب بالذات أمراً ثبوتياً لكان ممكناً لذاته ، وهذا محال ، فذاك محال ، بيان الشرطية : أن الوجوب ينقسم إلى ما يكون وجوبا بالذات، وإلى ما يكون وجوبا بالغير، والمفهوم من الوجوب هو الذي به يشترك هذان القسمان ، وكونه بالذات هو الذي به يتميز أحد هذين القسمين عن الثاني ، وما به المشاركة غير ما به المباينة ، فكونه وجوبا ، مغاير لكونه بالذات وإذا كان كذلك كان الوجوب بالذات ماهية مركبة وكل ماهية مركبة فهي ينتج أنه لو كان الوجوب بالذات موجوداً ، لكان ممكنا . وإنما قلنا : إن ذلك محال لأن كونه وجوبا بالذات، ماهية مناقضة للإمكان . فالقول يكون إحدى الماهيتين نفس الثاني ، جمع بين النقيضين وهو محال .
Fourth: If necessity by itself were a positive thing, it would be possible in itself, and this is impossible, so that is impossible. Explanation of the conditional: Necessity is divided into what is necessity by itself and what is necessity by another. The concept of necessity is that by which these two divisions share, and its being 'by itself' is that by which one of the two divisions is distinguished from the other. That by which they share is other than that by which they differ. So its being necessity is different from its being 'by itself'. If that is so, then necessity by itself is a composite essence, and every composite essence is possible. It follows that if necessity by itself existed, it would be possible. And we say that is impossible because its being necessity by itself is an essence that contradicts possibility. So the statement that one of the two essences is the same as the second would be a combination of contradictories, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof. It begins by assuming, for reductio, that necessity by itself (wujūb bi al-dhāt) is a positive attribute. The argument then analyses the concept: 'necessity' is a genus shared by necessary-by-itself and necessary-by-another. The specification 'by itself' is a differentia. Since the shared concept differs from the distinguishing concept, necessity by itself is a composite (murakkab) of genus and differentia. Every composite is possible (mumkin) because it depends on its parts. Therefore, if necessity by itself were a real existent, it would be possible, which contradicts the nature of necessity by itself, which is essentially incompatible with possibility. This would entail joining two contradictories, which is absurd. Hence, necessity by itself cannot be a positive attribute.

Key Terms
وجوب بالذات
وجوب بالغير
مفهوم
مشاركة
مباينة
ماهية مركبة
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessity by itself is a simple, positive attribute without composition.
Correction: The argument shows that the concept of necessity by itself necessarily involves two distinct components: the genus 'necessity' and the differentia 'by itself'. Therefore, if it were an existent, it would be composite and contingent.

Checkpoint

B) Because it would be a composite essence
C) Because it would be dependent on another
D) Because it would be a negation

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof demonstrates that necessity by itself cannot be a positive attribute because its concept is inherently composite, and composition entails possibility, which contradicts the nature of necessity by itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Will you commit to purifying your conception of God's oneness, especially when you pray or speak about His attributes?
?
The proof shows that necessity by itself necessarily excludes possibility. Have you ever doubted God's existence or acted as if it were merely probable? What concrete step can you take today to root your certainty in the fact that God is necessary by Himself, not dependent on anything?
?
You use terms like 'God's mercy' or 'God's power' as if they are distinct. How does this lesson challenge you to see them as identical with His essence? Will you examine your speech and thoughts to ensure you are not unconsciously attributing composition to the Creator?

Two Meanings of Self-Necessity and Why Neither Is Positive
Analysis showing that the only intelligible concepts of necessity are negative or lead to regress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the only two intelligible meanings of 'necessity by itself' do not yield a positive attribute, and how this supports the thesis that self-necessity is not a real attribute.

Core Idea
Self-necessity cannot be a positive attribute—it is either a negation or leads to regress.

This passage presents the fifth argument of the section, which carefully examines what we can actually conceive when we say something is 'necessary by itself.' The author shows that we either mean it does not depend on anything else (a negative idea) or that it causes its own existence (which, if taken as a real attribute, leads to an infinite regress). Thus, necessity by itself cannot be a real, positive attribute.

Guided Path
1
Understand the question
What does 'necessary by itself' mean? The author is investigating whether this phrase refers to a positive attribute.
This is the core of the fifth proof.
2
Identify the two meanings
The passage gives exactly two possible meanings: lack of dependence on an external cause, and the essence necessitating its own existence. Note that no third meaning is intelligible.
These are the only two options.
3
Examine the first meaning
The first meaning is clearly a negative concept (mafhūm sulbī), not a positive one. The text says this is known by necessity.
So this cannot be a positive attribute.
4
Examine the second meaning
If we take the second meaning as a positive thing distinct from the essence and from necessity itself, then it becomes an accidental attribute requiring another necessitation, leading to regress. Therefore it cannot be a positive attribute either.
The argument is left incomplete but the direction is clear.
5
Draw the conclusion
Since neither meaning yields a positive attribute, 'necessity by itself' does not denote a real attribute. This supports the overall thesis of the section.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : وهو أن المعقول من الوجوب بالذات، إما عدم افتقاره إلى سبب منفصل ، وإما كونه مقتضيا لوجود نفسه . ولا يعقل من الوجوب بالذات أمر آخر سوى هذين المفهومين . أما الأول : وهو عدم افتقاره إلى سبب منفصل ، فلا شك أن هذا المفهوم مفهوم سلبي لا ثبوتي. والعلم بأن الأمر كذلك علم ضروري . وأما الثاني : وهو أن كون تلك الماهية مقتضية لوجود نفسها ، فنقول : ذلك الافتقار إن كان أمرا ثبوتيا ، مغايرا لتلك الماهية ، ولذلك الوجوب ، كان ذلك الاقتضاء لاحقا من لواحق تلك الماهية ، وصفة من صفاتها ، فلا تكون قائمة بنفسها ، مستقلة بذاتها، فلا بد لها من اقتضاء آخر ، وذلك يوجب
Fifth: That which is intelligible from necessity by itself is either its lack of dependence on a separate cause, or its being a necessitator of its own existence. There is no other meaning of necessity by itself besides these two concepts. As for the first—its lack of dependence on a separate cause—there is no doubt that this concept is a negative concept, not positive. Knowing that this is so is necessary knowledge. As for the second—that the essence is a necessitator of its own existence—we say: that dependence, if it were a positive thing, different from that essence and from that necessity, then that necessitation would be an accident among the accidents of that essence, an attribute among its attributes, not subsistent by itself or independent in its essence; it would require another necessitation, and that necessitates...
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth and final proof on these pages. It first identifies the only two intelligible meanings of 'necessity by itself': (1) the absence of dependence on an external cause, and (2) the essence necessitating its own existence. The first meaning is clearly a negative concept (mafhūm sulbī), not a positive attribute. The second meaning, if taken as a positive thing distinct from the essence, would be an accidental attribute requiring a further necessitation, leading to regress. Thus, neither meaning yields a positive attribute for necessity. The argument is left incomplete at the bottom of the page, but the direction is clear: necessity by itself cannot be a positive attribute.

Correction: The only two intelligible meanings of self-necessity are either a negative concept (no dependence) or a positive concept that leads to regress. Neither yields a coherent positive attribute.

Key Takeaway

Second Objection: Divine Knowledge Necessitates Unity of Will
Objection: divine knowledge necessitates unity of will

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that divine knowledge prevents conflict of wills because each god knows the actual outcome and cannot will the impossible.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge forces divine will into unity — the impossible cannot be willed.

The objector responds to the claim that conflict in wills is possible by presenting several arguments. The first relies on the nature of divine knowledge: if each god knows exactly what will happen, they cannot will otherwise. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and its implications for the unity of divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the concessive opening and the structure of the first argument (al-wajh al-awwal).
This argument is a logical chain: knowledge → impossibility → will alignment.
2
Identify the key premises
Find the two key premises: (1) each god knows all things, including the actual outcome, and (2) will must conform to knowledge.
The actual world must be one of two contradictory states (e.g., motion or rest).
3
Understand the impossibility principle
Grasp why willing what is known to be impossible is itself impossible. This is the linchpin of the argument.
This principle is based on the nature of divine will and knowledge.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Trace how the premises lead to the conclusion that both gods will the same thing, making conflict impossible.
The argument does not prove unity of essence, only unity of will in this specific case.
5
Reflect on theological implications
Consider what this argument implies about the possibility of multiple gods. Does it effectively refute the possibility of conflict?
This is one of several objections the author will address.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : إن دل ما ذكرتم على أن حصول المخالفة بينها جائز ، إلا أنه حصل عندنا وجوه دالة على امتناع ذلك • فالوجه الأول : إن كل واحد منها يجب أن يكون عالما بجميع المعلومات ، ومن المعلوم بالضرورة أنه لا بد وأن يكون الواقع أحد القسمين إما الحركة وإما السكون ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، كان كل واحد من الإلهين عالما بأن الداخل في الوجود هو ذلك القسم ، والإرادة يجب أن تكون على وفق العلم لأن خلاف المعلوم محال الوقوع . وما كان محال الوقوع امتنع أن يكون مرادا ، مع العلم بكونه محال الوقوع ، وإذا كان كل واحد منهما عالما بأنه لا يقع إلا ذلك الشيء ، وثبت أن ذلك يوجب الاتحاد في الإرادة ، فحينئذ يظهر أن المخالفة بينهما في الإرادة ممتنعة
The second question: If what you have mentioned indicates that the occurrence of conflict between them is permissible, yet we have aspects indicating its impossibility. The first aspect: Each one of them must be knowing of all known things, and it is necessarily known that the actual must be one of two divisions, either motion or rest. When this is so, each of the two gods knows that what enters into existence is that division, and will must be in accordance with knowledge, because opposition to the known is impossible of occurrence. And what is impossible of occurrence cannot be willed, with the knowledge of its being impossible. And when each of them knows that only that thing occurs, and it is established that this necessitates unity in will, then it becomes clear that conflict between them in will is impossible.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection (su'al) against the possibility of two gods having conflicting wills. The objector concedes that the previous discussion might show conflict is permissible, but he offers counter-arguments (wujuh) demonstrating its impossibility. The first argument is based on divine knowledge. Each god must know all things, and it is necessarily known that the actual world will be either motion or rest. Therefore, each god knows which state will actually occur. Since will must conform to knowledge (it is impossible to will what one knows cannot happen), both gods will will the same thing. Thus their wills are united, and conflict is impossible. This argument uses the principle that divine knowledge is all-encompassing and infallible, and will is subordinate to knowledge.

Key Terms
العلم

Correction: The argument does not claim that knowledge forces a single outcome, but that will cannot be directed at what is known to be impossible.

Refuting the Argument for Two Gods' Agreement
The author refutes three arguments that two gods must agree on an action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes three arguments for the necessary agreement between two gods by exposing a false premise about isolation versus conjunction.

Core Idea
What holds true in isolation may not hold in conjunction.

This lesson examines a key debate in Islamic theology: could two gods exist and necessarily agree? The author presents three arguments that claim such agreement is required, then refutes them by identifying a hidden assumption. By focusing on the logical pivot, you will see how a single false premise can collapse an entire argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow. Notice the pattern: three arguments followed by a refutation.
The passage uses technical terms like 'الانفراد' and 'الاجتماع'.
2
Identify the first argument
The first argument says: if a claim's affirmation leads to its negation, it is false. The opponent claims that the existence of two gods leads to contradiction.
This is a form of reductio ad absurdum.
3
Identify the second and third arguments
The second argument relies on the eternity of wills—if wills are eternal, they cannot change. The third argument points out that if two gods will opposites, they become incapable, which is contradictory for deities.
These arguments are meant to show that two gods must will the same thing.
4
Examine the refutation
The author reveals the common premise: that what holds in isolation (each god alone) must hold in conjunction (together). He then rejects this premise by noting that each god alone can achieve his desire, but together they cannot.
The refutation does not attack each argument individually but undermines their shared assumption.
5
Reflect on the logical structure
Consider why this refutation is effective. It shows that the opponent's arguments are not valid because they assume an unjustified transfer of properties from one context to another.
This is a common fallacy in theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل . وكل شيء يفضى ثبوته إلى نفيه ، أو نفيه إلى ثبوته كان باطلا ، فوجب أن يكون هذا الكلام باطلا الثاني : وهو أن تعلق إرادة كل واحد منهما بذلك الفعل المعين ، حكم ثبت من الأزل ، والأزل لا يجوز زواله ، فوجب أن لا تبطل تلك الإرادة الثالث : إنه لما أراد هذا شيئا ، وأراد الثاني ضده. ثم عجزا عن تحصيل مراديهما ، عند الاجتماع. كان هذا العاجز أقوى ، والضعيف أكمل فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذا المقام . لأنا نقول : مدار هذا الكلام على أن كل حكم يصح حصوله عند الانفراد، وجب أن يبقى صحيحاً ممكناً عند الاجتماع. فنقول : وهذا باطل بدليل : أن هذا عند الانفراد قادر على تحصيل مراده ، وذلك عند الانفراد أيضاً قادر على تحصيل مراده ، وأما عند الاجتماع فلم يبقيا على هذه الحالة ، فيثبت أنه لا يلزم من حصول حكم حال الانفراد ، إمكان حصوله حال الاجتماع ، فظهر سقوط هذا الكلام
The proof: Everything whose affirmation leads to its negation, or its negation to its affirmation, is false. So necessarily this speech is false. Second: that the attachment of the will of each of them to that specific action is a judgment established from eternity, and eternity cannot cease, so necessarily that will cannot be invalidated. Third: that when one willed a thing, and the second willed its opposite, then they became incapable of achieving their desires upon conjunction. So this incapable one would be stronger, and the weak one more perfect. This completes the discourse in establishing this position. We say: The pivot of this speech is that every judgment that is valid when alone must remain valid and possible when together. So we say: This is false, by evidence that this one when alone is able to achieve his desire, and that one when alone is also able to achieve his desire, but when together they do not remain in this state. So it is established that it does not follow from the occurrence of a judgment in the state of isolation the possibility of its occurrence in the state of conjunction. So the failure of this speech becomes clear.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. The author first presents the opponent's three arguments for the necessary agreement of two gods, then refutes them by exposing a false premise. The opponent's first argument states that any claim whose affirmation entails its negation is false, and here the claim leads to contradiction. The second argument says that the wills of the two gods are eternal, and since eternity is immutable, their wills cannot be invalidated. The third argument claims that if they will opposites, they become incapable, making the incapable one stronger—a contradiction. The author then responds by identifying the core assumption: that what holds in isolation must hold in conjunction. He rejects this by noting that while each god alone can achieve his desire, together they cannot, so the premise is false. Thus the opponent's arguments collapse.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
مراد (intended object)
الأزل (eternity)
الانفراد (isolation)
الاجتماع (conjunction)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the author is proving the impossibility of multiple gods, but he is only refuting a specific argument for their necessary agreement.
Correction: The author does not assert the impossibility of two gods; he merely shows that the opponent's arguments fail.

Checkpoint
What is the central premise that the author refutes in this passage?
A) Eternal judgments cannot change.
B) What is valid in isolation must be valid in conjunction.
C) Two gods cannot will opposites.
D) The weak are always stronger.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's arguments fail because they assume that what is true for each god alone remains true when they are together, which is not necessarily the case.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The opponent's argument seems logically sound at first, yet the author reveals a hidden assumption. Where in your own understanding of Allah's attributes might you be making an assumption that seems logical but is actually not supported by revelation or sound reason? Will you examine that assumption today?
?
If you were in a disagreement with a fellow Muslim about a matter of belief, would you be willing to have your own premises exposed as false like the opponent's were? Or do you cling to arguments that 'seem right' without checking their foundations? What step will you take this week to test the logical consistency of your own aqeedah?

Fourth Objection: Prevention by Knowledge
Sanusi answers the objection about prevention by knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author deflects the objection that each god might be prevented from achieving his will by appealing to divine knowledge about the other's possible interference.

Core Idea
Knowledge of possible prevention suffices to prevent action, without contradiction.

In the fourth objection, a skeptic questions why each god cannot simply be prevented from achieving his desire, which might imply a flaw. The author deftly responds by proposing that the preventer is each god's own knowledge that the other would block him, thereby avoiding the absurdity of simultaneous desires. This clever move safeguards divine attributes without conceding impotence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening question: 'Why is it not permissible that each of them be unable to achieve his desire?' Recognize this as an attempt to find a contradiction in the concept of multiple gods.
The objector assumes that prevention implies a defect in power.
2
Examine the opponent's argument
The opponent (quoting previous reasoning) says that if both desires are prevented, the preventer must be the other's desire occurring, which would force both desires to occur together—an absurdity.
This is the classical dilemma: either one desire occurs or both, but mutual prevention seems impossible.
3
Follow the author's novel answer
The author suggests that the preventer is not the actual occurrence of the other's desire, but each god's knowledge that if he tries, the other will prevent him. This knowledge alone stops the attempt.
Knowledge here is a cognitive state, not a competing desire.
4
See why the absurdity is avoided
Since the knowledge prevents any attempt, both desires are never simultaneously willed or realized. No contradiction arises because the scenario only involves potential prevention, not actual conflict.
The key is that prevention operates before any desire is enacted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع : لم لا يجوز أن يتعذر على كل واحد منهما تحصيل مراده ؟ قوله : ( المانع لكل واحد منهما عن تحصيل مراده ، هو حصول مراد الثاني ، فلو امتنع المرادان معا ، لوجب حصول المرادين معاً . وأنه باطل ، قلنا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : المانع لكل واحد منهما عن تحصيل مراده ، علمه بأنه لو حال تحصيل مراده ، يمنعه الآخر منه. فهذا العلم هو المانع فقط ، وحينئذ لا يلزم المحذور المذكور
The fourth question: Why is it not permissible that each of them be unable to achieve his desire? He said: 'The preventer for each of them from achieving his desire is the occurrence of the other's desired, so if both desired objects were prevented, it would necessitate the occurrence of both desired objects together. And that is false.' We said: Why is it not permissible to say: The preventer for each of them from achieving his desire is his knowledge that if he tries to achieve his desire, the other would prevent him from it. So this knowledge alone is the preventer, and then the mentioned absurdity does not follow.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector asks why each god cannot simply be unable to achieve his will. The opponent (perhaps quoting the author's previous reasoning) argues that if both wills are prevented, then both desires would have to occur simultaneously (since prevention is by the opposite's desire), which is contradictory. The author replies by proposing an alternative: the preventer is not the actual occurrence of the other's will, but each god's knowledge that if he tries, the other will prevent him. This knowledge alone suffices to prevent the attempt, and no contradiction arises because both desires are never simultaneously realized. Thus the objection is deflected.

Key Terms
مانع (preventer)
علم (knowledge)
مراد (desired object)
محذور (absurdity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author is introducing a new limitation on divine power (i.e., ignorance or inability).
Correction: The author is only explaining how mutual prevention could occur without contradiction. Knowledge here is a cognitive attribute, not a defect. It does not imply lack of power, but rather wise restraint.

Checkpoint
According to the author's answer to the fourth objection, what prevents each god from achieving his desire?
A) The actual occurrence of the other's desire.
B) Each god's knowledge that the other will prevent him.
C) A lack of power in both gods.
D) The eternity of their wills.

Key Takeaway
The author shows that the problem of mutual prevention in multiple gods is resolved by considering knowledge as the preventing factor, avoiding logical absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you stop yourself because you know Allah sees, or only when you fear worldly consequences? Do you truly let the knowledge of His awareness prevent you from wrong?
?
The lesson shows that knowledge alone can prevent action without removing ability. Is there a sin you claim you cannot resist, but in truth you know it is displeasing to Allah? What would it take for your knowledge of His command to become your actual preventer?
?
If you believed with certainty that Allah's knowledge prevents any contradiction in His decree, would you stop worrying about outcomes you cannot control? What specific fear would you surrender to Him today?

Eternal Powerlessness vs. Logical Impossibility
Refutes claim that eternal powerless being is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the impossibility of acting in pre-eternity does not imply that God is powerless, and why the objection conflates logical impossibility with deficiency.

Core Idea
An act with a beginning cannot occur in a beginningless state.

This lesson tackles a key objection to divine power: if God is eternal and could not act in pre-eternity, doesn't that make Him essentially powerless? The author refutes this by showing that acting in pre-eternity is logically impossible—not because of a lack of power, but because an act requires a beginning, while pre-eternity has no beginning. Thus the inability to act there is due to the nature of the act, not a deficiency in the agent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Identify the opponent's claim: an eternal being who is powerless is impossible. Notice that this objection assumes powerlessness is a defect.
The objection is stated in the first line of the passage.
2
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. The author gives a proof: God existed in pre-eternity and could not perform an act because acts have a beginning. Pay attention to the logic: pre-eternity (azal) has no beginning, so any act (which has a beginning) cannot occur there.
The key terms are الأزل (pre-eternity) and المحال (impossible).
3
Analyze the implication
Reflect on why this refutes the objection. The inability to act in pre-eternity is due to the impossibility of the act itself, not a lack of power. Thus the eternal being is not truly powerless; the act is simply not an object of power.
This shows the objector conflates two different kinds of inability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله ثانياً : هو القديم العاجز محال ، قلنا : لا نسلم . والدليل عليه هو : أن الإله تعالى كان موجوداً في الأزل مع أنه ما كان قادراً البتة على الفعل في الأزل . لأن الفعل ماله أول . والأزل مالا أول له . والجمع بينهما محال . والمحال لا قدرة عليه .
As for his second statement: 'An eternal powerless being is impossible.' We say: We do not accept. The proof of that is that God, exalted, existed in pre-eternity while He was not at all able to act in pre-eternity, because an act has a beginning, while pre-eternity has no beginning. Combining them is impossible, and the impossible is not within capability.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage responds to an objection that an eternal being who is powerless is impossible. The author rejects this assertion. The proof offered is that God existed in pre-eternity (al-azal) and during that state He was not capable of performing any act, because acts have a beginning (first moment) while pre-eternity is beginningless. Acting in pre-eternity is therefore impossible because it would require combining a beginning with no beginning. Since the impossible is not an object of power, God's lack of ability there does not imply true powerlessness. Thus an eternal being can be without power in certain respects without contradiction.

Key Terms
القديم
العاجز
الأزل
القدرة
المحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God cannot do something (e.g., act in pre-eternity), then He lacks power and is therefore imperfect.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the inability is due to the logical impossibility of the act itself—not a deficiency in power. The impossible is not an object of power.

Checkpoint
Why was God not able to act in pre-eternity according to the answer?
A) Because He lacked power.
B) Because acts have a beginning and pre-eternity has no beginning, making action impossible.
C) Because He chose not to act.
D) Because another god prevented Him.

Key Takeaway
The inability to act in pre-eternity is due to logical impossibility, not a lack of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God's inability to act in pre-eternity is due to the impossibility of the act itself, not a flaw in His power, does your heart truly rest in His absolute perfection? Or do you still harbor doubts about His ability to handle the impossible in your life? Confess those doubts now and affirm that nothing truly possible is beyond Him.
?
Have you ever accused God of being powerless because He didn't answer a prayer in the way you expected? This lesson shows that some things are logically impossible for anyone. Are you willing to trust that God's 'no' is actually a protection from the impossible? Examine a specific disappointment and repent of any attribution of weakness to God.
?
If the impossible is not an object of God's power, what does that mean for your fears? Are you afraid of something that is actually impossible? Surrender that fear today, knowing that God's power is sufficient for all that is truly possible.

Refuting the Omniscience Objection to Divine Unity
Sanusi replies to the omniscience objection by unmasking hidden circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical order of knowledge, will, and occurrence, and how this refutes the claim that omniscience prevents disagreement between multiple gods.

Core Idea
Knowledge follows will and occurrence, not the other way around.

This lesson addresses a key objection to the classical proof of divine unity: if each god knows every detail of the future, how could they ever disagree? The author shows that this objection misunderstands the relationship between knowledge, will, and occurrence. By analyzing the dependency chain, he exposes a hidden circularity in the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
First, read the objector's argument in the Arabic and translation. Note the sequence: each god knows all → knows which outcome will happen → cannot will otherwise → no disagreement.
The objection seems plausible at first glance.
2
Examine the author's reply
Read the author's response. He breaks down the chain: knowledge of occurrence follows occurrence, which follows will. This shows that knowledge is downstream of will.
3
Understand the circularity problem
Notice that if we reversed the order (will depending on knowledge), we would create a logical circle: will depends on knowledge, knowledge depends on occurrence, occurrence depends on will. The author calls this 'دور' (circularity) which is invalid.
This is the core refutation.
4
Connect to the larger argument
Consider how this reply defends the possibility of multiple gods having distinct wills despite shared knowledge. The objection fails because it assumes knowledge determines will, whereas the opposite is true.
This preserves the possibility of disagreement, which is needed for the proof of unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله في المعارضة الأولى : ( إن كل واحد منهما عالم بجميع المعلومات ، فيكون كل واحد منهما عالماً بأن أي الطرفين يقع ، وأيهما لا يقع ؟ وإذا كان كذلك امتنع أن تريد ، إلا ذلك الواحد ، فيمتنع وقوع المخالفة بينهما ) قلنا : العلم بالوقوع تبع للوقوع الذي هو تبع للإرادة الوقوع ، فلو جعلنا إرادة الوقوع تابعة للعلم بالوقوع ، لزم الدور ، وأنه باطل .
As for his statement in the first objection: 'Each of them knows all objects of knowledge, so each of them knows which of the two alternatives will occur and which will not occur. And if that is the case, it is impossible that they will except that one, so the occurrence of disagreement between them is impossible.' We say: Knowledge of occurrence follows occurrence, which follows the will and occurrence. For if we made the will of occurrence dependent upon knowledge of occurrence, circularity would follow, and that is false.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage addresses the first objection. The objector argues that if each god knows all things, then each knows which outcome will happen. Therefore, they cannot will differently, so disagreement is impossible. The author replies by analyzing the order of things: knowledge of what will occur depends on the occurrence itself. The occurrence, in turn, follows the will. So knowledge is subsequent to will and occurrence, not prior. If we reversed this and made the will depend on knowledge, we would have a circular dependency: will would depend on knowledge, but knowledge depends on occurrence which depends on will. That is a logical circle, which is invalid. Thus the objection fails because it assumes that knowledge determines will, whereas actually will determines occurrence, and knowledge follows occurrence.

Key Terms
علم
إرادة
وقوع
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's knowledge predetermines His will, so multiple gods would necessarily will the same thing.
Correction: In reality, will causes occurrence, and knowledge merely records what occurs. Thus omniscience does not constrain will.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does knowledge of an occurrence depend on?
A) The will alone
B) The occurrence itself, which depends on will
C) The objector's assumption
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because it inverts the real order: will determines occurrence, and knowledge follows occurrence, not the other way around.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that because God knows everything, your choices are already set and you have no real will? How does the lesson's order (will then occurrence then knowledge) change your responsibility for your actions?
?
What happens in your heart when you realize that your will is not forced by God's knowledge, but rather God's knowledge follows what you truly will? Are you willing to own that freedom today?
?
If knowledge follows will and occurrence, are you basing your knowledge of God's attributes on what the Qur'an and reason say, or are you letting your human assumptions about causality shape your belief? Which one is true worship?
