The Role of Motives in Refuting the Second Objection
The author answers the objection that both gods, knowing what is most beneficial, would will the same, by discussing the role of motives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the concept of motives (dawā'ī) to refute the objection that two omniscient gods would necessarily will the same thing.

Core Idea
Motive-free action shows that even equal knowledge does not force equal will.

In this passage, the author confronts a key objection against the possibility of two gods: if both know which course is most beneficial, they would both will that course, so no disagreement. The author's reply hinges on the role of motives in action. He shows that under either assumption—action depends on motives or not—the conclusion of equal will does not follow. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection quoted at the beginning: the objector argues that if each god knows the preponderant side in benefit, both must will that side.
Notice the objector's implicit assumption that knowledge of benefit directly determines will.
2
Examine the first branch
The author considers the case where action depends on a motive. Note the infinite regress argument: if the motive were produced by us, that would require another motive, leading to impossibility. So motives are created by God.
This leads to the conclusion that all human actions are created by God.
3
Derive the consequence
From the first branch, the author concludes that God's agency does not depend on consideration of benefits. Thus, it does not follow that both gods must will the same beneficial action.
This refutes the objection under the assumption that actions depend on motives.
4
Examine the second branch
Now consider the case where action does not depend on motives. When motion and rest are equal in benefit and harm, it is possible for one god to intend motion and the other rest.
This leads to disagreement in intention and creation.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
In both branches, the objection fails: either God's will is not bound by benefit, or disagreement is possible. The proof against two gods is thereby supported.
The author has successfully defended the original argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله في المعارضة الثانية : ( إن كل واحد منهما يكون عالماً بأن الطرف الراجح في المصلحة أيهما ؟ فوجب كونهما مريدين لذلك الطرف ) قلنا : الفعل إما أن يتوقف على الداعي أو لا يتوقف. فإن توقف على الداعي ، توقف صدور الفعل منا على الداعي ، وتلك الداعية لم تحصل بنا وإلا لافتقرت إلى داعية أخرى ، ولزم التسلسل ، وهو محال . فعلى هذا أفعال العباد مستندة إلى دواعي يخلقها الله تعالى ، وتكون كل تلك الدواعي موجبات لهذه الأفعال ، فالقاصد إلى تكوين السبب الموجب ، قاصد إلى تكوين المسبب، فيلزم أن تكون أفعال العباد بأسرها واقعة بتكوين الله تعالى. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فحينئذ لا تتوقف فاعلية الله تعالى على رعاية المصالح، بل قد يقع ما يكون على خلاف رعاية المصالح ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، لم يلزم من الإيجاد في وجه المصلحة : المساواة في الإرادة والفعل. وأما إن لم يتوقف الفعل على الدواعي فعند استواء التحريك والتسكين في جميع جهات المصالح والمفاسد ، لا يمتنع وقوع أن يحاول أحدهما التحريك ويحاول الآخر التسكين. وعند وقوع هذا التقدير تحصل المخالفة في القصد والتكوين ويتم الدليل المذكور.
As for his statement in the second objection: 'Each of them knows which side is preponderant in benefit? So it would be necessary that both of them will that side.' We say: Action either depends on a motive or does not depend. If it depends on a motive, then the occurrence of the action from us depends on the motive, and that motive is not produced by us; otherwise it would need another motive, and infinite regress would follow, which is impossible. On this basis, the acts of servants are based on motives that God creates, and all those motives necessitate these actions. So the one who intends to create the necessitating cause is intending to create the effect. So it follows that all the acts of servants occur by God's creation. If that is the case, then God’s agency does not depend on consideration of benefits; rather, what is contrary to consideration of benefits may occur. And if that is the case, it does not follow from creation in the direction of benefit that there is equality in will and action. But if action does not depend on motives, then when motion and rest are equal in all aspects of benefits and harms, it is not impossible that one of them attempts motion and the other attempts rest. And upon the occurrence of this assumption, disagreement occurs in intention and creation, and the mentioned proof is completed.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage handles the second objection. The objector argues that each god knows which course of action is most beneficial, so they must both will that same course, thus no disagreement. The author replies by distinguishing two cases: whether action depends on a motive (داعي) or not. If action depends on a motive, then the motive that causes the action is created by God, not by us (otherwise infinite regress). Hence all human actions are ultimately created by God through created motives. In that case, God's acting does not require consideration of benefits; He may act against apparent benefit. Therefore, it does not follow that both gods must will the same thing based on benefit. If action does not depend on motives, then when the two actions (motion and rest) are equal in benefit and harm, it is possible that one intends motion and the other rest, resulting in disagreement. Thus in either case, the original proof against two gods stands. The author thereby refutes the objection by showing that the premise of equal will is not necessary.

Key Terms
داعي
تسلسل
مصلحة
فاعلية
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God always acts according to what is most beneficial for creatures.
Correction: The author argues that God's agency does not depend on consideration of benefits; He may act contrary to apparent benefit.

Checkpoint
According to the author's answer to the second objection, what is the consequence if action depends on a motive?
A) Both gods would necessarily will the same because motives align.
B) Human actions are ultimately created by God, and God's agency is not bound by benefits, so equality of will is not required.
C) The sequence of motives leads to an infinite regress, proving that actions cannot depend on motives.
D) Each god has his own set of motives, leading to disagreement.

Key Takeaway
The objection that two gods would will the same because they know what is best fails because God's will is not constrained by perceived benefits, and without dependence on motives, disagreement remains possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes assume that because God knows what is best, His will must always match your understanding of what is good? How does this passage challenge that assumption, and what does it call you to change in how you respond to events that seem unjust or pointless?
?
If God's actions are not tied to what you perceive as beneficial, how should that affect your trust in Him when you face trials that appear harmful? What is one specific situation where you need to submit to His will without understanding the benefit?
?
The passage argues that even if two beings had the same knowledge, their wills could differ. How does this truth challenge your tendency to judge others' intentions based on what you think they should know? How can you apply this humility in your relationships?

Knowledge Exchange Proves Unity
The author presents the third argument against two gods using exchange of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the exchangeability of knowledge between hypothetical two gods leads to their contingency and thus impossibility.

Core Idea
If two gods had identical knowledge, the knowledge could swap, requiring a creator for the swap.

The third argument against two gods is more abstract. It shows that if there were two all-knowing gods, their knowledge would be identical and thus interchangeable. This interchangeability forces us to ask why each god has its own knowledge, leading to the conclusion that the knowledge must depend on an external specifier—making it created, not divine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the logical chain from two gods to impossibility.
2
Identify the premise of two knowledges
The argument begins with supposing two gods knowing all things. Their knowledges relate to the same objects. Note the phrase 'تعلقا بمعلوم واحد'.
3
Understand the similarity and interchangeability
Two knowledges related to the same object are similar (مثلان). Whatever is true of one is true of its similar, so each knowledge could subsist in either essence.
4
Recognize the need for a specifier
Since the knowledge could be in either essence, the actual specification must come from an external specifier (مخصص) and maker (جاعل).
5
Conclude the impossibility
If the knowledge is made by an agent, it is originated (حادث). But God's attributes are eternal, so the supposition is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة في المسألة: قالوا: لو قدرنا إلهين عالمين بكل المعلومات، فحينئذ يكون علم كل واحد منهما متعلقاً بعين ما تعلق به علم الثاني، وكل علمين تعلقا بمعلوم واحد على وجه واحد فهما مثلان، فيلزم أن يكون علم كل واحد منهما مثلاً لعلم الآخر، وكل ما صح على الشيء، صح على مثله، فكما صح على هذا العلم أن يكون قائماً بهذه الذات، فكذلك وجب على العلم القائم بالثاني، أن يكون قائماً بالأول، فيثبت أن كل واحد يصح من هذين العلمين، يصح قيامه بكل واحد منها بدلاً عن الآخر. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك كان اختصاص هذه الذات بهذا العلم دون الذات الأخرى وجب أن يكون بتخصيص مخصص وجعل جاعل، وكل ما حصل بالفاعل الجاعل، كان حادثاً، فلزم أن يكون علم الله محدثاً، وأن تكون قدرته محدثة، وكل ما كان كذلك امتنع كونه إلهاً. فيثبت: أن القول بوجود الإلهين يفضي إلى هذه المحالات، فوجب أن يكون محالاً. وفي تقرير هذه الحجة وتزييفها أبحاث كثيرة غامضة.
The third argument in the issue: They said: If we suppose two gods knowing all known things, then the knowledge of each one would be related to exactly what the knowledge of the second is related to, and any two knowledges that are related to one known thing in one way are two similars, so it would necessitate that the knowledge of each one is a similar to the knowledge of the other. And whatever is true of a thing is true of its similar. So just as it is true of this knowledge that it is subsisting (qa'im) in this essence, so too it would be necessary for the knowledge subsisting in the second to be subsisting in the first. Thus it is established that each of these two knowledges, it is valid for it to subsist in each one (of the essences) in place of the other. And when the matter is thus, the specification of this essence with this knowledge to the exclusion of the other essence must be by a specification (takhsis) of a specifier (mukhasis) and a making of a maker (ja'il). And whatever comes about by an agent maker is originated (hadith). So it would necessitate that the knowledge of God is originated, and that His power is originated. And whatever is thus is impossible to be a god. So it is established that the statement of the existence of two gods leads to these impossibilities, so it must be impossible. And in the exposition of this argument and its refutation there are many intricate investigations.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against the existence of two gods, based on the exchangeability of their knowledge. The argument proceeds as follows: If there were two gods, each would know all things, so their knowledge would attach to exactly the same objects. Two knowledges that relate to the same object in the same way are similar. Therefore, the knowledge of each god is similar to the knowledge of the other. Whatever is true of one thing is true of its similar; thus, since the knowledge of the first subsists in the first essence, the knowledge of the second must also subsist in the first essence, and vice versa. This means that each knowledge could subsist in either essence. Consequently, the fact that one essence has its own knowledge and not the other's must be due to an external specifier and maker, which would make that knowledge (and power) originated. But God's attributes must be eternal and uncreated, so the supposition leads to impossibility. Therefore, the existence of two gods is impossible. The author notes that there are many detailed discussions regarding the formulation and refutation of this argument.

معلوم
مثلان
قيام
اختصاص
تخصيص
مخصص
جاعل
حادث
إله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two gods could coexist with identical attributes without conflict.
Correction: This argument shows that identical attributes force interchangeability, which requires an external cause for their specification, making the attributes created and thus incompatible with divinity.

Checkpoint
What is the immediate consequence of two knowledges being related to the same object in the same way?
A) They are opposites
B) They are similar (mithlan)
C) They are identical
D) They are independent

Key Takeaway
The exchangeability of knowledge between hypothetical two gods demonstrates their contingency, proving that only one uncreated God can exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson shows that even the possibility of two gods is absurd—what does that say about the exclusivity of the knowledge He has of you personally? Will you today surrender to His sole knowledge and stop trying to hide anything from Him?
?
The argument proves that every specification requires a maker. Consider your own life: what 'specifications' have you attributed to chance or to your own doing rather than to the Creator's precise will? Will you now consciously trace every detail—your abilities, your circumstances, your very existence—back to His sole agency, and thank Him accordingly?
?
The impossibility of two gods means that your worship can only be directed to One who has absolutely no equal. Is your heart truly single in its devotion, or do you give parts of your trust, fear, or hope to other 'gods' like status, wealth, or people? What one thing in your life today competes with God's exclusivity, and what concrete step will you take to remove that competition?

Is Oneness an Attribute or Essence? Arguments for a Superadded Attribute
Objection and two proofs for oneness as superadded attribute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to oneness being an attribute and the two proofs that oneness is a superadded attribute distinct from the essence.

Core Idea
Oneness is a distinct attribute superadded to the divine essence, not identical to it.

The debate over whether God's oneness is identical to His essence or a separate attribute has deep implications for how we understand divine simplicity. This passage presents an initial objection and then two important proofs that oneness is indeed a superadded attribute. We will explore each argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and objection
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the opening objection: if oneness were an attribute, it would require another oneness ad infinitum, which is impossible.
This objection challenges the very idea of oneness as an attribute.
2
Examine Proof 1: Division of the One
The first proof divides 'one' into necessary and contingent. Since 'one' is common to both, it must be different from the specific quiddity of the necessary.
3
Examine Proof 2: Contrast with Many
The second proof compares the contrasts of 'one' (with many) and 'necessary' (with not-necessary). Their different contrasts show that the meanings are distinct.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how these proofs together support the position that oneness is a superadded attribute. How does this affect our understanding of divine attributes?
This connects to the broader debate on divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث في أن وحدة الله تعالى هل هي عين الذات أو صفة قائمة بالذات؟ لقائل أن يقول : لو كانت الوحدة صفة ، لكانت تلك الصفة واحدة ، فيلزم أن يكون للوحدة وحدة أخرى إلى غير النهاية . وأنه محال . ولقائل آخر أن يقول : الوحدة لا بد وأن تكون صفة زائلة على الذات . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إنه يصح تقسيم الواحد إلى الواجب لذاته ، وإلى الممكن لذاته . فيقال : الواحد إما أن يكون واجباً لذاته ، أو ممكناً لذاته ، ومورد التقسيم مشترك بين القسمين ، فكونه واحداً مشترك فيه بين الواجب لذاته وبين الممكن لذاته ، وخصوص كونه واجباً لذاته غير مشترك فيه بين القسمين ، وهذا يدل على أن كونه واحداً ، مغاير الخصوص كـونـه واجب الوجود لذاته • والثاني : إن الواحد يقابل الكثير ، والواجب لذاته يقابل ما ليس واجباً لذاته ، وما يصلح لأن يكون مقابلاً لأحدهما ، لا يصلح أن يكون مقابلاً للثاني ، وهذا يوجب أن يكون كونه واحداً ، مغايراً لكونه واجب الوجود لذاته
The third chapter: On whether the oneness of God is identical to the essence or an attribute subsisting in the essence. One might say: If oneness were an attribute, that attribute would be one, so oneness would have another oneness ad infinitum, which is impossible. Another might say: Oneness must be a superadded attribute upon the essence. Evidence for it: First: The one can be divided into the necessary in itself and the contingent in itself. So it is said: The one is either necessary in itself or contingent in itself, and the subject of division is common between the two divisions. So being one is common between the necessary in itself and the contingent in itself, whereas being necessary in itself is specific not shared between the divisions. This indicates that being one is different from being necessary in itself. Second: The one is contrasted with the many, and the necessary in itself is contrasted with what is not necessary in itself. And what can be a contrast to one of them cannot be a contrast to the other. This necessitates that being one is different from being necessary in itself.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage opens the third chapter on whether God's oneness is identical to His essence or an attribute. It first states an objection: if oneness is an attribute, it would require another oneness for that attribute, leading to infinite regress. Then it presents two proofs that oneness is a superadded attribute. Proof 1: The concept of 'one' can be divided into the necessary and the contingent, showing that 'one' is a common property and thus distinct from the specific quiddity of the necessary. Proof 2: The contrasts of 'one' (many) and 'necessary' (not necessary) are different, indicating that the two meanings are distinct.

صفة زائدة
واحد
واجب لذاته
ممكن لذاته
مقابلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Oneness is merely a negative attribute (negation of multiplicity).
Correction: This passage argues oneness is a positive, superadded attribute distinct from the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof given in the text, what shows that 'oneness' is different from being necessary in itself?
A) The one can be divided into the necessary and contingent
B) Oneness leads to infinite regress
C) The necessary has no contrast
D) The one is a negative concept

Key Takeaway
Oneness is a superadded attribute distinct from the divine essence, as shown by the commonality of 'one' between necessary and contingent and the difference in contrasts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Problem of God's Oneness: Identity or Attribute?
Razi states the problem and presents two opposing views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the central problem regarding God's oneness and the two opposing positions: the infinite regress objection and the thesis that oneness is a superadded attribute.

Core Idea
Is God's oneness identical to His essence or a superadded attribute? That is the question.

Razi begins this chapter by posing a fundamental theological question: Is God's oneness the same as His essence, or is it an additional attribute? He then presents two opposing views. The first raises a logical objection against the attribute view, while the second insists on the attribute view and hints at proofs to come.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own words introduce the problem.
Notice the phrase 'زائلة على الذات' – it is likely a corruption of 'زائدة' (superadded).
2
Identify the two views
The passage presents two opposing views. Find the first view that objects to oneness being an attribute, and the second view that insists on it being an attribute.
The first view is introduced by 'لقائل أن يقول', the second by 'ولقائل آخر أن يقول'.
3
Examine the infinite regress objection
The first view argues that if oneness were an attribute, that attribute would itself be one, leading to an infinite regress of onenesses – an impossibility. Understand why this is considered a strong objection.
This objection is a standard philosophical argument against superadded attributes.
4
Note the promise of proofs
The second view states that oneness must be an attribute and then says 'ويدل عليه وجوه' (and proofs for it). This signals that the author will provide evidence for the attribute view in the following text.
The proofs are not given in this passage; they are previewed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ويدل عليه وجوه :
The third chapter: On whether the oneness of God, exalted, is identical to the essence or an attribute subsisting in the essence. One may say: If oneness were an attribute, then that attribute would be one, necessitating an infinite regress of onenesses. That is impossible. And another may say: Oneness must be an attribute passing away upon the essence. And proofs for it:
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter's central question: Is God's oneness identical to His essence or a superadded attribute? It presents two opposing views. The first is an objection that if oneness were an attribute, that attribute would itself be one, leading to an infinite regress of onenesses—an impossibility. The second view insists that oneness must be a superadded attribute, and the author then promises proofs to support this. The teacher would note that the infinite regress objection is a common philosophical challenge, and the subsequent proofs aim to establish the attribute view despite it.

Correction: The objection is presented as a challenge, but the author promises proofs that will address it, indicating that the attribute view can be defended.

Key Takeaway

First Proof: Oneness as a Distinct Attribute
Razi's first proof via logical division of the one

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's logical division proof that oneness is a distinct attribute from necessary existence.

Core Idea
Division of the one shows oneness is common, thus distinct from necessary existence.

Razi begins by presenting a simple but powerful logical argument. He takes the concept 'one' and notes that it applies both to Allah (necessary in itself) and to contingent beings (possible in itself). If 'oneness' were identical to 'necessary existence', it could not apply to contingent beings. Since it does apply to both, oneness must be a distinct attribute. This lesson walks through that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read its literal translation. Pay attention to the logical terms: division, necessary, contingent, common.
The Arabic uses concise logical language.
2
Understand the logical division
Identify what is being divided: 'the one' is divided into 'necessary in itself' and 'contingent in itself'. Notice that the subject of division ('the one') is common to both divisions.
3
See the implication of commonality
Because 'one' applies to both necessary and contingent, oneness cannot be identical to necessary existence (which applies only to one division). Therefore, oneness must be a different attribute.
This is the core insight: common predication implies distinction.
4
Confirm the conclusion
Razi states directly that this indicates oneness is different (maghanir) from the particularity of necessary existence. Restate the reasoning in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إنه يصح تقسيم الواحد إلى الواجب لذاته، وإلى الممكن لذاته . فيقال : الواحد إما أن يكون واجباً لذاته، أو ممكناً لذاته، ومورد التقسيم مشترك بين القسمين، فكونه واحداً مشترك فيه [ بين الواجب لذاته وبين الممكن لذاته، وخصوص كونه واجباً لذاته غير مشترك فيه (٢) ] بين القسمين، وهذا يدل على أن كونه واحداً، مغاير الخصوص كـونـه واجب الوجود لذاته
The first: It is valid to divide the one into the necessary in itself and the contingent in itself. So it is said: The one is either necessary in itself or contingent in itself, and the subject of division is common to both divisions. So its being one is common [between the necessary in itself and the contingent in itself, while the particularity of its being necessary in itself is not shared (2)] between the divisions. And this indicates that its being one is different from the particularity of its being necessary of existence in itself.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This is the first proof, structured as a logical division. The term 'one' applies to both necessary and contingent beings. If oneness were identical to necessary existence, it could not be predicated of contingent beings. Since we validly divide 'the one' into necessary and contingent, the concept of oneness must be common to both, while necessary existence is specific to one division. Therefore, oneness is a distinct attribute, not identical to the essence.

Key Terms
تقسيم
واجب لذاته
ممكن لذاته
مورد التقسيم
مشترك
مغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof assumes oneness is a separate entity added to the essence.
Correction: The proof does not assume a separate entity; it merely argues from predication: because 'one' is predicated of both necessary and contingent, it must be conceptually distinct from necessary existence.

Checkpoint
What does the first proof use to show that oneness is different from necessary existence?
A) The impossibility of infinite regress.
B) The fact that 'one' is predicated of both necessary and contingent beings.
C) The concept of oneness is negative.
D) The division of attributes into essential and accidental.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that oneness must be a distinct attribute because it applies to both necessary and contingent beings, while necessary existence applies only to the necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that the attribute of oneness applies to both the Creator and created beings, does this challenge any assumption you have about God's uniqueness in a way that makes you feel closer to understanding His transcendence? How will you ensure that your belief in Tawheed is not just intellectual but transforms your daily reliance on Him?
?
This proof uses logic to clarify a truth about God. Are you willing to let reasoned reflection correct or deepen your understanding of your faith, even if it feels unfamiliar? What specific step will you take this week to reflect more deeply on one of God's attributes?
?
If oneness is distinct from God's essence, then 'Allah is One' means more than 'Allah exists'. What difference does this make in how you call upon Him or trust in His uniqueness? Write down one area of your life where you can practically affirm His oneness beyond mere words.

The Proof from Contrasting Opposites
Razi proves oneness is an attribute by contrasting opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that 'one' has a different opposite than 'necessary', proving they are distinct attributes.

Core Idea
Different opposites imply different concepts.

In this lesson, we examine a concise logical argument by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. He shows that the term 'one' is opposed to 'many', while 'necessary' is opposed to 'contingent'. Since these opposites are not interchangeable, the concepts themselves must be distinct. This reasoning reinforces that oneness is a separate attribute from necessary existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without translation. Notice the key terms: يقابل (opposite), الكثير (the many), مقابلاً (opposite).
2
Examine the translation and logic
Compare the literal translation. Identify the two pairs of opposites: one/many and necessary/contingent. Follow the argument: since one's opposite is many, and necessary's opposite is contingent, and these opposites are different, the original concepts must also be different.
This is a form of argument by contraposition.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: Does the argument prove that oneness is a distinct attribute? Consider the premise that different opposites entail different concepts. This is foundational for differentiating divine attributes.
The conclusion is that oneness is not identical to necessary existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : إن الواحد يقابل الكثير، والواجب لذاته يقابل ما ليس واجباً لذاته، وما يصلح لأن يكون مقابلاً لأحدهما، لا يصلح أن يكون مقابلاً للثاني، وهذا يوجب أن يكون كونه واحداً، مغايراً لكونه واجب الوجود لذاته
And the second: The one is opposite to the many, and the necessary in itself is opposite to what is not necessary in itself. And what is suitable to be opposite to one of them is not suitable to be opposite to the other. And this necessitates that its being one is different from its being necessary of existence in itself.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This proof relies on the principle that if two terms have different opposites, they themselves are different. 'One' is opposite to 'many', while 'necessary' is opposite to 'not necessary'. These opposites are not interchangeable: 'many' is not the opposite of 'necessary', and 'not necessary' is not the opposite of 'one'. Therefore, the concepts of oneness and necessary existence are distinct.

Key Terms
يقابل
الكثير
مقابلاً
الواجب لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Oneness and necessary existence are the same thing.
Correction: This proof shows they have different opposites, so they are conceptually distinct.

Checkpoint
What does the second proof use to argue that oneness and necessary existence are different?
A) The fact that oneness is a positive attribute.
B) The difference in their opposites.
C) The infinite regress argument.
D) The division of the one.

Key Takeaway
Razi's proof from contrast of opposites demonstrates that oneness is a distinct attribute from necessary existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How would your prayer (salah) change if you consciously affirmed 'Allah is One' as a real attribute separate from 'Allah is self-sufficient'? Pick one moment in your day to pause and say 'الواحد' with full awareness of its meaning.
?
If you previously assumed that 'one' and 'necessary' are the same because both describe God, what does this distinction reveal about how carefully you think about His names? Is your worship based on clear knowledge or fuzzy impressions? Write down one specific way you will reflect on the attribute of oneness (al-wahid) in your next dhikr session.
?
Razi uses opposites to prove a point. Consider an area in your life where you confuse two similar concepts (e.g., love and attachment). How can distinguishing opposites help you see the truth? Commit to applying this method to one confusion you face this week.

The Proof of Conceptual Independence
Razi proves oneness is an attribute via mental separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses mental separation to prove that oneness is a distinct attribute of Allah, not identical to necessary existence.

Core Idea
Conceiving one without the other proves they are distinct.

Razi’s third proof is a concise argument from conceptual independence. If we can think of something as ‘one’ without yet knowing whether it is necessary or contingent, then the concept of oneness does not depend on the concept of necessity. Likewise for necessity without oneness. This logical test shows they are different attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice the two parallel mental operations: conceiving oneness with doubt about necessity, and conceiving necessity with doubt about oneness.
The passage is short but packed.
2
Identify the two premises
Razi first states we can conceive oneness while doubting whether it is necessary or contingent. Second, we can conceive necessity while doubting whether it is one or many. Write these down.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since the known (oneness) is different from the unknown (necessity) in that scenario, they are distinct. Therefore, oneness is an attribute separate from necessary existence.
The conclusion is derived from the principle that conceptually separable ideas are really distinct.
4
Reflect on the significance
This proof shows that oneness is not reducible to necessity; it is an additional attribute. This matters for understanding Allah’s attributes as multiple and real.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث : إنا نعقل كونه واحداً مع الشك في أن ذلك الواحد واجب لذاته، أو ممكن لذاته . وكذلك قد نعقل ماهية الواجب لذاته، مع الشك في أنه واحد أو كثير . والمعلوم مغاير لغير المعلوم، فوجب أن يكون كونه واحداً مغايراً لكونه واجب الوجود لذاته
And the third: We conceive its being one while doubting whether that one is necessary in itself or contingent in itself. Likewise, we conceive the essence of the necessary in itself while doubting whether it is one or many. And the known is different from the unknown. So it is necessary that its being one is different from its being necessary of existence in itself.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This proof uses mental separation. We can think of something as 'one' without knowing whether it is necessary or contingent; likewise, we can think of something as 'necessary' without knowing if it is one or many. Since we can conceive one without the other, the two concepts are distinct. Therefore, oneness is a different attribute from necessary existence.

Key Terms
نعقل
شك
معلوم
مغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof relies on our subjective ignorance: just because we don't know something doesn't mean the concepts are distinct.
Correction: Razi is not arguing from mere ignorance but from the possibility of conceiving one concept without the other, which shows conceptual independence. The doubt is not about our knowledge but about whether the concepts entail each other.

Checkpoint
What does Razi use to prove that oneness and necessity are distinct?
A) The fact that oneness is found in contingent beings.
B) The ability to conceive oneness while doubting necessity, and vice versa.
C) The difference between necessary and contingent existence.
D) The impossibility of a necessary being being multiple.

Key Takeaway
Because we can conceive oneness without necessity, and necessity without oneness, they are distinct attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Is there an area of your faith where you assume two truths are the same because you haven't bothered to separate them in your mind? What would change if you did the work of distinguishing them?
?
If you truly believed that 'oneness' and 'necessity' are distinct attributes, how would that affect the way you worship—would you feel more awe for the One who is both unique and self-sufficient? What one concrete step can you take today to reflect on that distinction in your prayer?

Fourth Proof: Oneness is Distinct from Necessity
Razi proves oneness is an attribute by showing it is predicated of contingent beings unlike necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Fakhr al-Din al-Razi proves that the attribute of oneness (al-wāḥid) is distinct in quiddity from necessary existence (wājib al-dhāt).

Core Idea
Oneness is predicated of contingents, but necessary existence is not; therefore they differ.

This short but powerful proof uses the simple fact that we describe contingent beings like humans as 'one' but never as 'necessary in itself.' This shows that oneness and necessary existence are different in essence. Let's follow the logic carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the words of Imam al-Razi settle in your mind.
2
Identify the two premises
The argument has two premises: (1) 'One' is truly predicated of the contingent; (2) 'Necessary in itself' is absolutely not predicated of the contingent. Locate them in the text.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion follows directly: if A applies where B does not, then A and B differ in quiddity. So 'one' differs from 'necessary.'
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Some think this is just about word usage, but it is about logical extension. Ask yourself: could we ever call a contingent being 'necessary in itself'? No. So the concepts are different.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع : إن الواحد قد يصدق حمله على الممكن لذاته، والواجب بالذات البتة لا يصدق حمله على الممكن . فالواحد مغاير في الماهية للواجب بالذات
And the fourth: 'One' can be truly predicated of the contingent in itself, while 'necessary in itself' is absolutely not truly predicated of the contingent. So the one differs in quiddity from the necessary in itself.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This proof is straightforward: we apply the term 'one' to contingent things (like a human), but we never apply 'necessary in itself' to them. If oneness were the same as necessary existence, it could not be applied to contingents. Since it is applied, they are different in quiddity.

Key Terms
يصدق حمله
الممكن
الواجب بالذات
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof is just about how we use words.
Correction: The proof is about the logical extension of concepts—'one' applies to things that 'necessary' does not, showing they are different in essence.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth proof argue?
A) 'One' is predicated of necessary beings only.
B) 'One' is predicated of necessary and contingent beings, but 'necessary' is not predicated of contingent.
C) 'One' and 'necessary' are synonyms.
D) 'One' is a negative concept.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof establishes that oneness (al-wāḥid) is a distinct attribute from necessary existence because it applies to contingents in a way necessity does not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a contingent being—dependent on God for every moment of your existence. Does this make you feel fragile or free? How does your awareness of contingency change the way you rely on God today?
?
Razi proves that 'oneness' is an attribute, not the very essence. Reflect on your own identity: Is your sense of being 'one' (unified, consistent) a core part of who you are, or is it something you must strive to maintain? How does this relate to God's perfect oneness, which is not an addition but His very self?
?
If 'one' applies to both you and God, yet God's oneness is unique and necessary, what does this teach you about your own need to be a true servant of the One? Are there areas in your life where you are divided in loyalty, acting as if there is more than one source of significance?

Oneness is a Positive Attribute
Razi argues that oneness is a positive, not negative, attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's proof that oneness (tawheed) is a positive, real attribute superadded to God's essence, not a mere negation.

Core Idea
Oneness is not a privation; it is a real, positive attribute.

This passage presents a crisp logical argument from Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi that the attribute of oneness is not a negative concept but a positive, existential attribute. Razi shows that if oneness were merely the negation of number, it would lead to absurd consequences. The argument concludes that oneness is a superadded attribute (sifa za'ida) to God's necessary existence. This lesson will walk you through the proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice the logical flow from premise to conclusion.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key concepts
Look for the terms: 'negative' (salb), 'number' ('adad), 'units' (wahadat), 'non-existences' ('adamat), and 'positive attribute' (sifa wujudiyya).
These are the building blocks of the argument.
3
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
Razi assumes oneness is negative, then traces the consequences: number is aggregate of units; if units were non-existences, number would be aggregate of non-existences (absurd). So oneness cannot be negative.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Understand the conclusion
The argument establishes that oneness is a positive, superadded attribute. Ask yourself: why does this matter for theology?
It affirms that God's attributes are real, not mere negations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : المفهوم من كونه واحداً ليس سلبياً . والدليل عليه : أنه لو كان سلبياً لكان سلبا للعدد . والعدد عبارة عن مجموع وحدات ، فالوحدة إن كانت عدما ، كان العدد عبارة عن مجموع عدمات ، فيكون العدد مفهوماً عدمياً . فإذا كانت الوحدة عبارة عن سلب العدد وكان العدد مفهوماً عدمياً كانت الوحدة عبارة عن عدم العدم. فوجب كونها صفة وجودية وأما إن كانت الوحدة عبارة عن مفهوم وجودي ، فذلك هو المطلوب . فيثبت : أن الوحدة صفة وجودية زائدة على كونه واجب الوجود لذاته . وههنا آخر الكلام في المباحث المتعلقة بالتنزيه . [ والحمد لله حق حمده
Then we say: The concept of its being one is not negative. The proof of that: If it were negative, it would be a negation of number. Number is the aggregate of units. If oneness were non-existence, then number would be an aggregate of non-existences, so number would be a non-existent concept. So if oneness were the negation of number and number is a non-existent concept, then oneness would be the negation of non-existence. So it must be a positive attribute. And if oneness is a positive concept, then that is what is desired. So it is established that oneness is a positive attribute superadded to its being necessary of existence in itself. Here ends the discussion on matters related to transcendence. [And praise be to God, truly His praise.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage concludes the chapter by proving oneness is a positive attribute. The argument: If oneness were negative, it would be the negation of number. But number is an aggregate of units. If units were non-existent, number would be an aggregate of non-existences, which is absurd. So oneness cannot be negative; it must be a positive, superadded attribute. The teacher would note that this refutes those who claim oneness is merely a privation.

Key Terms
سلبياً
العدد
وحدات
عدمات
عدم العدم
صفة وجودية
زائدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that oneness is merely the absence of division or multiplicity, a negative attribute.
Correction: Razi shows that oneness must be a real positive attribute, otherwise number and oneness would be absurdly defined as non-existences.

Checkpoint
What absurdity does Razi say would follow if oneness were a negative concept?
A) God would be composed of parts.
B) Number would be an aggregate of non-existences.
C) Oneness would be identical to necessary existence.
D) The attribute would lead to infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
Razi's proof establishes that divine oneness is a real, positive attribute superadded to God's essence, not a mere negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi says oneness is a positive attribute superadded to God's essence. But you cannot fully comprehend this. Does your heart accept that God's oneness is beyond your grasp, or do you secretly try to reduce it to something you can fully imagine?
?
If divine oneness is not merely the absence of partners but a real, positive perfection, how does that change the way you recite 'Allahu Ahad' in your prayers? Is it just negation, or do you feel the weight of a positive reality?
?
The argument ends the discussion on transcendence. But have you turned tanzih into a mere intellectual exercise? When you affirm that God is not like anything, does that lead you to greater awe and love, or does it remain a cold theological statement?

The Third Proof: Distinction Through Conceivability
Al-Razi's third argument for the distinctness of oneness and necessary existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument that the attribute of oneness is distinct from necessary existence because the two concepts can be conceived independently.

Core Idea
If two things can be known separately, they are really distinct.

Al-Razi presents a rational argument based on our ability to conceive of oneness without necessity and necessity without oneness. This shows that the attribute of being one is not identical to the essence of the Necessary Existent. The argument relies on the principle that distinct conceptions point to distinct realities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the third argument. Notice how al-Razi sets up two scenarios of conceivability.
2
Understand the two conceivability scenarios
We can conceive of something as 'one' while doubting its necessity or possibility. Similarly, we can conceive of the Necessary Existent while doubting whether it is one or many.
These are two distinct cognitive acts.
3
Grasp the inference
Since what is known in each case is different, the objects known must be different. Therefore, being one is different from being necessary.
This is based on the principle that knowledge of distinct objects is distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث : إنا نعقل كونه واحداً مع الشك في أن ذلك الواحد واجب لذاته ، أو ممكن لذاته . وكذلك قد نعقل ماهية الواجب لذاته ، مع الشك في أنه واحد أو كثير . والمعلوم مغاير لغير المعلوم ، فوجب أن يكون كونه واحداً مغايراً لكونه واجب الوجود لذاته
والرابع : إن الواحد قد يصدق حمله على الممكن لذاته ، والواجب بالذات البتة لا يصدق حمله على الممكن . فالواحد مغاير في الماهية للواجب بالذات
ثم نقول : المفهوم من كونه واحداً ليس سلبياً . والدليل عليه : أنه لو كان سلبياً لكان سلبا للعدد . والعدد عبارة عن مجموع وحدات ، فالوحدة إن كانت عدما ، كان العدد عبارة عن مجموع عدمات ، فيكون العدد مفهوماً عدمياً . فإذا كانت الوحدة عبارة عن سلب العدد وكان العدد مفهوماً عدمياً كانت الوحدة عبارة عن عدم العدم. فوجب كونها صفة وجودية وأما إن كانت الوحدة عبارة عن مفهوم وجودي ، فذلك هو المطلوب . فيثبت : الا الوجدة صفة وجودية زائدة على كونه واجب الوجود لذاته . وههنا آخر الكلام في المباحث المتعلقة بالتنزيه . [ والحمد لله حق حمده
And the third: We conceive of it being one while doubting whether that one is necessary in itself or possible in itself. Likewise, we may conceive the essence of the Necessary in itself while doubting whether it is one or many. And what is known is different from what is not known, so it must be that its being one is different from its being necessary of existence in itself. And the fourth: The one may truly be predicated of the possible in itself, while the necessary in itself is absolutely not predicated of the possible. So the one is different in essence from the necessary in itself. Then we say: The concept of its being one is not negative. The proof of this: If it were negative, it would be a negation of number. And number is an aggregate of units. So if unity were non-existence, number would be an aggregate of non-existences, so number would be a non-existential concept. So if unity is the negation of number and number is a non-existential concept, then unity would be the negation of non-existence. So it must be an existential attribute. And if unity is an existential concept, that is what is desired. So it is established: Unity is an existential attribute additional to its being necessary of existence in itself. And here ends the discourse on the topics related to transcendence. [And praise be to God, worthy of His praise]
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the attribute 'one' (al-wahid) is a positive attribute (sifa wujudiyya) additional to the essence of the Necessary Existent. Al-Razi presents three arguments. The first (third) argument: we can conceive of something being one while doubting whether it is necessary or possible, and we can conceive of the essence of the Necessary Existent while doubting whether it is one or many. Since what is known (ma'lum) of one is different from what is known of the other, the concept of being one is distinct from the concept of being necessary. The second (fourth) argument: the predicate 'one' can be truly applied to a possible being, but the predicate 'necessary of existence' can never be applied to a possible being. Therefore, the essence of 'one' is different from the essence of 'necessary'. Then al-Razi presents a third argument: the concept of 'one' is not a negative concept. If it were negative, it would be the negation of number. But number is a collection of units. If unity were non-existence, then number would be a collection of non-existences, making number a negative concept. If unity is the negation of number and number is negative, then unity would be the negation of non-existence, which is a positive concept. Therefore, unity is a positive attribute. Thus, it is established that unity (al-wahda) is a positive attribute additional to the attribute of being necessary of existence. This concludes the discussion on divine transcendence (tanzih).

Key Terms
الواحد
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's third argument, how do we know that being one is different from being necessary?
A) Because 'one' is a positive attribute
B) Because we can conceive of one without necessity and necessity without one
C) Because number is an aggregate
D) Because possible beings are one

Key Takeaway
The distinctness of oneness and necessity is proven by our ability to conceive each independently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call Allah 'Al-Wahid' (The One), do you consider it as a separate truth from the fact that He is As-Samad (The Self-Sufficient)? How often do you merge His attributes in your mind, and what does that say about your understanding of His perfection?
?
Al-Razi forces us to admit we can think of oneness without necessity. Is your faith in Allah's oneness ever tested by doubts about His control over your life? How can separating these concepts strengthen your reliance on Him?
?
This proof relies on your ability to doubt one truth while affirming another. When you face a personal trial, do you doubt Allah's wisdom while still affirming His existence? How can this lesson help you trust that His attributes—even if hard to reconcile—are distinct but perfectly coherent?

The Fourth Proof: Distinction Through Predication
Al-Razi's fourth argument for the distinctness of oneness from necessary existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument that the attribute of oneness is distinct from necessary existence because 'one' can be predicated of possible beings while 'necessary' cannot.

Core Idea
A difference in what can be predicated shows a difference in essence.

Al-Razi's fourth argument uses the logic of predication: the term 'one' can be truthfully applied to possible beings, but 'necessary of existence' can never be applied to them. Since the predicates have different extensions, the concepts they express must be different. This reinforces that oneness is an additional attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the fourth argument. Focus on the key predication statements.
2
Identify the two predicates
Note that 'one' applies to both possible and necessary beings, while 'necessary' applies only to the Necessary Existent.
This shows a clear difference in scope.
3
Draw the inference
If two predicates have different ranges of application, they cannot express the same essence. Therefore, oneness is different from necessity.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the attribute 'one' (al-wahid) is a positive attribute (sifa wujudiyya) additional to the essence of the Necessary Existent. Al-Razi presents three arguments. The first (third) argument: we can conceive of something being one while doubting whether it is necessary or possible, and we can conceive of the essence of the Necessary Existent while doubting whether it is one or many. Since what is known (ma'lum) of one is different from what is known of the other, the concept of being one is distinct from the concept of being necessary. The second (fourth) argument: the predicate 'one' can be truly applied to a possible being, but the predicate 'necessary of existence' can never be applied to a possible being. Therefore, the essence of 'one' is different from the essence of 'necessary'. Then al-Razi presents a third argument: the concept of 'one' is not a negative concept. If it were negative, it would be the negation of number. But number is a collection of units. If unity were non-existence, then number would be a collection of non-existences, making number a negative concept. If unity is the negation of number and number is negative, then unity would be the negation of non-existence, which is a positive concept. Therefore, unity is a positive attribute. Thus, it is established that unity (al-wahda) is a positive attribute additional to the attribute of being necessary of existence. This concludes the discussion on divine transcendence (tanzih).

Checkpoint
What does al-Razi's fourth argument use to show that 'one' and 'necessary' are different?
A) The concept of number
B) The difference in predication over possible beings
C) The impossibility of conceiving them separately
D) The positive nature of unity

Key Takeaway
The difference in predication over possible beings proves that oneness is not identical to necessity.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Unity is a Positive Attribute Additional to Necessity
Al-Razi's proof that unity is a positive attribute and conclusion of the discourse on transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's proof that the attribute of unity is a positive (existential) attribute, not a negative one, and that it is additional to necessary existence.

Core Idea
Unity is not just 'not many'; it is a real positive quality.

In this concluding argument, al-Razi addresses a potential misunderstanding: that unity might be a negative attribute (simply the negation of number). He demonstrates that if unity were negative, it would lead to absurd consequences, and instead shows it must be a positive existential attribute. This establishes that unity is an additional positive attribute over and above the essence of the Necessary Existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation from 'ثم نقول' to the end. This is a self-contained proof.
2
Understand the assumption and its consequence
Al-Razi assumes unity is negative, i.e., the negation of number. He then examines the nature of number.
Number is an aggregate of units, so if unity is non-existence, number becomes an aggregate of non-existences, which is absurd.
3
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
If unity is non-existence, then number is an aggregate of non-existences, making number a negative concept. Then if unity is the negation of number, it becomes the negation of a negative, which is positive. Alternatively, if unity is directly existential, that also yields the desired result.
Either way, unity must be existential.
4
Conclusion
Therefore, unity is an existential (positive) attribute additional to being necessary of existence. This ends the discussion on transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث : إنا نعقل كونه واحداً مع الشك في أن ذلك الواحد واجب لذاته ، أو ممكن لذاته . وكذلك قد نعقل ماهية الواجب لذاته ، مع الشك في أنه واحد أو كثير . والمعلوم مغاير لغير المعلوم ، فوجب أن يكون كونه واحداً مغايراً لكونه واجب الوجود لذاته
والرابع : إن الواحد قد يصدق حمله على الممكن لذاته ، والواجب بالذات البتة لا يصدق حمله على الممكن . فالواحد مغاير في الماهية للواجب بالذات
ثم نقول : المفهوم من كونه واحداً ليس سلبياً . والدليل عليه : أنه لو كان سلبياً لكان سلبا للعدد . والعدد عبارة عن مجموع وحدات ، فالوحدة إن كانت عدما ، كان العدد عبارة عن مجموع عدمات ، فيكون العدد مفهوماً عدمياً . فإذا كانت الوحدة عبارة عن سلب العدد وكان العدد مفهوماً عدمياً كانت الوحدة عبارة عن عدم العدم. فوجب كونها صفة وجودية وأما إن كانت الوحدة عبارة عن مفهوم وجودي ، فذلك هو المطلوب . فيثبت : الا الوجدة صفة وجودية زائدة على كونه واجب الوجود لذاته . وههنا آخر الكلام في المباحث المتعلقة بالتنزيه . [ والحمد لله حق حمده
And the third: We conceive of it being one while doubting whether that one is necessary in itself or possible in itself. Likewise, we may conceive the essence of the Necessary in itself while doubting whether it is one or many. And what is known is different from what is not known, so it must be that its being one is different from its being necessary of existence in itself. And the fourth: The one may truly be predicated of the possible in itself, while the necessary in itself is absolutely not predicated of the possible. So the one is different in essence from the necessary in itself. Then we say: The concept of its being one is not negative. The proof of this: If it were negative, it would be a negation of number. And number is an aggregate of units. So if unity were non-existence, number would be an aggregate of non-existences, so number would be a non-existential concept. So if unity is the negation of number and number is a non-existential concept, then unity would be the negation of non-existence. So it must be an existential attribute. And if unity is an existential concept, that is what is desired. So it is established: Unity is an existential attribute additional to its being necessary of existence in itself. And here ends the discourse on the topics related to transcendence. [And praise be to God, worthy of His praise]

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that calling God 'one' is just a way of saying He is not many, i.e., a negative attribute.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that unity cannot be merely negative because it would lead to absurd consequences; it must be a positive attribute.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion al-Razi reaches about the attribute of unity?
A) It is a negative attribute
B) It is identical to necessary existence
C) It is an existential attribute additional to necessary existence
D) It is a relative attribute

Key Takeaway
Unity is a positive, existential attribute that is distinct from and additional to the attribute of necessary existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is One', do you only mean that there is no other god besides Him, or do you affirm a positive reality of unity as an attribute? How might this affect the way you relate to Him?
?
Al-Razi argues that unity is an existential attribute distinct from necessary existence itself. Does your understanding of God's oneness go beyond mere negation? What does it mean for you personally that God's unity is a real quality?
?
If unity were merely negative, your worship might only be avoidance of false gods. But since it is positive, what does that demand from you in terms of devotion and acknowledgement? Can you point to a moment in your life when you have truly affirmed this positive oneness?
